Revisiting Functions in Julia
Functions are essential parts of any programming language, and Julia is no
different. They allow us to write reusable pieces of code that can be called
with different arguments to perform a speciﬁc task. In Chapter 2, Getting
started with Julia , we learnt to deﬁne and call functions in Julia. In this
chapter , we will understand the basic idea behind a function as well as how
to use them to write modular and efﬁcient code.
A general function in Julia consists of ﬁve parts.
1. The function-end keyword
2. The name of the function
3. The ar guments used in the function
4. The body of the function
5. The return statement of the function
Figure 3.1:  Parts of a function
The function-end keyword
Like any other language, Julia starts with the keyword function. In Julia, the
function keyword is used to deﬁne a new function. For example, consider the
following function deﬁnition:
julia> function saluer(name)
println(“Bonjour, $name”)
end
This deﬁnes a function saluer that takes a single argument name and prints a
greeting to the screen. To call the function, you would use its name followed
by the ar guments in parentheses:
julia> saluer(“Julia”)
This would print Bonjour, Julia  on the screen.
Unlike other languages, Julia detects the end of a function by the end
keyword. In Julia, the end keyword is used to mark the end of a block of
code. It is often used to mark the end of a function deﬁnition. For example,
julia> function saluer(name)
println(“Bienvenue, $name”)
end
Here, the end keyword marks the end of the saluer function deﬁnition. Every
function deﬁnition in Julia must end with the end keyword.
The end keywor d is also used to mark the end of other types of code blocks
in Julia,  such as loop constructs, conditional statements, and compound
expressions.
The name of the function
In Julia, the name of a function is the identiﬁer that is used to refer to the
function. It is speciﬁed when the function is deﬁned and is used to call the
function. For example, consider the following function deﬁnition:
julia> function saluer(name)
println(“Bonne nuit, $name”)
end
The name of this function is saluer. To call the function, you would use its
name followed by the ar guments in parentheses:
julia> saluer(“Julia”)
This would print “Bonne nuit, Julia” on the screen.
The name of the function can be any valid Julia identiﬁer , as long as it is not
already in use as a variable, function, or type name in the current scope.
Function names are usually chosen to be descriptive of the function’ s
purpose, to make it easier for other developers to understand what the
function does.
Function naming rules in Julia
In Julia, there are a few rules that you should follow when naming functions:
Function names  must start with a letter or an underscore (_). They can
also contain letters, digits, and underscores.
Function names  are case-sensitive. This means that foo and Foo are
considered to be dif ferent names.
Function names  cannot be the same as any of the Julia built-in
keywords, such as function, end, if, and so on.
Function names should be descriptive and meaningful. It’s a good idea
to choose names that reﬂect the purpose of the function.
It’s also a good idea to follow the conventions of the Julia community
when naming your functions, such as using snake_case  for function
names.
Some examples of valid function names in Julia are foo, _private,
calculate_sum , and mean_and_variance .
julia> function add_two_numbers(x, y)
return x + y
end
julia> function _private_function()
# do something
end
julia> function compute_average(numbers)
total = sum(numbers)
return total/length(numbers)
end
On the other hand, the following names would not be allowed:
julia> function 123_invalid_name()
# this is not allowed because it starts with a digit
end
julia> function function()
# this is not allowed because  function  is a built-in keyword
end
The Arguments Used in the Function
In Julia, arguments are the values that are passed to a function when it is
called. A functio n can have any number of arguments, and the arguments can
be of any type. To deﬁne a function that takes arguments, you specify the
names of the arguments in the function deﬁnition, separated by commas. For
example,
julia> function add(x, y)
return x + y
end
This deﬁnes a function add that takes two arguments, x and y. When calling
the function, you pass the arguments in the same order as they are deﬁned in
the function deﬁnition: julia> result = add(3, 4)
This would call the add function  with the arguments 3 and 4, and assign the
result (7) to the variable result.
You can also deﬁne functions with multiple arguments, keywor d arguments,
and default values for ar guments. For example,
julia> function saluer(name; greeting=”Bonjour”,
punctuation=”!”)
println(“$greeting, $name$punctuation”)
end
This deﬁnes a function saluer that takes a single positional argument: name
and two keyword arguments: greeting and punctuation. The keyword
arguments have default values, so you can call the function with just the
name ar gument:
julia> saluer(“Julia”)
This will print Bonjour, Julia!.  You can also specify the values of the
keyword ar guments by name:
julia> greet(“Julia”, greeting=”Bonsoir”, punctuation=”!!”)
This will print Bonsoir, Julia!!.
The Body of the Function
In Julia, the body of a function is the block of code that is executed when the
function is called. The body of a function is typically deﬁned between the
function keyword and the end keyword and consists of one or more
statements that perform some action or calculation. You can also deﬁne
functions with more complex bodies, consistin g of multiple statements and
control structures such as if statements, for loops, and while loops.
The return Statement of the Function
In Julia, the return statement is used to specify the value that a function
should return when it is ﬁnished  executing. When the function is called, the
result of the return statement will be the value that is returned to the caller .
result = add(3, 4) would call the add function with the arguments 3 and
4, and assign the result (7) to the variable result.
The return statement is optional in Julia. If a function doesn’ t have a return
statement, or the return statemen t is not reached, the function will return the
special value nothing. This can be useful when you want a function to
perform an action, but don’ t need to return a speciﬁc value.
julia> function saluer(name; greeting=”Bonjour”,
punctuation=”!”)
println(“$greeting, $name$punctuation”)
end
julia> saluer(“Julia”)
This function does not have a return statement, so it will return nothing when
it is called.
Positional and keyword arguments
Positional arguments in Julia are arguments that are passed to a function in a
speciﬁc order , based on the position of the argument in the function
deﬁnition. For example,
Figure 3.2:  Positional and keywor d arguments of a function
julia> function greet(greeting, name)
println(“$greeting, $name”)
end
julia> greet(“Hello”, “Julia”)
Here, the greet function takes two arguments: greeting and name. When the
function is called with the arguments Hello  and Julia , the values are
assigned to the greeting and name arguments in the order in which they are
deﬁned in the function deﬁnition.
Keyword arguments in Julia are arguments that are passed to a function by
name, rather than by position. For example,
julia> function greet(; greeting=”Hello”, name)
println(“$greeting, $name”)
end
julia> greet(name=”Julia”, greeting=”Hola”)
Here, the greet function takes two arguments: a keyword argument greeting
with a default value of Hello , and a positional argument name. When the
function is called with the arguments name=”Julia ” and greeting=”Hola” ,
the values are assigned to the name and greeting arguments based on the
names of the ar guments, rather than their position.
Here is a pictor ial representatio n of the difference between positional and
keyword
arguments: Positional arguments:
function greet(greeting, name)
greet(“Hello”, “Julia”)
-------------------
| greeting | name |
-------------------
| “Hello” | “Julia” |
-------------------
Keyword arguments:
function greet(; greeting=”Hello”, name)
greet(name=”Julia”, greeting=”Hola”)
-------------------
| greeting | name |
-------------------
| “Hola” | “Julia” |
-------------------
Passing arguments in Julia
The use of the = assignment to set the default values of the parameters
demonstrates another essential aspect of Julia. If you send a value to a
function, Julia binds the function parameter name to this value, in the same
way, binding a variable to a value works. Adding! as a sufﬁx to the function
name (func!)  is a stylistic practice that informs users that the function’ s
parameters may change. Here is an example of a function named f! that
alters the contents of an array by adding 1 to each element:
julia> function func!(arr)
for i in 1:length(arr)
arr[i] += 1
end
end
# Test the function
julia> arr = [1, 2, 3, 4]
julia> func!(arr)
julia> println(arr) # Output: [2, 3, 4, 5]
Here is another example of a function named func! that alters
the contents of a dictionary by adding a key-value pair:
julia> function func!(dict)
dict[“new key”] = “new value”
end
# Test the function
julia> dict = {“key1” => “value1”, “key2” => “value2”}
julia> func!(dict)
println(dict) # Output: Dict(“key1”=>”value1”,
“key2”=>”value2”, “new key”=>”new value”)
Note that the ! at the end of the function name is a convention that signals to
users that the function may modify its arguments. It is not required for the
function to actually modify its arguments, but it is good practice to use this
convention to indicate to other users of the function that it may have side
effects.
Short Syntax for Writing Functions in Julia
You can also use the short syntax for deﬁning functions, which omits the
function keywor d and uses the = symbol to separate the function name and
arguments from  the function body . For example, you can deﬁne an add
function that takes in x, and y as its arguments and returns the sum. julia>
add(x, y) = x + y
When passing a function as an argument to another function, it is common to
deﬁne a function that does not require a name. We just want to deﬁne such a
function momen tarily and then pass it to another function. In Julia, such
unnamed functions are referred to as anonymous functions.
The syntax is quite similar to the brief syntax we studied before, except that
the function name is skipped, and = is replaced with ->.
The input to the sum function in this example is a function and a list. The
function is deﬁned as julia> x -> x ^ 2, which is a shorthand way of
writing an anonymous function that takes an input x and return s x squared.
The list is [1, 2, 3].
The sum functio n applies the anonymous function to each element of the list
and returns the sum of the results. In this case, the anonymous function is
applied to the elements 1, 2, and 3 in turn, resulting in the value s 1, 4, and 9.
The sum of these values is 14, which is the output of the sum function. So
the code sum(x -> x ^ 2, [1, 2, 3]) calculates the sum of the squares of
the elements in the list [1, 2, 3],  which is 14.
Type System in Julia
The type system in Julia is a powerful tool that helps the language to be fast
and efﬁcient. It allows us to specify the types of variables and function
arguments, whic h can help the compiler to generate more efﬁci ent code and
also prevent common mistakes. We will learn about the different types in
Julia and how to use them ef fectively .
The ability to deﬁne multiple methods for a single function is a powerful
feature of Julia, and it is made possible by the language’ s strong type system.
In Julia,  types are used to represent the kind of data that a variable can hold,
and they play a central role in determining which method of a function will
be called.
In Julia , a function can have multiple methods, which are different
implementations of the function that are selected based on the types of
arguments passed to the function. This is known as multiple dispatch, and it
is one of the key features of Julia.
For example, consider the following function deﬁnition:
julia> function f(x, y)
println(“default method”)
end
julia> f(x::Int, y::Int) = println(“method for integer
arguments”) julia> f(x::Float64, y::Float64) = println(“method
for float arguments”)
In this case, the function f has three metho ds: the default method, which is
selected if none of the other methods match the types of the arguments, and
two specialized methods that are selected if the arguments are integers or
ﬂoating-point numbers, respectively .
To call a speciﬁc method, you can use the @which macro, like this:
julia> @which f(1, 2)
julia> @which f(1.0, 2.0)
This will print out the code for the speciﬁc method that will be called in each
case. In the function deﬁnition above, the specialized methods for f are
selected based on the types of the arguments x and y, which must be either
integers or ﬂoating-point numbers. This allows the programm er to write
efﬁcient and specialized code for different types of data, and it also helps the
Julia compiler to generate highly optimized code. Overall, the combination
of multi ple dispatch and a stron g type system makes Julia a powerful and
ﬂexible programming language for a wide range of applications.
In Julia,  every value has a type,  which determines the set of operations that
can be performed on that value. Julia has a rich set of built-in types,
including Int, Float64, String, and Array , as well as a number of composite
types such as Tuple and Dict. Julia also supports user-deﬁned types,
allowing you to create custom types that can be used in your code.
In Julia,  types are organized into a hierarchy , with more general types at the
top and more speciﬁc types at the bottom. This allows you to deﬁne methods
for a function that apply to a range of related types.
For example, consider the following function deﬁnition:
julia> function f(x)
println(“default method”)
end
julia> f(x::Number) = println(“method for numbers”)
julia> f(x::Integer) = println(“method for integers”)
julia> f(x::Real) = println(“method for reals”)
In this case, the function f has four methods: the default metho d, and three
specialized meth ods that are selected based on the type of the argument x.
The Number method will be selected if x is any kind of number , including
integers and ﬂoating-point numb ers. The Integer method will be selected if x
is an integer . The Real method will be selected if x is a real number , which
includes both integers and ﬂoating-point numbers.
This allows you to write meth ods that apply to a range of related types,
while still allow ing you to write  specialized methods for more speciﬁc types
if needed. It also allows the Julia compiler to generate highly efﬁcient code
by selecting the most speciﬁ c method that matches the type of the
arguments.
The type system in Julia is a system for classifying and organizi ng data types
in the language. This is an important feature of Julia because it allows the
compiler to optimize code for speciﬁc data types, leading to faster execution
times and fewer runtime errors.
One of the key features of the type system in Julia is its support for
parametric types, which allow you to deﬁne types that are parameterized by
other types. This allows you to write generic code that can work with a
variety of different types. For example, you can deﬁne a Vector{T}  type that
represents a vector of values of type T, allowing you to write code that works
with vectors of any type.
Julia’ s type system includes support for parametric types, which are types
that can take one or more parameters to specify more speciﬁc types. This
allows you to deﬁne types that are parameterized by other types, and it is a
powerful feature that enables a wide range of programming techniques. In
Julia, parametric types are a way to deﬁne types that can be parameterized
by other  types. They are similar to generics in other programming languages,
and they allow you to write ﬂexible and reusable code that can work with a
wide range of types.
For example, consider the Vector type in Julia, which is deﬁned as
follows: julia> immutable Vector{T}
data::Array{T}
end
In this deﬁnition, Vector is a parametric type that takes a single type
parameter T, which speciﬁes the type of the elements in the vector . This
means that you can create a Vector of any type by specify ing the type
parameter when you create a new instance of the type:
julia> v1 = Vector{Int}([1, 2, 3])
julia> v2 = Vector{Float64}([1.0, 2.0, 3.0])
In this case, v1 is a Vector of integers and v2 is a Vector of ﬂoating-point
numbers.
Parametric types are a powerful feature of Julia’ s type system  that allows
you to write generic and ﬂexible code that can work with a wide range of
types. They are often used in combination with multiple dispatch to deﬁne
functions that can work with a variety of different types in a type-safe and
efﬁcient way .
For example, consider the following deﬁnition of a Vector type:
julia> struct Vector{T}
data::Array{T}
end
Here, the Vector type is parame terized by the type T, which represents the
type of the elem ents in the vecto r. This means that you can deﬁne a Vector of
any type by specifying the type parameter when you create an instance of the
type:
julia> v1 = Vector{Int}([1, 2, 3]) # a vector of integers
julia> v2 = Vector{Float64}([1.0, 2.0, 3.0])
# a vector of floating-point numbers
You can also deﬁne methods for the Vector type that are param eterized by
the type of the elements. For example:
julia> function dot(v::Vector{T}, u::Vector{T}) where T
# compute dot product of v and u
end
This method will work for any type of Vector as long as the element types
are the same.
Parametric types are a powerful feature of Julia, and they are used in many
built-in types and libraries. They  allow you to write code that is generic and
ﬂexible, while still taking advan tage of the strong type system and multiple
dispatch features of Julia.
Hierarchy in Julia’s Type System
In Julia,  types can be organize d into a hierarchy , with subtypes inheriting
properties and behaviors from their supertypes. This is similar to the way
that classes work in object-oriented languages like Python or Java.
Figure 3.3:  Type hierar chy in Julia
For example, consider the following type of hierarchy:
Any
|
AbstractString
|
|---- ASCIIString
|
|---- UTF8String
|
|---- UTF16String
|
|---- UTF32String
In this hierarchy , AbstractString is a supertype of all of the string types, and
Any is the top-level supertype of all types in Julia. This means that a
variable of type AbstractString can hold any kind of string, and a variable of
type Any can hold any kind of value.
When deﬁning methods for a function, you can use the type hierarchy to
deﬁne methods that apply to a range of types. For example, you could deﬁne
a method that applies to all types of strings by using the Abstra ctString type
as the ar gument type:
f(s::AbstractString) = println(“input is a string”)
This method will be called for any input that is a subtype of AbstractString,
including ASCIIString, UTF8String, UTF16String, and UTF32String.
Using the type hierarchy in this way allows you to write generic  and ﬂexible
code that can handle a wide range of types, while still allowing you to deﬁne
specialized methods for speciﬁc types when needed.
In Julia, types are arranged in a hierarchy , with a root type calle d Any at the
top. All other types in Julia are subtypes of Any. This means that any value
of any type in Julia can be stored in a variable of type Any. For Example:
julia> x = 1
1
julia> y = “hello”
“hello”
julia> z = [1, 2, 3]
[1, 2, 3]
julia> a: Any = x
1
julia> b: Any = y
“hello”
julia> c: Any = z
[1, 2, 3]
In Julia,  it is also possible to deﬁne custom types, and these types will also
be subtypes of Any. For example:
julia> struct MyType
field::Int
end
julia> x = MyType(1)
MyType(1)
julia> y: Any = x
MyType(1)
It is also possible to deﬁne subtypes of existing types in Julia using the <:
operator . For example:
julia> struct MySubType <: MyType
field::Int
extra_field::Int
end
julia> x = MySubType(1, 2)
MySubType(1, 2)
julia> y: MyType = x
MySubType(1, 2)
julia> z: Any = x
MySubType(1, 2)
This type hierarchy allows for powerful type-based dispatch in Julia, where
methods can be deﬁned to apply to speciﬁc subtypes of a given type.
To ﬁnd all the supertypes of a given type in Julia, you can use the supertypes
function. This function takes a type as an argument and returns a tuple of all
the supertypes of that type.
For exam ple, to ﬁnd all the supertypes of the Int64  type, you can do the
following:
julia> supertypes(Int64)
(Signed, Integer, Real, Number, Any)
This shows that the Int64 type is a subtype of the Signed, Integer, Real,
Number , and Any abstract types.
julia> super(Int64)
(Signed, Integer, Real, Number, Any)
This returns the same result as supertypes because Int64  directly inherits
from all of its supertypes. However , if you use super on a type that inherits
indirectly from some of its supertypes, you will see those supertypes in the
result as well.
Note : supertypes()  function only returns the supertypes that are directly
inherited by the given type. To ﬁnd all the supertypes of a type, including
indirect supertypes, you can use the super function. This function returns a
tuple of all the supertypes of a given type, including indirect supertypes, in
the order that they are inherited.
You can also use the <: operator to check if a type is a subtype of another
type. For examp le, to check if the Int64 type is a subtype of the Integer type,
you can do the following:
julia> Int64 <: Integer
true
The supertype function only returns direct supertypes, not transitive
supertypes. For example, if you want to ﬁnd all the transitive supertypes of
the Int64  type (i.e., all the supertypes of Int64, as well as the supertypes of
those supertype s, and so on), you can use the transitive_supertype()
function:
julia> transitive_supertype(Int64)
(Signed, Integer, Real, AbstractFloat, FloatingPoint,
RealDomain, Number, Any)
To ﬁnd all supertypes of a type, including the immediate supertype and all of
its supertypes, you can use a recursive function like the following:
julia> function all_supertypes(T)
supertypes = []
while true
S = supertype(T)
if S == Any
return supertypes
end
push!(supertypes, S)
T = S
end
end
Here’ s an example of how you can use this function:
julia> all_supertypes(Int)
3-element Array{Any,1}:
Signed
Real
AbstractFloat
This function will return an array of all supertypes of the given type, with
the most immediate supertype ﬁrst and the ultimate supertype (Any) last.
In Julia, you can use the subtypes function to ﬁnd the immediate  subtypes of
a given type. For example:
julia> subtypes(Real)
2-element Array{Union{DataType, UnionAll},1}:
AbstractFloat
Integer
To ﬁnd all subtypes of a type, including the immediate subtypes and all of
their subtypes, you can use a recursive function like the following:
julia> function all_subtypes(T)
subtypes = []
for S in subtypes(T)
push!(subtypes, S)
push!(subtypes, all_subtypes(S))
end
return subtypes
end
Here’ s an example of how you can use this function:
julia> all_subtypes(Real)
4-element Array{Any,1}:
AbstractFloat
Float16
Float32
Float64
Integer
BigInt
Note : This function will return an array of all subtypes of the given type,
with the most immediate subtyp es ﬁrst and the ultimate subtypes last. This
will also include all subtypes of the immediate subtypes, and so on,
recursively .
You can also use the subtypes()  function  to ﬁnd all subtypes of a particular
type that have been deﬁned in the current environment. For example:
julia> subtypes(Real)
2-element Array{Union{DataType, UnionAll},1}:
AbstractFloat
Integer
julia> subtypes(AbstractFloat)
3-element Array{Union{DataType, UnionAll},1}:
Float16
Float32
Float64
This can be useful if you want to ﬁnd all subtypes of a particular type that
have been deﬁned in your code, rather than all subtypes of a type in the
entire Julia language.
subtypes()  function returns a Vector of all subtypes of the given type,
including abstract and concrete types. Here’ s an example of how you can use
the subtypes ( ) function:
julia> subtypes(Number)
2-element Vector{Any}:
Complex
Real
julia> subtypes(Real)
4-element Vector{Any}:
AbstractFloat
AbstractIrrational
Integer
Rational
In Julia, you can use the Union type to represent the union of two or more
types. The Union type is a generic type that takes a variable number of type
arguments. For example, you can create a Union of two types T1 and T2 like
this:
julia> Union{T1, T2}
You can then use this Union type to represent values that can be of either the
T1 or T2 type. For example:
julia> x = Union{Int, String}(5)
5
julia> x = Union{Int, String}(“hello”)
“hello”
You can also use the Union type to deﬁne a function that takes arguments of
either T1 or T2 type:
julia> function foo(x::Union{Int, String})
if isa(x, Int)
println(“x is an Int: “, x)
elseif isa(x, String)
println(“x is a String: “, x)
else
println(“x is of an unexpected type”)
end
end
foo (generic function with 1 method)
julia> foo(5)
x is an Int: 5
julia> foo(“hello”)
x is a String: hello
Note : The Union type is not the same as the union function in Julia, which is
used to compute the union of two sets.
When deﬁning a function or method in Julia, it’s generally a good idea to
specify the types of arguments and return value as accurately as possible in
the function signature. This helps to ensure that the function is called with
the corre ct types of arguments and can help the compiler to generate more
efﬁcient code.
There are a few factors to consider when deciding what type restrictions to
put in a method signature:
Usefulness : The type restrictions should be useful in helping the
function to perform its intended task. For example, if a function
expects a matrix as an argumen t, it would be useful to restrict the type
of argument to AbstractMatrix .
Precision:  The type restri ctions should be as precise as possible. For
example, if a function expects a matrix with ﬂoating point elem ents, it
would be more precise to restrict the type of the argument to
AbstractMatrix{<:FloatingPoint}  rather than just AbstractMatrix.
Performance:  The type restrictions should be chosen with
performance in mind. For example, if a function expects a matrix with
ﬂoating point elements, it would be more efﬁcient to use
AbstractMatrix{<:FloatingPoint}  rather  than AbstractMatrix
because the former allows the compiler to generate more efﬁcient code.
Flexibility : The type restric tions should be ﬂexible enough to allow the
function to be used in a wide range of contexts. For example, if a
function expects a matrix with ﬂoating point elements, it would be
more ﬂexible to use
AbstractMatrix{<:FloatingPoint}  rather than
Matrix{Float64}  because the former allows the function to be
used with any type of ﬂoatin g point matrix, not just Float 64
matrices.
Ultimately , the choice of type restrictions will depend on the speciﬁc needs
and constraints of the function you are deﬁning. It’s usually a good idea to
start with the most general type restrictions that are still useful, and then
make them more speciﬁc as needed.
In Julia,  compos ite types are user-deﬁned types that are composed of a set of
ﬁelds, each of which has a name and a type. Composite types are similar to
structs or classes in other programming languages. Here is an example of
how you can deﬁne a composite type in Julia:
julia> struct Person
name::String
age::Int
end
This deﬁnes a type called Person  that has two ﬁelds: name, which is a String,
and age, which is an Int.
You can create an instance of a composite type by using the Person
constructor and providing values for the ﬁelds:
jane = Person(“Jane”, 30)
You can access the ﬁelds of a composite type using dot notation:
println(jane.name) # prints “Jane”
println(jane.age) # prints 30
You can also deﬁne methods for a composite type. For example, you might
deﬁne a greet method for the Person type like this:
function greet(p::Person)
println(“Hello, my name is $(p.name) and I am $(p.age) years
old.”)
end
greet(jane) # prints “Hello, my name is Jane and I am 30 years
old.”
Composite types are a powerful feature of Julia that allows you to create
custom data structures with associated behavior . You can learn more about
composite types in the Julia documentation.
Various Data Types in Julia
Julia has two major categories  of data types - concrete and abstract. A
concrete data type is a speciﬁc implementation of a data type, while an
abstract data type is a set of operations that can be performed on a data type,
without specifying how those operations are implemented.
Concrete data types are typicall y deﬁned using the struct or mutable struct
keyword in Julia. There are many concrete types in Julia that implement the
AbstractArray interface, including Array, SparseMatrixCSC , and
StridedArray , each of which represents arrays in a different way. For
example, the following code deﬁnes a concrete data type called Person :
struct Person
name::String
age::Int
end
This data type has two ﬁelds: name, which is a string, and age, which is an
integer . To create an instance of this data type, you can use the following
syntax:
jane = Person(“Jane”, 30)
You can then access the fields of the Person instance using dot
notation: jane.name # Returns “Jane”
jane.age # Returns 30
Abstract data types, on the other hand, are deﬁned using the abstract
keyword in Julia. An abstract data type is essentially a templa te for a data
type, specifying  the operations that can be performed on it, but not the
details of how those operations are implemented.
For example, the following code deﬁnes an abstract data type called Stack:
abstract type Stack
push!(stack::Stack, item)
pop!(stack::Stack)
top(stack::Stack)
end
This abstract data type deﬁnes three operations: push!,  which adds an item
to the stack; pop!, which removes the top item from the stack; and top,
which returns the top item from the stack.
To implement an abstract data type, you can deﬁne a concrete data type that
implements the operations speciﬁed in the abstract data type. For example,
the following code deﬁnes a concrete data type called ArrayStack  that
implements the Stack  abstract data type using an array:
struct ArrayStack <: Stack
items::Array{Any,1}
function push!(stack::ArrayStack, item)
push!(stack.items, item)
end
function pop!(stack::ArrayStack)
pop!(stack.items)
end
function top(stack::ArrayStack)
stack.items[end]
end
end
You can then use the ArrayStack  data type like any other concrete data type,
but it will also have all the operations speciﬁed in the Stack abstract data
type. For example, in Julia, the AbstractArray type is an abstract data type
that represents a multi-dimensional array of values. It deﬁnes operations like
size , getindex , and setindex!,  which allow you to manipulate arrays in a
generic way , regardless of the underlying representation of the array .
Julia also has a number of built-in abstract data types that are used to
represent common data structu res, such as lists, queues, and sets. These
types deﬁne a set of operations that can be performed on the data structure,
and different concrete types can be used to implement those operations in
different ways. For example, the AbstractSet  type represents a set of
values, and concrete types like Set and Dict  can be used to implement sets
in dif ferent ways.
In additi on to the built-in abstra ct data types, you can also deﬁne your own
abstract data types in Julia. To do this, you can use the abstract keyword to
deﬁne a type that represents a set of operations, and then deﬁne concrete
types that implement those operations. For example, you might deﬁne an
abstract type called Shape that represents a geometric shape, and then deﬁne
concrete types like Circle , Rectangle , and Triangle  that implement the
operations deﬁned by the Shape type.
An immutable value is one that cannot be changed after it has been created.
For example, integers and ﬂoating-point numbers are immutable in Julia, as
are strings and symbols. When you assign an immutable value to a variable,
the variable becomes a referenc e to the value. You can change the value of
the varia ble by assigning it a new value, but you cannot modify the value
itself.
A mutable value, on the other  hand, can be modiﬁed after it has been
created. Arrays, dictionaries, and composite types are all mutable in Julia.
When you assign a mutable value to a variable, the variable becomes a
reference to the value, just as with immutable values. However , you can also
modify the value itself by using functions or methods that change its state. In
Julia, the type system is aware of mutability , which means that you can
deﬁne types that are either mutable or immutable. This can be useful when
you want to ensure that certain values are never modiﬁed, or when you want
to allow values of a certain type to be modiﬁed in certain ways. To deﬁne an
immutable type in Julia, you can use the immutable keyword when deﬁning
the type. Startin g from Julia 0.7, immutable keyword has been replaced with
struct . For example,
struct Point
x::Float64
y::Float64
end
This deﬁnes an immutable type called Point , which has two ﬁelds: x and y,
both of which are Float64 values. You can create an instance of Point using
the constructor and access its ﬁelds using dot notation, just as with any other
composite type:
p = Point(1.0, 2.0)
println(p.x) # prints 1.0
println(p.y) # prints 2.0
However , you cannot modify the ﬁelds of an instance of Point after it has
been created. If you try to do so, you will get an error . For example:
p.x = 3.0 # error: cannot assign to immutable field `x`
To deﬁn e a mutable type in Julia , you can use the mutable struct syntax. For
example,
mutable struct MutablePoint
x::Float64
y::Float64
end
This deﬁnes a mutable type called MutablePoint  which has the same ﬁelds
as the Point type. You can create an instance of MutablePoint  using the
constructor and access its ﬁelds using dot notation, just as with any other
composite type.  However , you can also modify the ﬁelds of an instance of
MutablePoint  after it has been created:
mp = MutablePoint(1.0, 2.0)
mp.x = 3.0
println(mp.x) # prints 3.0
In gener al, it is a good idea to use immutable types whenever possible in
Julia, since they can be easier to work with and can lead to more predictable
and reliable code. However , mutable types can be usefu l in certain
situations, such as when you need to store and modify lar ge amounts of data.
The type system in Julia is also closely tied to the multiple dispatch feature
of the language. Multiple dispatch allows you to deﬁne multiple  methods for
a function, each specialized for a particular combination of argument types.
This allows the function to behave differently depending on the types of its
arguments, making it easier to write code that is expressive  and efﬁcient.
Overall, the type system in Julia is a key feature of the language that allows
you to write code that is optimiz ed for speciﬁc data types and that can work
with a variety of dif ferent types.
Multiple Dispatch in Julia
Multiple dispatc h is a unique feature of Julia that allows us to deﬁne
functions that can behave differently depending on the types of their
arguments. This allows us to write code that is both ﬂexible and efﬁcient, as
the correct implementation of a function can be selected at runtime based on
the types of its ar guments.
Figure 3.4:  Multiple Dispatch in Julia
In Julia , the method ambiguity problem occurs when there are multiple
methods deﬁned for a function that could be selected to handle a particular
set of argumen ts. This can occur when the methods have overlapping
deﬁnitions in terms of the types of their ar guments.
For example, consider the following function:
function foo(x::Int, y::Int)
return “Int, Int”
end
function foo(x::Int, y::Float64)
return “Int, Float64”
end
function foo(x::Float64, y::Int)
return “Float64, Int”
end
function foo(x::Float64, y::Float64)
return “Float64, Float64”
end
In this example, the foo function has four methods deﬁned, each with a
different combin ation of argument types. If we call foo with Int arguments,
both the ﬁrst and second methods could poten tially be selected. Similarly , if
we call foo with Float64 arguments, all four methods could potentially be
selected.
When this happens, Julia will raise a MethodError , indicating that the
method selectio n process was ambiguous and could not determine which
method to execute.
To avoid  the method ambiguity problem, it is important to carefully consider
the types of arguments that a function will accept and to ensure that the
methods are deﬁned in a way that avoids overlap. For example, in the case of
the foo function above, we could deﬁne the methods in a different order to
avoid ambiguity:
function foo(x::Int, y::Int)
return “Int, Int”
end
function foo(x::Float64, y::Float64)
return “Float64, Float64”
end
function foo(x::Int, y::Float64)
return “Int, Float64”
end
function foo(x::Float64, y::Int)
return “Float64, Int”
end
With this deﬁnition, the metho d selection process will always be able to
determine the most speciﬁc method to execute based on the types of
arguments. This is called multiple dispatch .
In Julia, multiple dispatch is a feature that allows a function to behave
differently based on the types of its arguments. This is different from
traditional funct ion overloading,  which determines the behavior of a function
based on the number or types  of its arguments, but does not allow the
function to behave differently depending on the combination of argument
types. Multiple dispatch is achieved by deﬁning multiple methods for a
function, each of which is specialized for a particular combination of
argument types. When a functio n is called with a set of argumen ts, Julia will
select the most speciﬁc method that matches the argument types and execute
it.
To use multiple dispatch in a function, you ﬁrst deﬁne the function and its
methods. Each method is deﬁne d by specifying the function name, the types
of its arguments, and the body of the method. Here is an example of how
multiple dispatch can be used in Julia:
julia> function add(x::Int, y::Int)
return x + y
end
add (generic function with 1 method)
julia> function add(x::Float64, y::Float64)
return x + y
end
add (generic function with 2 methods)
julia> add(1, 2)
3
julia> add(1.0, 2.0)
3.0
In this example, the add function has two methods deﬁned: one for Int
arguments and another for Float64  argume nts. When add is called with Int
arguments, the ﬁrst method is selected and executed, while the second
method is selected and executed  when add is called with Float64  argume nts.
This allows the add function to behave differently depending on the types of
its ar guments.
Multiple dispatc h is a useful feature because it allows a function to behave
differently based on the types of its arguments. This allows you to write code
that is more ﬂexible and efﬁcie nt, as it can adapt to a variety  of different
input types and situations.
One of the main beneﬁts of multiple dispatch is that it allows you to write
code that is more expressive  and intuitive. With traditional function
overloading, you might have to write separate functions for different
combinations of argument types, leading to code that is more difﬁcult to read
and understand. With multiple dispatch, you can deﬁne a single function
with multiple methods, each specialized for a particular combination of
argument types, making it easier  to understand how the function will behave
in dif ferent situations.
Additionally , multiple dispatch can lead to more efﬁcient code because the
most speciﬁc method for a given  set of argument types is selected at runtime,
allowing the code  to take advantage of type information that may not be
available at compile time. This can lead to faster execution times and fewer
runtime errors.
Overall, multiple dispatch is a powerful feature of Julia that allows you to
write more expressive, intuitive, and efﬁcient code.
Managing code structure in Julia
Managing packages and modules is an important aspect of working with
Julia. In Julia, you can use modules to structure your code and keep related
functions and variables together in a single ﬁle or a set of ﬁles. A module is a
collection of Julia code that is deﬁned in a separate ﬁle, and it can be used to
organize your code into logical units. Modules allow us to organize our code
into logical groups and prevent naming conﬂicts.
Modules are used to specify distinct variable namespaces. Each module
speciﬁes its own independent global scope. The default global scope in Julia
is called the Main module . To create a module, you can use the module
keyword followed by the name of the module and a block of code:
julia> module MyModule
export myfunc
function myfunc()
# code for myfunc
println(“Aloha!”)
end
end # defining MyModule
Main.MyModule
The export keyword speciﬁes which functions, types, and constants deﬁned
in the module should be made available to users of the module. You can also
use the using keyword to impo rt all the exports from a module into your
current namespace:
using MyModule
myfunc()
You can also selectively import speciﬁc functions, types, or constants from a
module using the import keyword:
import MyModule.myfunc
myfunct()
Modules can also contain types and constants. For example,
module MyModule
export MyType, MY_CONSTANT
struct MyType
# fields for MyType
W
b
end
const MY_CONSTANT = 3.14
end #defining a module
Main.MyModule
end keyword marks the end of the module in Julia. Usually , we comment on
the code at the end keyword to show that the module ends at that time. If you
are reproducing the code using a Julia REPL, then you can see that the
output shows the namespace and the module name. In this case, the REPL
prints out Main.MyModule.  You can use the exported types and constants in
the same way as exported functions.
In Julia, you can use the includ e function to include the contents of another
ﬁle or module in the current module. This can be useful for breaking up large
modules into smaller ﬁles or for including code from externa l libraries in
your module. To use the include function, you simply pass the ﬁle or module
that you want to include as an ar gument. For example,
module MyModule
include(“foo.jl”)
include(“bar.jl”)
end
This will include the content s of the foo.jl  and bar.jl  ﬁles in the
MyModule module. You can also use the include function to include the
contents of another module. For example,
module MyModule
include(OtherModule)
end
This will include the contents of the OtherModule  module  in the MyModule
module. It is important to note that the include function does not import the
deﬁnitions from the included ﬁle or module into the current namespace.
Instead, it simply evaluates the code in the included ﬁle or module within the
context of the current module. To make the deﬁnitions from an included ﬁle
or module available to your code, you can use the using or import keywords.
Modules can also be nested, with inner modules being able to access the
exports of their outer modules. This can be useful for organizing your code
into logical hiera rchies. Module s are a useful way to organize and reuse your
code in Julia. They allow you to group related deﬁnitions together and
provide a clear separation between dif ferent parts of your code.
In Julia,  packag es are collections of modules (that is, Julia code ﬁles) that
provide additional functionality for your programs. Packages are collections
of Julia code that can be easily shared and reused. When we register a
module at the Julia general registry
(https://github.com/JuliaRegistries/General ), it becomes a package. This
package is available to other users who want to use the function s, types, and
constants from the package in their code.
We learnt to add a speciﬁc package to our current code by using Julia’ s
package manage r, Pkg. Once the package is added to the namespace, we can
access the package at any time, even after you reload a new Julia session.
Once a package  is installed, you can use it in your code by using the using
keyword followe d by the name of the package. This will import the exported
deﬁnitions from the package into the current namespace.
using ExamplePackage
# now you can use the definitions from the ExamplePackage
package
You can also import speciﬁc deﬁnitions from a package using the import
keyword. For example:
import ExamplePackage: foo
# now you can use the foo function from the ExamplePackage
package
Packages in Julia are often distributed as compiled code, whic h means that
they are pre compiled for a speciﬁc platform and can be loaded into Julia
quickly . This makes packages an efﬁcient way to include large amounts of
code in your projects. In addition to reusable code, Julia packages can also
include documentation, tests, and other resources. You can use the Pkg
package manager to access these resources and manage the packages that
you have install ed. Overall, packages are a powerful tool for organizing and
sharing code in Julia, and they are an important part of the Julia ecosystem.
Using Macros
Macros are a powerful feature that allows us to modify the behavior of the
language itself and are often used to create domain-speciﬁc languages or to
optimize performance-critical code. We will learn how to deﬁne and use
macros in Julia, and how they can help us to write more expressive and
efﬁcient code.
In Julia, a macro is a piece of code that is executed at compile-time to
generate other code. Macros allow you to generate code dynamically and can
be used to implement powerful and ﬂexible abstractions in Julia.
To deﬁne a macro in Julia, you can use the @ symbol followed by the name
of the macro and any ar guments that the macro takes. For example:
macro mymacro(x)
quote
# Code generated by the macro goes here
end
end
The quote  and end keywor ds deﬁne  a block of code that is treated as a single
expression and can be passed around and manipulated like any other value in
Julia.
To use a macro, you can call it like a function, using the @ symbol  followe d
by the name of the macro and any ar guments that it takes. For example:
@mymacro(42)
Inside the body of the macro, you can use the esc function to escape the
code generated by the macro and the: operator to unquote symbols. For
example:
macro mymacro(x)
quote
y = $(esc(x)) + 1
z = :($(y))
end
end
This macro takes a single argument x and generates code that adds 1 to x and
creates a symbol with the name of the resulting value.
macro my_macro(x)
return :($x + 1)
end
This macro deﬁnes a simple macro that takes an argument x and returns a
new expression that increments x by 1.
To use a macro, you can call it like a function and pass it the arguments that
you want to use as inputs. The macro will then generate the corresponding
code and return  the value. Otherwise, you can call the macro  without the
parenthesis with just a space in between the macro name and the argument
used. For example:
julia> @my_macro(2)
3
julia> @my_macro 2
3
Macros are functions that accept a parsed reference of Julia code and return
the transformed code. Macros work at the level of abstract syntax trees
(https://en.wikipedia.or g/wiki/Abstract_syntax_tree ). Macro s are performed
after Julia code has been parsed  but before it has been compiled. Macros are
a powerful feature of Julia that allow you to generate code dynamically and
create abstractions that are not possible with normal functions. They are
often used to implement domain-speciﬁc languages, optimize performance,
and simplify code by hiding complex details behind a simple interface.
We will discuss some of the most used macros in Julia like time, elapsed,
assert and benchmark.
The time macro in Julia is a tool for measuring the performance of code. It
can be used to measure the time it takes to execute a block of code or to
measure the time it takes to execute a speciﬁc function or expression.
To use the time macro, you can use the @time  or @elapsed  macro. The @time
macro will execute the code that follows it and print the wall-clock time, the
CPU time, and the allocated memory used by the code. The @elapsed  macro
will only print the wall-clock time that the code took to run.
For example, you can use the @time macro to measure the time it takes to
run a function like this:
@time my_function()
Or you can use the @elapsed macro to measure the time it takes to evaluate
an expression like this:
@elapsed result = my_expression
The @time  macro will print the wall clock time, the CPU time, and the
allocated memo ry used by the code. You can also use the @ela psed macro,
which will only print the wall-clock time that the code took to run.
@time begin
# code to measure goes here
end
@elapsed begin
# code to measure goes here
end
Both @time  and @elapsed  can be used to measure the performance of
speciﬁc functions or expressions.
It’s important to note that the time it takes to execute code can vary
depending on a number of factors, such as the speed of the CPU, the
workload of the system, and the presence of other running processes. As
such, it is generally a good idea to take multiple measurements and calculate
an average time when trying to determine the performance of code.
Here is an example of how you can use the @time macro in Julia to measure
the time it takes to run a function:
# Define a function that takes some time to run
function slow_function()
sleep(1) # pause for 1 second
end
# Measure the time it takes to run the function
@time slow_function()
The output of this code might look something like this:
0.000352 seconds (5 allocations: 176 bytes)
The @time  macro will print the wall clock time, the CPU time, and the
allocated memory used by the code. In this case, the function took about
0.000352 second s to run, and it required 5 memory allocation s that used a
total of 176 bytes.
You can also use the @elapsed  macro to measu re the time it takes to run a
function. For example:
# Define a function that takes some time to run
function slow_function()
sleep(1) # pause for 1 second
end
# Measure the time it takes to run the function
@elapsed slow_function()
The output of this code might look something like this:
1.000625 seconds
The @elapsed  macro will only print the wall-c lock time that the code took to
run. In this case, the function took about 1.000625 seconds to run.
The assert macro in Julia is used to check if a condition is true, and throw an
error if it is not. It is commonly used as a way to validate the correctness of
code and to catch and handle errors that might occur during execution.
To use the assert macro, you provide a condition as the ﬁrst argument and an
optional error message as the second argument. If the condition  is true, then
the assert macro does nothing and the code continues to execute. If the
condition is false, then the assert macro throws an AssertionError , which
can be caught and handled using a try/catch block. Here are some examples
of how you can use the assert macro in Julia:
# Check that a variable has a certain value
x = 10
assert x == 10, “x should be 10”
# Check that a function returns a certain value
function test_function()
return 10
end
assert test_function() == 10, “test_function should return 10”
# Check that an expression evaluates to true
assert 1 + 1 == 2, “1 + 1 should equal 2”
# Check that a value is within a certain range
x = 10
assert x >= 0 && x <= 10, “x should be between 0 and 10”
If the condition being checked by the assert macro is true, then nothing will
happen and the code will contin ue to execute. If the condition is false, then
an AssertionError will be thrown and the code will stop executing.
You can also use the @assert  macro to includ e the expression being checked
in the error message if the condition is not met. For example:
# Check that a variable has a certain value
x = 10
@assert x == 10 “x should be 10, but it is $(x)”
This will include the value of x in the error message if the condition is not
met, which can be helpful for debugging. The assert macro is often used in
conjunction with a try/catch block to handle errors and preven t them from
crashing the program. For example,
try
# Code that might throw an error goes here
x = y # y is not defined, so this will throw an error
assert x == 10, “x should be 10”
catch e
# Code to handle the error goes here
println(“An error occurred: $(e)”)
end
In this example, the code in the try block will be executed, and if an error
occurs it will be caught by the catch block, which will print an error message
to the console.
The macroexpand  function in Julia is used to expand the macro into its
underlying code. Macros in Julia are used to write code that generates other
code at compile time, which  can be helpful for tasks such as code
generation, code optimization, and metaprogramming.
To use the macroexpand  function , you pass it a macro as an argument and it
will return the expanded code as an Expr object. For example,
# Define a simple macro that adds 1 to a number
macro add_one(x)
return :($x + 1)
end
# Expand the macro
expanded_code = macroexpand(:(@add_one 10))
# The expanded code is now an expression that can be evaluated
eval(expanded_code) # returns 11
In this example,  the add_one  macro takes a single argument x and returns an
expression that adds 1 to x. When the macro is expanded using
macroexpand , it returns the expanded code as an expression that can be
evaluated using eval.
You can also use the @macroexpand  macro, which will print the expanded
code to the console instead of returning it as an Expr object.
# Define a simple macro that adds 1 to a number
macro add_one(x)
return :($x + 1)
end
# Expand the macro
@macroexpand @add_one 10
# Output: quote
# 10 + 1
# end
The macroexpand  function can be helpful for debugging macros and
understanding how they work. It can also be used to optim ize code by
expanding macr os at compile time to avoid the overhead of macro expansion
at runtime.
Conclusion
Julia is a powerful programming  language that offers a variety of features for
managing code structure and complexity . Functions are a key part of Julia’ s
design, and the language provi des a number of features for deﬁning and
calling functions, including optional and keyword arguments,  anonymous
functions, and currying. The Julia type system is also a key feature of the
language, allowing you to deﬁne and work with composite types, immutable
values, and mutable values. Julia’ s support for multiple dispatch allows you
to deﬁne  functions that behave differently depending on the types of their
arguments, making it easier to write ﬂexib le and reusable code. Managing
code structure in Julia is made easier by the use of modules and packages,
which allow you to organize your code into logical units and share it with
others. Julia’s macro system allows you to extend the language with custom
syntax and transform code at compile time, making it easier to write concise
and expressive code. Overall, Julia’ s functions, type system, multiple
dispatch, and support for code organization and macros make it a powerful
and ﬂexible language for a wide range of programming tasks.
In the next chapter , we will delve deeper into Julia’ s key featu res and data
structures. We will learn about arrays, mapping values to keys, and tuples.
We will also explore broadcastin g and module subtyping in Julia. Finally , we
will learn how to integrate Julia  with Python and R and use these languages
with Julia packages.

with composite types, immutable values, and mutable values?
