Mixture models are a powerful and ﬂexible class of probabilistic models that can
be used to model complex data distributions. Distributions.jl  provid es a
convenient and ﬂexible framework for working with mixture models in Julia.
Mixture models and convolutions in Distributions.jl
Mixture models and convolutions are both used to construct complex probability
distributions from simpler ones in Distributions.jl, but they differ in their
underlying mathematical operations.
A mixture model is a weighted sum (prior distribution) of two or more probability
distributions. The weights of each distribution represent the proportion of the
mixture that each distribution contributes. For example, a mixture of two normal
distributions can be created as follows:
Figure 7.27:  Using two varying Normal distributions and adding them up in a mixtur e model
In this example, a mixture is a probability distribution that is a weighted sum of
the two normal distributions, d1 and d2. Each of the normal distributions
contributes equally to the mixture, as indicated by the weights [0.5, 0.5].
On the other hand, convolution is a way of combining probability distributions to
represent the distribution of the sum of random variables. For example, if we have
two independen t normal distributions with mean 0 and standard  deviation 1, their
sum will also be a normal distribution with mean 0 and standard deviation
sqrt(2) . This can be computed using the convolution of the two normal
distributions:
Figure 7.28:  Adding up two varying normal distributions
In this example, convolution is a probability distribution that represents the
distribution of the sum of two independent normal distributions . The convolve()
function is used to compute the convolution of the two distributions.
In summ ary, mixture models are used to combine distributions into a weighted
sum (prior distribution), while convolutions are used to combine distributions to
represent the distribution of the sum of random variables.
Fitting a probability distribution
In statistics, ﬁtting a distribution to a dataset means ﬁnding the parameters of a
probability distribution that best ﬁt the observed data. The Julia programming
language provides a package called Distributions, which offers a wide range of
statistical distributions and methods for ﬁtting them to data.
To ﬁt a distribution, one can use the fit function , which  takes a distribution type
and a sample dataset as input and returns a ﬁtted distribution. The most commonly
used method for ﬁtting a distrib ution is maximum likelihood estimation (MLE),
which is the default method used by the fit function. Here is an example of
ﬁtting a normal distribution to a set of random numbers, that is, data:
# Generate a set of random numbers from a normal distribution
julia> data = randn(100)
Figure 7.29:  Fitting a normal distribution to the data
If the data is weighted, one can use the fit_mle  function instead, which takes an
additional input for the weights.
# Generate a set of random numbers from a normal distribution
julia> data = randn(100)
# Create a set of weights for the data
julia> weights = ones(100)
Figure 7.30:  Fitting a normal weighted data matrix
For some distributions, the estimation can be based on sufﬁcient statistics
computed from the dataset. In such cases, one can use the suffstats method to
compute sufﬁcient statistics and then use them to ﬁt the distribution using
fit_mle .
# Generate a set of random numbers from an exponential distribution
julia> data = rand(Exponential(2), 100)
Figure 7.31:  Sufﬁcient statistics for the exponential distribution
The Distributions.jl package provides a powerful set of tools for ﬁtting statistical
distributions to data, which can be useful in a wide range of applications.
Conclusion
In this chapter , we have provided an introduction to probability and its importance
in various ﬁelds, such as statistics and machine learning. We have also introduced
the probability package in Julia, which is a powerful tool for working with
probability distributions.
One of the key packages in the Julia probability ecosystem is the Distributions.jl
package, which provides a comprehensive collection of univariate and
multivariate probability distributions, as well as various methods for working with
them. We have covered the common functions available in Distributions.jl,
including sampling, PDF and CDF evaluation, and moments calculation.
We have also discussed three important types of probability distributions -
censored, trunca ted, and mixture models - and how they can be implemented in
Distributions.jl. We have highlighted the importance of mixture models in
modeling complex data distributions and how Distributions.jl provides a
convenient framework for working with them.
Finally , we have brieﬂy covered the topic of ﬁtting a probability distribution to
data, which is an important task in many statistical application s. We have shown
how Distributions.jl provides various methods for ﬁtting probability distributions
to data, including maximum likelihood estimation and Bayesian methods.
In the next chapter , we will learn about DataFrames and handling data in Julia.

CHAPTER  8
Framing Data in Julia
Framing data in Julia refers to the process of portraying data in a structured and
organized way that is suitable for data analysis and manipulation. In Julia, data is
typically framed as a table or a matrix, with each row representi ng an observation
or data point and each column representing a variable or feature.
DataFrames.jl  is a highly capable and efﬁcient package that provides a range  of
features to handle tabular data. It allows users to create, modify , and investigate
structured data in a way that is easy to understand and use. The package provides
a simila r interfa ce to that of R’s data.frame and Python’ s pandas library , which
makes it easy for users to migrate from these languages to Julia. This familiar
interface lets users perform a wide range of data manipulation tasks, including
data cleaning, ﬁltering, sorting, grouping, merging, and more. Users can apply the
package to create DataFrames from various sources, such as CSV  ﬁles, Excel
spreadsheets, SQL  databases, and more.
DataFrames.jl  is desig ned to work with large datasets, making it an essential
component of Julia’ s data analysis toolkit. It offers fast and memory-efﬁcient
operations, enab ling users to work with big data in a timely manner . The package
also provides several convenie nt functions for data import and export, which
makes it easy for users to transfer data from other sources into Julia.
The package also provides a range of functions for cleaning and transforming
data, such as imputing missing values, scaling and normalizing data, and encoding
categorical varia bles. User can save time and effort by using these functions to
make it easier for them to get their data ready for analysis, saving time and ef fort.
In addition to these features, DataFrames.jl  provides a rich set of functions for
data exploration and visualizatio n. Users can summarize their data using various
statistics and visualizatio ns, such as histograms, scatterplots, and bar charts, to
gain insights and explore relationships between variables.
Structure
In this chapter , the following topics will be covered:
Introducing DataFrames.jl package
Setting up the environment
Constructing DataFrame objects
Fetching, inspecting and manipulating data
Major data manipulation frameworks in Julia
Comparing with other data manipulation frameworks
Introducing DataFrames.jl package
DataFrames.jl is a powerful tool for working with tabular data in Julia, providing
similar functionality and design to other popular data science tools like pandas in
Python, and data.frame, data.table  and dplyr  in R. It is an essential
component of the Julia data ecosystem, with tight integrations with a range of
different librarie s. This chapter aims to provide everything needed to get up and
run with DataFrames.jl , and to showca se its functionality in conjunction with
other popular packages like GLM.jl, StatsModels.jl , and Plots.jl . Additionally ,
users will ﬁnd it effective to explore some of the other popular libraries in the
Julia data ecosystem, like for MLJ.jl  machine learning, HypothesisT ests.jl  for
hypothesis testing, and Plots.jl  for plotting.
Setting up the environment
Setting up the environment refers to the process of installing and conﬁguring the
necessary software and package s required to work with a particular programming
language, package, or library . In this case, we are setting up the environment for
working with the DataFrames.j l package, which is a package for working with
tabular data in Julia.
To use the DataFrames.jl packag e in Julia, you ﬁrst need to install it. This can be
done using the Pkg module in Julia, which provides a package manager for Julia.
To begin, you need to load the Pkg module by typing using Pkg in the Julia
console.
Once the Pkg module is loaded, you can install the DataFrames.jl package by
running Pkg.add(“DataFrames”)  in the Julia console. This command will
download and install the packag e and its dependencies, and make it available for
use in your Julia environment.
Alternatively , you can use the package manager REPL  mode to add the package
by typing ] in the Julia console. This will switch to the package manager REPL,
and you can type add DataFrames  to add the package.
After installing the DataFrames  package, it is recommended to run its tests to
ensure that everything is working as expected. You can do this by typing
Pkg.test(“DataFrames”)  in the Julia console. Note that running the tests may
take more than 30 minutes, depending on your system’ s performance.
To check the version of DataFrames.jl that you have installed, you can use the
status command in the package manager REPL  mode. Type ] in the Julia console
to switch  to the package manage r REPL, and then type status DataFrames to see
the version of the package.
Once you have installed and veriﬁed the package, you can load it into your Julia
environment using the using DataFrames command. This will make the
DataFrame type available, which is the most fundamental type provided by
DataFrames.jl. In a DataFrame, each row represents an observation, and each
column represents a feature. You can then use the various functions and
operations provi ded by the DataFrames package to manipulate  and analyze your
data.
Constructing DataFrame objects
In Julia, constructors are functions that create an instance of a type. The
DataFrame is a type provided by the DataFrames.jl package that represents
tabular data.
Creating a new DataFrame instance with no data is done  using an empty
constructor . For example, DataFrame()  creates an empt y DataFrame with no rows
or columns.
The most common way to creat e a DataFrame is by specifying the column names
and data. This can be done using a keyword argument constructor (Figur e 8.1).
For example,
julia> DataFrame(A=20:30, B=40:50, fixed=1)
Figure 8.1:  Creating a DataFrame with thr ee columns named “A”, “B”, and “ﬁxed” with their speciﬁed
values.
The “A” column contains the values 20, 21, …, and 30. The “B” column contains
the values 40, 41, 42, …., and 50. The “ﬁxed” column contains the scalar value 1,
which gets broadcasted to all rows. Both columns A and B should be of the same
dimensions.
If your column names contain spaces or are not valid Julia identiﬁers, you can use
the => syntax to associate the names with their values as shown in the following
example:
julia> DataFrame(“age” => [45, 40, 15], “first name” => [“Rohan”,
“Raghav”, “Avyukta”])
Figure 8.2:  Creating a DataFrame with two columns named “age” and “ﬁrst name”
DataFrames can also be created from dictionaries or NamedT uples. If the keys are
strings or symbols, you can pass the dictionary or NamedT uple directly to the
DataFrame constructor . For example,
julia> DataFrame(Dict(:age => [45, 40, 15], :first_name => [“Rohan”,
“Raghav”, “Avyukta”]))
Figure 8.3:  It cr eates the same DataFrame as the pr evious example
If the column names are not provided, but the data is in a matrix or an array of
NamedT uples, you can pass :auto as the second argument to automatically
generate column names. For example,
julia> DataFrame([7 1; 9 4], :auto)
Figure 8.4:  Creating a DataFrame with two columns named “x1” and “x2”
Finally , if you have a vector of column names, you can pass it as the second
argument to the DataFrame constructor . For example,
julia> DataFrame([11 23 7 0; 85 50 65 21; 63 11 16 49], [“a”, “b”,
“c”, “d”])
Figure 8.5:  Creating a DataFrame with four columns named “a”, “b”, “c”, and “d”
Fetching, inspecting and manipulating data
In Chapter 5, Working with datasets in Julia , we learnt about fetching and
inspecting data using various other methods, including CSV  reader objects. We
used the CSV.jl package to read and inspect our data. DataFrames.jl  is a
powerful packag e for working with tabular data in Julia, and it provides various
functions and tools for fetching, unpacking, and inspecting data.
CSV  is a Julia package that provides functionality for reading and writing tabular
data from CSV  and other delimited text ﬁles. The package is available for
download from the Julia package manager and can be installed using the
Pkg.add()  command.
Once installed, the package must be imported into the session  using the using
command. This makes the packa ge’s functions available to use in your code. With
Julia 1.9, one can skip Pkg.add  for registered packages.
Here’ s an example of how to read a dataset from a CSV  ﬁle:
julia> using CSV, DataFrames
julia> df = DataFrame(CSV.File(“path/to/input.csv”))
This code impo rts the CSV  and DataFrames packages and then reads a dataset
from a CSV  ﬁle located at “path /to/input.csv”. The resulting dataset is stored in a
DataFrame object called “df”.
Similarly , you can write a Data Frame object to a CSV  ﬁle using the following
code:
julia> CSV.write(“path/to/output.csv”, df)
This code writes the contents of the DataFrame object df to a ﬁle locate d at
path/to/output.cs3v .
You can also customize the behavior of the CSV  functions by using keyword
arguments. The available keywo rd arguments and their meanings can be found in
the Julia documentation.
In some  cases, the compilation latency of the CSV.jl package  can be an issue. In
such cases, the DelimitedFiles  module from the Julia standar d library can be
used instead. Here’ s an example of how to read data from a CSV  ﬁle using the
DelimitedFiles module:
julia> using DelimitedFiles, DataFrames
julia> data, header = readdlm(“path/to/input.csv”, ‘,’, header=true)
julia> df = DataFrame(data, vec(header))
This code imports the DelimitedFiles  and DataFrame s packages and reads the
contents of a CSV ﬁle located at “path/to/input.csv”. The resulting dataset is
stored in a DataFrame object called “df”.
It’s worth noting that the read and write functions provided by DelimitedFiles.jl
are more limited in functionality compared to CSV.jl, and cannot handle more
advanced use cases such as handling missing or non-numeric data. Therefore, it’s
recommended to use CSV .jl in most cases.
Finally , if you need more information on the CSV.jl functions, you can use the “?”
operator followed by the function name to access the documentation. For
example, typing  “?CSV .File” in the Julia REPL  will display the documentation for
the CSV.File function. Altern atively , you can check out the online CSV.jl
documentation for more information.
To fetch data using DataFrames.jl , users can use the CSV.read  function to read
data from a CSV ﬁle. The package also provides support for various other ﬁle
formats, including Excel spreadsheets and JSON ﬁles, using the appropriate
functions.
Fetching data
CSV  is a ﬁle format for tabular data, where each row is a record, and each column
represents a variable or feature. To start, we need to make sure that we have the
CSV.jl package installed in our Julia environment. We can do this by executing
the following commands:
julia> using Pkg
julia> Pkg.add(“CSV”)
To read in a CSV ﬁle using Julia’ s CSV.jl  package , ﬁrst make sure it’s installed.
Then, you can use the CSV.read  function to read the ﬁle. For example, if your
data is stored in a ﬁle called “iris.csv ” in the asset s directory of the
DataFrames.jl  repository , you can obtain the path of the package ﬁle, navigate  to
the assets directory using the joinpath()  function, and then use CSV.read()  to
read in the data.
julia> using CSV, DataFrames
# Obtain the path of the DataFrames.jl package file
julia> path_to_pkg = dirname(pathof(DataFrames))
# Navigate to the assets directory and the iris.csv file
julia> csv_file_path = joinpath(path_to_pkg, “..”, “docs”, “src”,
“assets”, “iris.csv”)
# Read in the CSV file and store the resulting data in a DataFrame
object
julia> df = CSV.read(csv_file_path, DataFrame)
Once the data is loaded, it’s a good idea to create a copy using the copy function
to avoid accidentally modifying the original data.
# Create a copy of the DataFrame object to avoid accidental
modification
julia> df_copy = copy(df)
Note:  Julia has different string types, including String7  and String15 , which
have a ﬁxed width that can make them faster and more efﬁcient  than the standard
String type. You need to add InlineStrings.jl  to your code base.
julia> # Define a fixed-width string
str7 = String7(“hello”)
“hello  “
julia> str15 = String15(“hello”)
“hello       “
julia> # Print the length of each string
println(length(str))   # 11
11
julia> println(length(str7))  # 7
7
julia> println(length(str15)) # 15
15
Figure 8.6:  Read and stor e the r eference of the iris dataset.
Keep in mind that your DataFrame object may be too wide or too tall to display in
its entirety , causing it to be cropped in the output. To view the entire DataFrame,
you need to change your environment variables such as,
# Set the option to show all columns and rows
julia> ENV[“COLUMNS”] = 1000
julia> ENV[“LINES”] = 1000
# Print the DataFrame
julia> println(df)
Inspecting data
Once the data has been fetched, DataFrames.jl  provides several functions for
unpacking and inspecting it. First, the size function is used to get the dimensions
of the DataFrame. The size function returns a tuple containing the number of rows
and columns in the DataFrame. For example, size(df)  returns (150, 5), which
means that the Iris DataFrame has 150 rows and 5 columns. We can also use the
size function with the optional argument 1 or 2 to retrieve the number of rows or
columns, respec tively . For instance, size(df, 1) returns 150, which is the
number of rows, and size(df, 2)  returns 5, which is the number of columns.
julia> size(df)
(150, 5)
julia> size(df, 1)
150
julia> size(df, 2)
5
The first()  and last()  function s can be used to displ ay the ﬁrst and last few
rows of the DataFrame.
julia> first(df)
DataFrameRow
Row │ SepalLength  SepalWidth  PetalLength  PetalWidth  Species
│ Float64   Float64  Float64   Float64  String15
─────┼─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
──
1 │      5.1      3.5       1.4      0.2  Iris-setosa
julia> last(df)
DataFrameRow
Row │ SepalLength  SepalWidth  PetalLength  PetalWidth  Species
│ Float64   Float64  Float64   Float64  String15
─────┼─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
─────
150 │      5.9      3.0       5.1      1.8  Iris-virginica
julia> first(df, 3)
3×5 DataFrame
Row │ SepalLength  SepalWidth  PetalLength  PetalWidth  Species
│ Float64   Float64  Float64   Float64  String15
─────┼─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
──
1 │      5.1      3.5       1.4      0.2  Iris-setosa
2 │      4.9      3.0       1.4      0.2  Iris-setosa
3 │      4.7      3.2       1.3      0.2  Iris-setosa
julia> last(df, 4)
4×5 DataFrame
Row │ SepalLength  SepalWidth  PetalLength  PetalWidth  Species
│ Float64   Float64  Float64   Float64  String15

─────
1 │      6.3      2.5       5.0      1.9  Iris-virginica
2 │      6.5      3.0       5.2      2.0  Iris-virginica
3 │      6.2      3.4       5.4      2.3  Iris-virginica
4 │      5.9      3.0       5.1      1.8  Iris-virginica
We can also use the nrow()  and ncol()  functions to get the number of rows and
columns in the DataFrame, respectively . For example, nrow(df)  returns  150,
which is the number of rows, and ncol(df) returns 5, which is the number of
columns.
julia> nrow(df)
150
julia> ncol(df)
5
To get basic statistics of the data in the DataFrame , we can use the describe()
function. The describe function displays the mean, minimum, median, maximum,
number of missing values, and element type of each numeric al column in the
DataFrame . By default, the function skips the missing values when computing the
summary statist ics. We can customize the shown statistics by passing optional
arguments to the describe function. For example, describe(df, cols=1:3)
returns a DataFrame  that only displays statistics for the ﬁrst three columns.
Figure 8.7:  describe() function is depicting the information about the iris dataset
Lastly , we can adjust how the DataFrame is displayed by manually calling the
show function. We can use the allrows  and allcols  optional arguments to show
all rows and all columns of the DataFrame, respectively . For instance, show(df,
allcols=true)  prints all the columns of the DataFrame, even if they do not ﬁt on
the screen.
julia> show(df)
150×5 DataFrame
julia> show(df, allrows=true)
150×5 DataFrame
In Julia, a DataF rame can be very large and can take up a lot of memory . To make
the DataFrame more memory efﬁcient, one can create a view of it. Creating a
view of a DataFrame is similar to creating a materialized selec tion, but a view is
more memory efﬁcient because  it only stores pointers to the data in the original
DataFrame.
In the code sample, the view function and @view macro can be used to create
views of a Data Frame. The view function is used to create a view by specifying
the DataFrame to be viewed and the row and column indices that will be included
in the view . @view  macro works in the same way, but it allows for more concise
and readable code by using a slicing syntax.
The syntax for accessing elements in a view is the same as for accessing elements
in a DataFrame. The only difference is that a view does not create a new object,
but rather provides a view into the existing DataFrame.
Figure 8.8:  Viewing only a certain part of the DataFrame
A view is more memory efﬁcient than creating a new DataFrame  or array becaus e
it only stores pointers to the data in the original DataFrame . The benchmark
comparison shows that creating a view is an order of magnitude faster and
allocates much less memory than creating a new DataFrame  or array .
Views can be useful when we want to manipulate a subset of a DataFrame  without
creating a copy . For example, we might want to multiply all the values in the
PetalWidth  column by 2:
julia> view1 = view(df, :, :PetalWidth)
150-element view(::Vector{Float64}, :) with eltype Float64:
0.2
0.2
…
julia> view1 .= 2 .* view1
150-element view(::Vector{Float64}, :) with eltype Float64:
0.4
0.4
…
Here, view1 is a view of the PetalWidth  column of df. The second line multiplies
every element of view1 by 2, which has the effect of modifying the original df
DataFrame .
We can also append a row to the DataFrame  using the view()  function.
Figure 8.9:  Appending the new r ow to the dataset using the view() function
However , a view points to the same memory as its parent, so changing a view will
change the parent, which can sometimes be undesirable. Additionally , some
operations migh t be a bit slower as DataFrames.jl  needs to perform  a mapping of
indices of a view to indices of the parent.
In this chapter , we will learn about a few other ways to fetch and inspect the data
at hand using DataFrames.jl . Data frames can be indexed using a syntax that
allows for a ﬂexible selection of rows and columns. By understanding the
available option s for indexing, you can extract the relevant data for your analysis.
A DataFrame can be indexed using the same syntax as matrices. The general
syntax for indexing is data_frame[selected_rows, selected_columns] , where
selected_rows  and selected_columns  are vectors or ranges of row or column
indices to select. Unlike matrices, which allow the selection of either rows or
columns, DataFrames always require both row and column selectors.
A colon : can be used as a wildcard to indicate that all rows or columns should be
retained. For example, df[1:5, [:SepalWidth, :PetalWidth]]  returns the ﬁrst
ﬁve rows of the “iris” DataFrame, with only the “SepalWidth ” and “PetalWidth ”
columns included.
Figure 8.10:  View only sepal and petal width
Similarly , df[1:5, :] returns the ﬁrst ﬁve rows of all columns in the “iris”
DataFrame .
Figure 8.1 1: View all the columns of the dataset but only fr om ﬁrst to ﬁfth r ows
A vector  of speciﬁc row indices can also be used to index the DataFrame, as in
df[[1, 6, 15], :], which returns only the ﬁrst, sixth, and ﬁfteenth rows of all
columns.
Figure 8.12:  View all the columns of the dataset but only of 1st, 6th and 15th row
To select speciﬁc columns, the [:, selected_c olumns]  syntax can be used. For
example, df[:, [:SepalLength, :PetalLength]]  returns all rows of only the
“SepalLength” and “PetalLength” columns.
Figure 8.13:  view only sepal and petal length
Note:  The syntax used for column selection can affect the type of the returned
object. df[:, [:PetalLength]]  and df[!, [:PetalLength]]  both return a
DataFrame with a single column, but df[:, :PetalLength]  and df[!,
:PetalLength]  return a vector of strings with only the “PetalLength ” column
data. The difference between the two syntaxes lies in the use of a single symbol
:PetalLength  versus a vector [:PetalLength] . When using the latter , a vector is
required to be passed (not just any iterable), so df[:, (:SepalLength,
:PetalLength)]  is not allowed, but df[:, [:SepalLength, :PetalLength]]  is
valid.
To perform basic operations on a DataFrame , such as extracting columns, one can
use different syntaxes. The simplest way to extract a column is to use the dot
notation: dataframe.column_name . This notation allows us to directly access the
column data without making a copy . Therefore, changes made to the extracted
column will affect the original DataFrame . For example, df.PetalWidth  returns a
vector of the petal width of various ﬂowers and changing the value of this vector
will also af fect the values stored in the iris DataFrame :
julia> df.PetalWidth
150-element Vector{Float64}:
julia> df.”PetalWidth”
150-element Vector{Float64}:
However , somet imes it is desirable to make a copy of a column  to avoid affecting
the original DataFrame. In such cases, one can use the indexing notation to extract
a colum n: dataframe[:, column_name] or dataframe[!, column_n ame] ,
where column_name  is a string or a symbol represen ting the name of the column.
dataframe[:, column_name]  will return a copy of the colum n and modifying it
will not affect the original DataFrame while dataframe[!, column_name]  returns
the original DataFrame .
julia> df[!, :PetalWidth]
julia> df[!, “PetalWidth”]
We can check that both expressions produce the same objec t and conﬁrm the
behavior described above using the  ===  function:
julia> df.PetalWidth === df[!, :PetalWidth]
true
julia> df.PetalWidth === df[:, :PetalWidth]
false
To get a vecto r of all colum n names in the DataFrame, one can use the
names(dataframe)  function. The function returns a vector of strings representing
the column names.
julia> names(df)
Figure 8.14:  Names of the columns of the iris datasets
One can also use this function to ﬁlter columns by their element type or other
criteria. For example, names(dataframe, AbstractString)  return a vector of
column names with elements of type  AbstractString .
julia> names(df, AbstractString)
1-element Vector{String}:
“Species”
If one needs to work with column names as symbols, the
propertynames(dataframe)  function can be used instead. It returns a vector of
symbols representing the column names.
julia> propertynames(df)
5-element Vector{Symbol}:
:SepalLength
:SepalWidth
:PetalLength
:PetalWidth
:Species
To get the data type of each column, one can use the eltype  functio n on an
iterator generated by the eachcol(dataframe)  function. The eachcol function
returns an iterat or over the columns of the DataFrame, and the eltype  function
returns the element type of a collection.
julia> eltype.(eachcol(df))
5-element Vector{DataType}:
Float64
Float64
Float64
Float64
String15
Finally , the empty(dataframe)  function returns an empty DataFrame with the
same column names and types as the original DataFrame . Howev er, it does not
modify the origi nal DataFrame . If one needs to empty the origi nal DataFrame , the
empty!(dataframe)  function should be used instead.
julia> empty(ref)
0×5 DataFrame
Row │ SepalLength  SepalWidth  PetalLength  PetalWidth  Species
│ Float64   Float64  Float64   Float64  String15
─────┴────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
julia> empty!(ref)
0×5 DataFrame
Row │ SepalLength  SepalWidth  PetalLength  PetalWidth  Species
│ Float64   Float64  Float64   Float64  String15
─────┴────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
It is important to note that colum n names in DataFrames.jl  can be either symbols
or string s. Whil e using symbols is slightly faster , using string s is simpler when
non-standard characters are present in the column names or one wants to
manipulate them. For non-standard symbols, one can use Symbol(“asd fgh”).
In Julia , one can perform broadcasting assignments using the .= operation.
Broadcasting is a powerful feature that allows performing operations element-
wise over an array . This is particularly useful when we want to apply a scalar or
vector operation on a column or a slice of a DataFrame.
The standard syntax to perform broadcasting in Julia is to use ‘.’ . For example, to
assign a scalar value to a slice of a vector , we need to use the .= operation. This is
because assigning a scalar value to multiple locations is not supported. For
instance, the following code would fail in Julia:
Figure 8.15:  A single value cannot be assigned to the multiple elements
Instead, we need to write:
julia> s[2:3] .= 0
2-element view(::Vector{Int64}, 2:3) with eltype Int64:
0
0
This will assign 0 to the elements in the slice [2,3] of the vector s. The
broadcasting syntax is fully supported in DataFrames.jl as well.
Here is an example using DataFrames.jl:
julia> df[3:8, :PetalLength] .= [8.5, 8.9, 7.8, 5.8, 9.6, 6.8] #
Broadcasting assignment
6-element view(::Vector{Float64}, 3:8) with eltype Float64:
8.5
8.9
7.8
5.8
9.6
6.8
This code will create a new column :PetalLength  in the DataFrame  df and assign
the values in the vector [8.5, 8.9, 7.8, 5.8, 9.6, 6.8] to it.
Note:  The ! operator in the broadcas ting syntax performs an in-place operation
while using : instead of, ! performs a replace operation. An in-place operation
replaces the old values with new ones in the existing vector , while the replace
operation alloca tes a fresh vector and replaces the old vector with it. The
difference is important when the new values have a different type than the old
ones. In the case of the replace operation, the new vector will have a new type.
For exam ple, the following code will perform an in-place replacement of values
stored in column number 3 by 4:
julia> df[:, 3] .= 4
150-element view(::Vector{Float64}, :) with eltype Float64:
The following code will replace the :PetalLength column with a new vector of
type String:
julia> df[!, :PetalLength] .= “Economics”
150-element Vector{String}:
It is worth noting that using broadcasting on a row selector will not work in-place,
and we need to use the ! operator instead for allocating a fresh vector .
Column selectio n is a fundamental operation when working with DataFrames .
Julia provides a set of ﬂexible and powerful column selection functions that allow
us to select one or more columns from a DataFrame  based on dif ferent criteria.
This response will explain four of the most useful column selectors for complex
scenarios. The Not, Between, Cols, and All selectors are used to exclude columns,
select a range of columns, select multiple columns, and select all columns,
respectively . Additionally , we will see an example of using a regular expression to
select columns based on their names.
The Not selector from the InvertedIndices.jl  packag e allows us to specify the
columns we want to exclude from the resulting DataFrame . We can put any other
valid column selector inside Not. If we want to drop the PetalLength  column
from the iris DataFrame , we can use:
Figure 8.16:  A part of the dataset can only be viewed and not some speciﬁc column or r ow of the dataset
using Not()
The Between selector allows us to specify a range of columns by passing the start
and stop columns using any of the single-column selector syntaxes. For instance,
to select columns starting from :SepalLength  and ending at :PetalWidth , we can
use:
Figure 8.17:  A part of the dataset can only be viewed and not some speciﬁc column or r ow of the dataset
using Between()
The Cols(…)  selector  picks a union of other selectors passed as its argumen ts. In
the following example, the Cols selector is picking a union of Age and
Between(“SepalWidth”, “PetalWidth”)  selectors passed as its ar guments:
Figure 8.18:  A part of the dataset can only be viewed and not some speciﬁc column of the dataset using
Cols()
Finally , the All()  selector  allows us to select all columns of a DataFrame, which
is the same as passing. For example, to select all columns of the df DataFrame,
we can use:

combine

columns that contain the transf ormed data, which can potentially combine the
rows of the original DataFrame . Here’ s an example:

Major data manipulation frameworks in Julia
Data manipulation frameworks are software tools that provide convenient
methods for working with tabular data, such as DataFrames, tables, or
spreadsheets. In Julia, three such frameworks are available for manipulating
DataFrames: DataFramesMeta.jl, DataFrameMacr os.jl,  and Query .jl. These
frameworks enable users to write code for data manipulation in a way that is
similar to dplyr in R or LINQ in C#.
We will understand the functionality of the three of these frameworks,
DataFramesMeta.jl , DataFrameMacr os.jl and Query .jl, and provide code
samples to demonstrate how they work.
DataFramesMeta.jl
The DataFramesMeta.jl package  provides a convenient macro-b ased interface
to work with DataFrames. It provides a more convenient syntax for the
transformation functions: transform, select, and combine via the macros
@transform, @select , @combine , and more. DataFramesMeta.jl  also reexports
the @chain  macro from Chain.jl, allowin g users to pipe the output of one
transformation as an input to another , as with |> and %>% in R.
To use DataFramesMeta.jl , ﬁrst, we need to install the package using the
following command:
using DataFramesMeta
then press y followed by Enter .

DataFrameMacros.jl
DataFrameMacr os.jl is an alternativ e to DataFramesMeta.jl , with a focus on
convenient solutions for the transformation of multiple columns at once. It
provides a similar functionality  to DataFramesMeta.jl  but is more ﬂexible in
how transformations can be applied.
To use DataFrameMacr os.jl, ﬁrst, we need to install the package using the
following command:
using DataFramesMacros

Query.jl
Query .jl is a package that implements a LINQ-like interface for querying data. It
allows for easy ﬁltering, grouping, joining and aggregating of DataFrames. The
following instructions are for version 1.0.5 of Query .jl.
First, install the Query .jl package:
using Query
then press y followed by Enter .
Query .jl uses the @> macro to chain together queries in a readable and ﬂexible
way. We present a few examples of its usage:
Figure 8.28:  Creating a subset of the DataFrame and selecting some columns to be visible thr ough @fr om,

Query .jl provide s the @from  macro, which creates a query object that can be used
to perform operations on the data. Queries can be ﬁltered, sorted, aggregated, and
joined.
In this example, the @from  macro creates a query object with a reference to the
input DataFrame. The query object is then used to ﬁlter the data with the @where
macro and selec t the desired columns with the @select  macro. Finally , the `>`
operator applies the query and converts the output to a DataFrame.
In addition to the examples shown here, Query .jl provides many more useful
functions for data manipulation , such as join, order , and distinct. In summary ,
DataFramesMeta.jl , DataFrameMacr os.jl, and Query .jl are three powerful
frameworks for data manipulation in Julia. They provide convenient methods for
ﬁltering, selectin g, transforming , and aggregating data in a way that is familiar to
users of dplyr or LINQ. Each framework has its strengths and weaknesses, so it is
worth exploring all three to ﬁnd the best ﬁt for your use case.
Comparing with other language frameworks
We will compare the Julia data manipulation framework DataFrames.jl  with
similar tools in other programm ing languages like Python, R, and Stata. We will
use the example of creating a data set, and then we will compare various functions
available in DataFrames.jl  with their counterparts in the pandas package in
Python.
Comparing with pandas - a Python framework
Both Pandas and DataFrames.jl  are designed to manipulate tabular data, but they
have some differences in terms of syntax, structure, and time taken for certain
operations. Here are some key dif ferences:
Language:  Pandas is a Python library , while DataFrames.jl  is a package
for the Julia language.
Syntax : Both libraries  have similar syntax for many operations, such as
ﬁltering and grouping data. How ever, there are some differences in syntax,
especially for more complex operations. For example, in Pandas, you can
use the apply()  method  to apply a function to each row or column of a
DataFrame , while in DataFrames.jl, you would use the transform()
function for the same purpose.
Data structur es: Both Pandas and DataFrames.jl  use a DataFrame
structure to represent tabular data. However , there are some differences in
the details of the data structure s. For example, DataFrames.jl  allows for
more ﬂexible handling of missing data, while Pandas has more built-in
support for time series data.
Time taken:  In general, Julia is faster than Python, which means that
DataFrames.jl  can be faster than Pandas for some operations. For example,
sorting a DataFrame  can be much faster in Julia, especially for larger
datasets.
Overall, both Pandas and DataFrames.jl  are powerful tools for working with
tabular data, and the choice between the two will depend on factors such as the
speciﬁc require ments of the task at hand and the progra mming language
preferences of the user .
Comparing with dplyr - an R framework
dplyr is a data manipulation package in R, while DataFrames.jl  is a data
manipulation package in Julia. Both dplyr and DataFrames.jl  provide simila r
functionality for working with DataFrames, but there are some differences in their
syntax and structure.
Time taken : DataFrames.jl  is gener ally faster than dplyr . Julia is designed
to be a faster language than R, and DataFrames.jl  is optimized for
performance.
Syntax : The syntax of dplyr  and DataFrames.jl  is similar but not identical.
In dplyr , you chain together multiple operations using the %>% operator . In
DataFrames.jl , you typically use the |> operator to chain together multiple
operations. For example, to ﬁlter rows in dplyr , you would use the filter()
function, while in DataFrames.j l, you would use the filter()  function and
the |> operator .
Structur e: The structure of DataFrames in dplyr and DataFrames.jl is also
similar but not identical. In dplyr, DataFrames are represented as tibble
objects, which are similar to DataFrames in base R but with additional
functionality . In DataFrames.jl,  DataFrames are represented as DataFrame
objects, which are similar to tibble objects in dplyr . However , DataFrame
objects in DataFrames.jl are more ﬂexible than tibble objects in dplyr .
Overall, dplyr  and DataFrames.jl  provide similar functionality for working with
DataFrame s, but there are some differences in their syntax and structu re. The
choice of which package to use depends on the project-speciﬁc  needs as well as
individual preferences and familiarity with the programming languages.
Conclusion
The DataFrames.jl  package is a powerful tool for data manipulation in the Julia
programming language. By setting up the environment and constructing
DataFrame  objects, users can easily fetch, inspect, and manipulate data. The
package provides numerous functionalities for handling data, including ﬁltering,
merging, sorting, and aggregating data.
Moreover , DataFrames.jl  is just one of the major data manipulation frameworks
in Julia, which offers great performance and ease of use, compared to other
popular data manipulation frameworks like Pandas and R. It also has features that
make it stand out: its integration with the wider Julia ecosystem, support for
parallel processing, and ability to cope with lar ge datasets efﬁciently .
Overall, DataFrames.jl  package provides a great option for users looking for a
fast and ﬂexible data manipulati on tool, and its ease of use and performance make
it a viable alternative to other popular data manipulation frameworks in other
programming languages.
DataFrames.jl  provide s robust tools for working with missing and categori cal
data, including advanced imputation techniques. It also offers efﬁcient functions
for sorting, reshaping, joining, and performing split-apply-combine operations on
data, making it a comprehensiv e data manipulation package for Julia users. We
will learn about these in the next chapter .

Missing data in DataFrames
Missing data is a common problem in datasets and can lead to biased or
incomplete results if not handled appropriately . There are different
approaches to handle missing data, such as deleting missing values, imputing
missing values, or ignoring missing values.
Deleting missing values involv es removing rows or columns with missing
data, but it can lead to a loss of information, bias in the results, and
decreased statistical power . This method is only recommended when the
proportion of missing data is small and the missing data is randomly
distributed.
Imputing missing values involves replacing missing values with estimated
values based on statistical techniques. Single imputation methods include
mean, median, and mode imputation, while multiple imputation creates
multiple imputed datasets and provides more reliable estimates. However ,
single imputation methods can lead to biased results and underestimate the
variance of the data.
Ignoring missing values is acceptable in some cases, but it can also lead to
biased results, especially when the missing data is not random. The choice of
method depends  on the type of data, the proportion of missing values, and
the characteristi cs of the dataset. Handling missing data is an essential step
in data preprocessing and requires careful consideration and appropriate
techniques.
Missing values in Julia
In Julia,  missing values in data are represented using the object missing,
which is the single instance of the type Missing. The Missing type lets users
create vectors and DataFrame columns with missing values.
To create a vector with a missing value, we can use the following code:
julia> using DataFrames
julia> x = [1, 2, missing]
3-element Vector{Union{Missing, Int64}}:
1
2
missing
The element type of the returned vector is Union{Missing, Int64}. We can
exclude missing values when performing operations by using skipmissing,
which returns a memory-efﬁcient iterator . For example:
julia> sum(skipmissing(x))
3
The coalesce function can be used to replace missing values with another
value. For example:
julia> coalesce.(x, 0)
3-element Vector{Int64}:
1
2
0
The dropmissing  and dropmissing!  functio ns can be used to remove the
rows containing missing values from a DataFrame  and either create a new
DataFrame  or mutate the original in place respectively . Here is an example:
Figure 9.1:  Create a DataFrame and use the dr opmissing() function to r emove all the missing values
By default, the dropmissing and dropmissing! functions keep the Union{T,
Missing}  element type in columns selected for row removal. To remove the
Missing part, if present, set the disallowmissing  keyword ar gument to true.
Finally , it is possible to allow or disallow support of missing values in some
columns of a DataFrame using the allowmissing , allowmissing! ,
disallowmissing , and disallowmissing ! functions. For example:
Figure 9.2:  Create a DataFrame and allow missing values in the DataFrame
Now df allows missing values in all its columns. We can take advantage of
this fact and set some of the values in df to missing. Let’s take the example
DataFrame  df = DataFrame(x=1:3, y=4:6) which has no missing values
initially . To allow missing values in all columns, we use allowmissing!(df)
which modiﬁes the DataFrame in place. Now , we can set some values to
missing, for example, df[1, 1] = missing .
The Julia language provides functions to allow or disallow missing values in
a DataFrame . These function s are useful when dealing with data that may
have missing values in some columns, but not in others. The allowmissing ,
allowmissing! , disallowmissing , and disallowmissing!  functions can be
used to allow or disallow missing values in a DataFrame .
To disallow missing values in some columns, we can use the
disallowmissing  function. If we want to disallow missing values in column
:y, we can use disallowmissing(df, :y). This creates a new DataFrame
with the speciﬁed column(s) disallowed missing values. If we want to
modify the original DataFrame  in place, we use disallowmissing!(df,
:y).
Figure 9.3:  Disallowing() function doesn’ t allow missing values in particular columns
If we want to disallow missing  values in all columns that do not contain
them, we can use disallowmissing(df, error=false)  which keeps the
columns that have some missing values unchanged without having to list
them explicitly .
Figure 9.4:  passmissing() function allows the missing values in the DataFrame
The Missings.jl  package provides additional convenience functions for
working with missing values. passmissing is a higher -order function that
takes a function as an argument and returns a new function that returns
missing if any of its positional arguments are missing and applies the
function otherw ise. This is useful when working with functions that do not
support missing values as ar guments.

supporting missing values, using the optional ﬁrst argument to specify the
element type.

Reshaping data in DataFrames
Reshaping data in a DataFrame refers to the process of transforming the
structure of the DataFrame to make it easier to analyze and visualize. There
are several ways to reshape data in DataFrames, includi ng pivoting,
stacking, and melting.
Pivoting: Pivoti ng is the process of transforming a DataFrame from a
long format to a wide format. In a long format DataFrame, each row
represents a unique observation,  and each column represents a variable.
In a wide forma t DataFrame, each row represents a unique variable,
and each column represents an observation. To pivot a DataFrame, we
use the pivot()  method and specify the index, columns, and values we
want to use.
Stacking: Stacking is the process of transforming a DataFrame from a
wide format to a long format. To stack a DataFrame, we use the
stack()  method.
Melting: Melting is the process of transforming a DataFrame from a
wide format to a long format by unpivoting it. It is similar to stacking,
but it allows us to specify which columns we want to use as the
identiﬁers and which columns we want to melt. To melt a DataFrame,
we use the melt()  method and specify the id_vars , value_vars ,
var_name , and value_name  parameters.
Reshaping data in DataFrames can be useful for several reasons. For
example, it can help us to better understand the relationsh ips between
variables, compare different observations or groups, and perform various
types of analysis and visualization.
Reshaping data in Julia
The stack()  functio n in Julia’ s DataFrames package is used to convert a
wide-format DataFrame  into a long-form at DataFrame . This means that the
function can be used to transfo rm a DataFrame where columns represent
multiple variables to a DataFrame where those variables are stacked in a
single column. The stack()  function takes two arguments, the ﬁrst being the
DataFrame to be transformed, and the second is an optional argument that
speciﬁes the columns that are to be stacked. You can look into
https://csv .juliadata.or g/stable/reading.html#CSV .read  to better understand
“..” passed as a parameter .
Here is some code that demonstrates the use of the stack()  function in Julia:
julia> using DataFrames, CSV
# Load the german dataset
julia> german = CSV.read(joinpath(dirname(pathof(DataFrames)),
“..”, “docs”, “src”, “assets”, “german.csv”), DataFrame)
# Print the first few rows of the original german DataFrame
julia> @show german[1:5, :]

Figure 9.17:  Viewing the german dataset fr om the 1st r ow to the 5th r ow
# Convert the german DataFrame from wide format to long format
julia> long_german = stack(german, 1:4)
Figure 9.18:  Viewing the german dataset without the ﬁrst four columns
# Print the first few rows of the long_german DataFrame
julia> @show long_german[1:5, :]
Figure 9.19:  Viewing the german dataset fr om the 1st r ow to the 5th r ow
In this example, the CSV.read()  function is used to load the german dataset
from a CSV  ﬁle. The stack()  function is then used to transform the
DataFrame from  a wide format to a long format. The 1:4 argument speciﬁes
that the ﬁrst four columns of the DataFrame shou ld be stacked. The resulting
DataFrame, long_german , has eight columns: Housing, Saving accounts,
Checking account , Credit amount , Duration , Purpose , variable , and
value . The Housing , Saving accounts , Checking account , Credit
amount , Duration , and Purpose  columns contain the values that were
originally in the respective columns of the wide format DataFrame. The
variable column  contains the names of the columns that were stacked, which
in this case are id, Age, Sex, and Job. The value column contains the values
that were originally in the columns that were stacked.
The stack function is a useful tool for converting data from a wide format to
a long format. It is easy to use and can be applied to a variety of datasets
with multiple columns representing the same variable.
unstack is a function that conv erts a long-format DataFrame  into a wide-
format DataFrame . The long-format DataFrame  has a column for each
variable, and each row contains  values for these variables. In contrast, the
wide-format DataFrame  has a column  for each varia ble, and each row
represents a single observation.  The unstack function is used to convert a
long-format DataFrame  into a wide-format DataFrame  by pivoting the data
based on speciﬁed columns.
Figure 9.20:  Viewing the german dataset after unstacking the columns
In the given code sample, the unstack function is applied to a long-format
DataFrame  long_german . The function is called with three arguments, which
specify the columns that will be used for id variables, column variable
names, and column values, respectively . The ﬁrst argument is :id, which
speciﬁes that the id column should be used as the id variable.  The second
argument is :variable, which speciﬁes that the variable column should be
used as the column variable names. The third argument is :value , which
speciﬁes that the value column should be used as the column values.
The resulting DataFrame  will have the id column as the ﬁrst column and
columns for each unique value in the variable column. If a cell does not have
a value for a particular combina tion of id and variable, the cell will contain a
missing value.
Figure 9.21:  Viewing the german dataset after unstacking columns without mentioning id, value, or
variable
If the remaining columns are unique, we can skip the id variable, it will
produce the same results. This will pivot the long_german DataFrame  so
that each unique value in the variable column becomes a row, and the values
in the id and other remaining columns become the column names. The
resulting DataFr ame will have a row for each unique value in the variable
column and columns for each unique value in the id and other remaining
columns.
The stack functi on in Julia is used to stack columns of a DataFrame on top
of each other , creating a new DataFrame with a single column of values and
a column of “keys” indicating which original column each value came from.
By default, the resulting DataFr ame is a copy of the original data, meaning
that it takes up additional memory . However , passing view=true to stack
returns a DataFrame whose columns  are views into the original wide
DataFrame. This means that the new DataFrame takes up less memory since
it does not create a copy of the original data.
Categorical data in DataFrames
In data analysis,  we often encou nter DataFrames with columns that contain
only a small number of unique values. Instead of representing  each unique
value as a string in a vector , we can use a more memory- efﬁcient and
computationally efﬁcient representation by replacing the strings with indices
into a small pool of levels.
Julia provides two types of arrays for level pooling: PooledV ector from
PooledArrays.jl  and CategoricalV ector from CategoricalArrays.jl .
PooledV ector is designed solely for data compression purposes, while
CategoricalV ector provides full support for working with categorical
variables, both with unordered (nominal variables) and ordered categories
(ordinal variab les). CategoricalV ector only allows AbstractString ,
AbstractChar , or Number element types, optionally in a union with
Missing.
In CategoricalArrays.jl , we can create a CategoricalVector  using the
categorical function. We can pass in a vector of values, and it will create a
CategoricalVector  with the same values, but the values will be encoded as
indices into a pool of levels. The resulting CategoricalVector  will have
lower memory usage and higher performance than a plain vector of strings.
Here is an example of creating a CategoricalVector :
Figure 9.22:  categorizing data with Categorical Arrays
We can see that cv is a CategoricalArray  with the same values as v but
with a more efﬁcient encoding.
We can use the levels function to retrieve the pool of levels.
Figure 9.23:  The levels() function r eturns unique values in the categorical array
This returns an array of the unique values in cv .
Overall, using CategoricalVectors  instead  of plain vectors of strings can
lead to signiﬁcant memory savings and performance improvements,
especially when dealing with lar ge datasets.
A CategoricalVector  in Julia is a type of array that allows for efﬁcient
storage and manipulation of data that takes on a small number of levels.
CategoricalVectors  can also support missing values.
In the code sample provided, we create a CategoricalVector  cv from a
regular vector that includes some missing values. We can determine the
allowed levels in cv using the levels function. In this case, there are two
levels: “Group A” and “Group B”. We can change the order of appearance of
the levels using the levels!  function. We can also compress the
CategoricalVector  to use less memory by calling the compress function.
Figure 9.24:  The levels() function r eturns unique values in the categorical array
Additionally , we can create an ordered CategoricalVector  by setting the
ordered keyword argument to true in the categorical function. An ordered
CategoricalVector  allows for comparisons between levels using the <
operator . We can check if a CategoricalVector  is ordered using the
isordered functi on and change between ordered and unordered using the
ordered!  function.
Figure 9.25:  The or dered() function maintains the or der of the categorical arrays
Joining data in DataFrames
When working with data, it is often necessary to combine multiple data sets
to get a more complete picture. This can be done using joins, which combine
rows from different data sets based on a common key. In Julia,
DataFrames.jl  provides several functions for performing different types of
joins:
innerjoin: returns rows for values of the key that exist in all
DataFrames.
leftjoin: returns  rows for values of the key that exist in the ﬁrst
DataFrame, whether or not that value exists in the second DataFrame.
rightjoin: return s rows for values of the key that exist in the second
DataFrame, whether or not that value exists in the ﬁrst DataFrame.
outerjoin: return s rows for values of the key that exist in any of the
DataFrames.
semijoin: like an inner join, but the output is restricted to colum ns from
the ﬁrst DataFrame.
antijoin: returns  rows for values of the key that exist in the ﬁrst
DataFrame but not the second DataFrame. As with semijoin, the output
is restricted to columns from the ﬁrst DataFrame.
crossjoin: returns the cartesian product of rows from all DataFrames.
Here are some examples of using these functions with two data sets:
Figure 9.26:  creates two DataFrames named house and occupation

Figure 9.27:  inner , outer , left and right join on the mentioned DataFrames
Figure 9.28:  semi, anti and cr ossjoin on the mentioned DataFrames
In these examples, house and occupation are deﬁned as DataFrames, each
with a Name column. The on parameter is used to specify the key column
for the join. Cross joins are the only kind of join that does not use an on key
`:`.
When joining two DataFrames, it’s common to use a key column to match
up rows  from each table. How ever, sometimes the key column may have
different names in the left and right tables, which can make it difﬁcult to join
them.
To solve  this problem, you can pass pairs of left and right key column names
to the on argument. This syntax  uses a special arrow (=>) to associate each
left key column name with the corresponding right key column name.
Figure 9.29:  inner join varieties with separate column names but similar column values
However , when there are duplicate keys in the DataFrames, the join
operation can result in multiple  rows being created. To avoid this, you can
set the validate argument to true when performing the join. This will check
that the columns passed as the on argument deﬁne unique keys in each input
DataFrame.
Figure 9.30:  inner join varieties with separate column names but similar column values and keeping
single key rather than multiple duplicate keys
Finally , if you want to keep track of the source DataFrame  for each row in
the resulting DataFrame , you can use the source argument. This adds a
column to the resulting DataFrame  indicating whether the given  row
appeared only in the left, the right or both DataFrames.
When joining two DataFrames, it is often useful to keep track of the source
of each column. To do this, you can pass the renamecols keyword argument
to the join function.
The ﬁrst exampl e shows how to add a sufﬁx to the column names to indicate
their source. The sufﬁx is added using a pair of left => right pairs, where the
left side is the sufﬁx for columns from the left table and the right side is the
sufﬁx for columns from the right table.
Figure 9.31:  inner join varieties with separate column names with newer r enamed column names
This changes the ﬁrst column name to lowercase and the last column name
to uppercase.
Figure 9.32:  inner join varieties with separate column names with newer r enamed column names
When joining two DataFrames,  if there are duplicate values for the joining
columns in one or both DataFr ames, then the join operation may produce
unexpected results. Speciﬁcally , when using innerjoin , outerjoin ,
leftjoin  or rightjoin , all possible combinations of matching rows will be
produced.
To avoid such unexpected results, the validate keyword argument can be
used. This argument takes a two-element tuple or pair of Boolean values,
where each elem ent correspond s to one of the input DataFrames. If set to
true, it checks whether the joining columns in each DataFrame contain
unique keys (that is, no duplicates). If duplicates are found, an error is raised
and the join operation is not performed. As the joining columns in both input
DataFrames contain duplicates, an error is raised as it produces 4 rows
instead of 2 due to duplicates in City/London and Job/Lawyer .
Figure 9.33:  inner join varieties with separate column names with newer r enamed column names
We use the validate argument in an innerjoin operation. In this example, the
on argument is set to join two pairs of columns, and validate is set to (true ,
true) .
Figure 9.34:  inner join varieties with separate column names with newer r enamed column name
values and keeping single key rather than multiple duplicate keys
The source keyword argument can be used to add a column to the resulting
DataFrame indic ating the source of each row (whether it appears only on the
left, only on the right, or in both input DataFrames).
julia> a = DataFrame(ID=[20, 40], Name=[“John”, “Jane”])
julia> b = DataFrame(ID=[20, 60], Job=[“Lawyer”, “Doctor”])
julia> outerjoin(a, b, on=:ID, validate=(true, true),
source=:source)
The code produces a DataFrame with 3 rows, indicating that the row with
ID=20 appears in both input DataFrames and the rows with ID=40 and
ID=60 appear only in the left and right input DataFrames, respectively .
Figure 9.35:  inner join varieties with separate column names with newer r enamed column name
values and keeping single key rather than multiple duplicate keys with a deﬁned sour ce
When joining two DataFrames based on a common key, it is important to
handle missing values in a proper way. By default, if the key column in one
or both of the DataFrames contains missing values, you will get an error .
However , you can choose to treat missing values as equal or drop the
corresponding rows.
Let’s see how this works with some code examples. Suppose we have two
DataFrames with a common key column, but one of them contains missing
values:
julia> df1 = DataFrame(id=[1, missing, 3], a=1:3)
julia> df2 = DataFrame(id=[1, 2, missing], b=1:3)
If we try to perform an inner join on the key column, we get an error because
of the missing values
Figure 9.36:  can’ t do an inner join on unavailable columns
To treat missing values as equa l and include them in the join, we can pass
the matchmissing=:equal  keyword argument. Note that the row with the
missing id value in the second DataFrame is included in the result.
Alternatively , if we want to drop all rows with missing value s in the key
column, we can pass the matchmissing=:notequal  keyword argument. In
this case, only the row with id=1 in both DataFrames is included in the
result. The row with missing id value in the second DataFrame is dropped.
Figure 9.37:  inner join on missing data
When performin g a join operation on two DataFrames, the order of the
resulting rows is not always guaranteed. However , you can control the order
of the rows in the resulting DataFrame  by specifying the order parameter .
julia> df_left = DataFrame(id=[1, 2, 4, 5], left=1:4)
julia> df_right = DataFrame(id=[2, 1, 3, 6, 7], right=1:5)
To control the order of the rows in the resulting DataFrame, you can specify
the order parameter . By default, order is set to :unsorted, which means that
the order of rows in the resulting DataFrame  is undeﬁned. To keep the order
of the rows in the left DataFrame , pass order=:left .
Figure 9.38(a):  outer join on missing data (left)
This will produce a resulting DataFrame with rows in the same  order as the
left DataFrame. Note that in this case, keys missing  from the left table are
put after the keys present in it. Similarly , you can keep the order of the rows
in the right DataFrame by passing order=:right .

Figure 9.38(b):  outer join on missing data (right)
This will produce a resulting DataFrame with rows in the same  order as the
right DataFrame. Note that keys not present in the right table are put at the
end of the resulting DataFrame.
Split-apply-combine data in DataFrames
The Split-Apply-Combine (SAC) strategy is a framework for data analysis
tasks that involve splitting a dataset into groups, applying some functions to
each group, and then combining  the results. The SAC strategy is used in the
DataFrames.jl  package through the groupby function, which creates a
GroupedDataFrame object from a DataFrame and a set of columns to group
by. Operations can be applied to each group using functi ons such as
combine, select, and transform. These functions take a speciﬁcation of one
or more functions to apply to each subset of the DataFrame, which can be in
the form of standard column selectors, cols => function pairs, col =>
target_cols  pairs, column-independent operations, vectors or matrices
containing transformations, or a function that will be called with a
SubDataFrame or the DataFrame  itself. The output is a new DataFrame with
the results of the speciﬁed computations.
Figure 9.39:  Creating a new dataframe by combining columns of another dataframe
Computing summary statistics by group using combine()
julia> using DataFrames,Random, Statistics
# create a dataframe
julia> df = DataFrame(A = repeat([1,2,3], inner=3), B =
repeat([4,5,6], outer=3), C = rand(9))
# group by column A and compute mean of column C for each group
julia> result = combine(groupby(df, :A), :C => mean)
Figure 9.40:  creating a new DataFrame by combining columns of another DataFrame using the
combine() function
Creating new columns using select()
# create a dataframe
julia> df = DataFrame(A = repeat([1,2,3], inner=3), B =
repeat([4,5,6], outer=3), C = rand(9))
# group by column A and create new column D with mean of column
C for each group
julia> result = select(groupby(df, :A), :C => mean => :D)
Figure 9.41(a):  creating a new dataframe by combining columns of another dataframe using select()
function
Figure 9.41(b):  Creating a new DataFrame by combining columns of another DataFrame using the
select() function
Modifying existing columns using transform()
# create a dataframe
julia> df = DataFrame(A = repeat([1,2,3], inner=3), B =
repeat([4,5,6], outer=3), C = rand(9))
# group by column A and modify column C to be the difference
between column C and its mean within each group
julia> result = transform(groupby(df, :A), :C => (c -> c .-
mean(c)) => :C)
Figure 9.42:  Creating a new DataFrame by combining columns of another DataFrame using the
transform() and gr oupby() function
The SQL WHERE clause is used to ﬁlter rows from a table based on certain
conditions. In Julia, you can simulate this functionality using
SubDataFrames.
A SubDataFram e is a view of a DataFrame that contains only a subset of the
rows and/or columns. You can create a SubDataFrame using the indexing
syntax: df[row_selector, col_select or]. The row_selector  can be a
vector of Boolea n values or an integer index, while the col_selector  can be
a vector of column names or an integer index.
Once you have a SubDataFrame, you can perform operations on it just like a
regular DataFra me. The only difference is that these operations may either
create a new DataFrame or modify the original DataFrame in place,
depending on whether you use the transform  or transform!  Function.
julia> df = DataFrame(a=1:5) # create a DataFrame
julia> sdf = @view df[2:3, :]
julia> transform(sdf, :a => ByRow(string))
julia> transform!(sdf, :a => ByRow(string)) # update the
original DataFrame in-place with the new column
Figure 9.43:  Creating a new DataFrame by combining columns of another DataFrame using the
transform function
julia> df # the new column was added to the original DataFrame,
with missing values in the filtered-out rows
Figure 9.44:  Creating a new DataFrame by combining columns of another DataFrame using the
combine() function
julia> select!(sdf, :a => -, renamecols=false) # subtract a
constant from the “a” column in the SubDataFrame
Figure 9.45:  Creating a new DataFrame by combining columns of another DataFrame using the
select!() function
julia> df # the “a” column in the original DataFrame was
replaced with the modified column, reusing values in filtered-
out rows

Figure 9.46:  Creating a new DataFrame by combining columns of another DataFrame using the
select!() function
In the ﬁrst example, we create a new column in the SubDataF rame sdf by
applying the string function to each row of the column. We use the transform
function, which creates a new DataFrame with the transformed column.
In the second example, we use the transform!  function to modify a column
of the original DataFrame df  in place with the transformed column from sdf.
In the third example, we subtrac t a constant from a column of sdf using the
select!  function. We set renamecols= false to keep the original column
name. We then use select!  again to update a column of df with the
modiﬁed column from sdf.
Note that in the third and fourth examples, the modiﬁed column in df reuses
values from the original colum n in ﬁltered-out rows. This is because the
SubDataFrame is a view of the original DataFrame, and changes to the
SubDataFrame af fect the original DataFrame.
In Julia,  the groupby function allows us to group rows of a DataFrame by
one or more columns. By default, the order of groups produced by groupby
is undeﬁned. However , if we want to specify a group order , we can use the
sort keyword ar gument.
julia> df = DataFrame(customer_id = [“a”, “b”, “b”, “b”, “c”,
“c”],
transaction_id = [12, 15, 19, 17, 13, 11],
volume = [2, 3, 1, 4, 5, 9])
Figure 9.47:  Output
We can then group the rows by the volume column
julia> groupby(df, :volume)
By default, the order of groups is undeﬁned. We can pass the sort keyword
argument to specify the group order
julia> groupby(df, :volume, sort=true)
Figure 9.48:  creating a new DataFrame by combining columns of another DataFrame using the
groupby() function
Alternatively , we can use the order wrapper when passing a column name to
a group by:
julia> groupby(df, [:customer_id, order(:volume, rev=true)])
We can also pass a named tuple  as the sort keyword argument. The named
tuple can contain one or more of the following ﬁelds:
alg: the sorting algorithm (default is sort)
lt: the comparison function (default is <)
by: the transformation function (default is identity)
rev: a boolean ﬂag indicating whether to sort in reverse order (default is
false)
order: the order in which to sort the elements (default is sortperm, you
can look into
https://dataframes.juliadata.or g/stable/lib/functions/#Base.sortperm  to
better understand sortperm)
For example, to sort the groups in reverse order of the volume column, we
can pass the named tuple (rev=true,).
julia> groupby(df, :customer_id, sort=(rev=true,))
Figure 9.49:  creating a new DataFrame by combining columns of another DataFrame using the
groupby() function
Conclusion
The chapter covers important aspects of data analysis using DataFrames in
Julia, including handling missin g data, sorting data, reshaping data, working
with categorical data, joining data, and split-apply-combine data analysis.
The Missings.jl package is used for handling missing data while sorting is
done using the sort and sort! functions. The melt and pivot functions are
used for reshaping data, and the CategoricalArrays.jl package is used for
working with categorical data. Joining DataFrames is done using the join
function, while split-apply-combine data analysis is done using  the groupby
and combine functions. These techniques can help data analysts gain
valuable insights from their data.
In the next chapter , we will learn about visualizing data. Julia has several
powerful data visualization libraries such as Plots.jl that provide a wide
range of options for creating interactive and high-quality plots and charts.
These libraries allow for easy integration with Julia’ s data manipulation and
analysis tools, making it an excellent choice for data visualization.

CHAPTER 10
Visualizing Data in Julia
Visualization is an essential aspect of data analysis and communication. In
the realm of scientiﬁc computing, the ability to create rich, interactive
visualizations of complex data sets is crucial. Julia, a high-performance
programming language for numerical computing, provides a variety of tools
for creating stunning visualizations.
We will introduce Plot.jl, a powerful and ﬂexible plotting package that
provides a wide variety of plot types and customization optio ns. We will
explore various plot types, including scatter plots, bar charts, and heat maps,
and see how they can be used to represent different types of data. Finally , we
will demonstrate how to create a recipe using Plot.jl, which allows us to
quickly reproduce complex visualizations with just a few lines of code.
StatsPlots.jl is a popular data visualization package in the Julia programming
language that provides a user-friendly and versatile interface  for creating
statistical plots. It builds on top of the widely used plotting library, Plots.jl,
and incorporates many common statistical plot types such as scatter plots,
histograms, box plots, and heatmaps. With StatsPlots.jl, users can easily
customize their plots with different colors, themes, and annotations to
effectively communicate their data insights. Additionally , StatsPlots.jl also
supports statistical transformations, such as log scales and probability
distributions, to enhance data visualization and analysis.
By the end of this chapter , you will have a solid understanding of the key
features of Plots.jl and StatsPlot s.jl and how they can be used to create high-
quality visualizations in Julia.
Structure

Introducing Plots.jl
