Introducing Plot.jl
Data visualizati on has evolved signiﬁcantly over the years, with plotting
software making trade-of fs between various features such as simplicity ,
speed, beauty , and static or dynamic interfaces. While some packages
generate a display that remains unchanged, others make real-time updates.
Plots.jl is a visualization interface and toolset that sits above various
backends, conne cting commands with implementation. This package allows
you to switch between backends such as GR, PythonPlot, PGFPlotsX, or
Plotly , without having to alter your code or learn a new syntax. With its
powerful, intuitive, concise, ﬂexible, consistent, lightweight , and smart
features, Plots.jl makes complex visualizations easy while maintaining
modularity and separation of front-end code, algorithms, and backend
graphics through the use of a preprocessing pipeline.
Plot.jl is a powerful and ﬂexib le plotting package for Julia, designed to
create a wide variety of high-qu ality plots with minimal boilerplate code. It
provides a simple, consistent interface for creating a wide range of plot
types, including scatter plots, line plots, bar charts, histograms, heat maps,
and more.
One of the key strengths of Plot.jl is its extensive support for customization.
Users can customize almost every aspect of their plots, including the colors,
line styles, marker types, fonts, and axis labels. The package also provides a
range of layout options, making  it easy to arrange multiple plots on a single
page.
Plot.jl is built on top of the GR.jl graphics library , which provides a fast,
high-quality rendering engine. This makes it possible to create interactive,
high-resolution plots that can be used in scientiﬁc publications,
presentations, or web applications.
Another notable feature of Plot.jl is its support for statistical plotting. It
provides several functions for creating statistical plots, such as box plots,
violin plots, and error bars, which can help users explore and analyze
complex datasets.
In addition to its core functionality , Plot.jl is also highly extensible, with a
large ecosystem  of community-developed packages that provid e additional
functionality , such as advanced statistical plotting, 3D plotting, and
animations.
Installing Plots.jl
Installing the Plots.jl package is a straightforward process. First, you need to
have Julia installed on your system. Once you have installed Julia, you can
launch the Julia REPL  (read-eval-print loop) by typing “julia” on the
terminal.
Next, you need to install the Plots package by typing the following
command in the Julia REPL:
julia> using Pkg
julia> Pkg.add(“Plots”)
This command will download  and install the Plots package and all its
dependencies.
After the install ation is complete, you can load the Plots package by typing
the following command in the Julia REPL:
julia> using Plots
This command will load the Plots package and make all its functions
available for use. You can now start creating beautiful and interactive
visualizations using the Plots package in Julia.
We can customize the Plots package by choosing a speciﬁc backend or
overriding defau lt settings. A backend is a speciﬁc library or tool that Plots
uses to create the visualizations. For example, some of the available
backends are GR, PGFPlotsX, and PlotlyJS. To choose a back end, we can
use a command like gr()  or plotly() , followed by optional parameters such
as size, legend,  or ticks. These  parameters can be used to customize the
visual appearance of the plot. If you want to use a speciﬁ c backe nd as the
default, you can set an environment variable in your
~/.julia/config/startup.jl  ﬁle.
Plots.jl package
The Plots packa ge provides a simple and ﬂexible way to create plots in Julia.
To create a plot, you can use the plot function, which takes various types of
input arguments . For instance, you can pass in a single number to create an
empty plot object with that many series, or a matrix to create a plot with
multiple series. You can also pass in functions or data to create plots with
custom series. To modify an existing plot, you can use the plot! function,
which takes similar arguments as the plot. The plot object is not displayed
automatically but can be shown by returning it to a REPL  prom pt or IJulia
cell.
julia> # Create an empty plot object
p = plot()
Warning: Ignoring XDG_SESSION_TYPE=wayland on Gnome. Use
QT_QPA_PLATFORM=wayland to run on Wayland anyway.
julia> # Add a scatter plot to the existing plot object
x = rand(10)
10-element Vector{Float64}:
julia> y = rand(10)
10-element Vector{Float64}:
julia> plot!(p, x, y, seriestype=:scatter)
julia> # Add a line plot to the existing plot object
z = sin.(x)
10-element Vector{Float64}:
julia> plot!(p, x, z, seriestype=:line)
julia> # Show the plot
display(p)
This code outputs a graph as Figur e 10.1 .
Figure 10.1:  Simple plot with random x and y values
Keyword arguments can be used to customize the plot appeara nce, such as
setting the color , marker , or ﬁll color for the series, as well as setting the plot
size, title, or legend. Many keyw ord arguments have aliases for convenience,
and some special arguments can set multiple properties at once. Additionally ,
some arguments  can accept multiple types of input, such as color names,
color gradients,  or vectors of colors or symbols. Understanding these
keyword argume nts is important for creating custom and visually appealing
plots.
julia> plot(rand(10, 5)) #Plot 5 series with x-axis values 1:10
Figure 10.2:  Plot for 5 series with x-axis values ranging between 1 and 10
We can also use keyword arguments to customize plots. These arguments
can be used to change properties such as colors, markers, labels, legends,
and more. Here is an example:
julia> using Plots
julia> # Plot a scatter plot with custom color and marker
julia> x = rand(10)
julia> y = rand(10)
julia> plot(x, y, color=:red, marker=:circle)
Figure 10.3:  A scatter plot with custom color and marker
julia> # Plot a line plot with custom labels and legend
julia> z = sin.(x)
julia> plot(x, z, label=”sin(x)”, xlabel=”x”, ylabel=”y”)
Figure 10.4:  A line plot with custom labels and legend
Note that the color and marker arguments are examples of keyword
arguments that accept symbols  as input. Other keyword arguments may
accept dif ferent types of input.
In Plots.jl, you can save your plots using the savefig  command. By default,
if the plot variable is not speciﬁed, the global CURRENT_PLOT  is used
automatically . The savefig  command takes the ﬁlename and extension as its
arguments, and it saves the current plot in the speciﬁed format. For example,
to save the current plot as a PNG ﬁle, you can use the following code:
julia> savefig(“myplot.png”)
“/home/dnabanita7/myplot.png”
julia> png(“myplot”)
“myplot.png”
julia> savefig(p, “myplot.pdf”)
You can also save a speciﬁc plot object p as a png ﬁle. In addition to
savefig , there are other convenience functions such as png()  that allow you
to save your plots without specifying the extension.
Attributes in plotting
Plots.jl is a plotting package in Julia that allows for the easy customization
of plots through  the use of attributes. Attributes are modiﬁers to plots that
can be applied using positional and keyword arguments, or through modiﬁer
functions. Positional arguments correspond to input data, while keyword
arguments correspond to attributes.
For exam ple, to change the line width of a plot, we can use the linewidth
attribute (or its alias lw) as a keyword ar gument, like this:
julia> x = range(0, 10, length=100)
julia> y1 = sin.(x)
julia> y2 = cos.(x)
julia> plot(x, [y1 y2], title=”Trigonometric functions”, label=
[“sin(x)” “cos(x)”], linewidth=3)
Figure 10.5:  A plot with various attributes added
Attributes can also be used to modify other aspects of a plot, such as axis
labels, legend position, and line styles. For example, to add a label for the x-
axis, we can use the xlabel attribute as a keyword argument or the xlabel!
modiﬁer function. Similarly , to change the legend position to be outside the
plot and to have three columns, we can use the legend and legendcolumns
attributes.
julia> xlabel!(“x”)
julia> plot!(legend=:outerbottom, legendcolumns=2)
Figure 10.6:  A plot with various attributes added with legend and labeled x-axis
In addit ion, Plots.jl works with LaTeXStrings.jl  to allow for LaTeX-
formatted equat ion strings to be used in plots. To use LaTeXStrings.jl ,
simply install it with Pkg.add(“LaTeXStrings”)  and prepend L to the
LaTeX-formatted string.
(@v1.9) pkg> add LaTeXStrings
julia> using LaTeXStrings
julia> x = 10 .^ range(0, 4, length=100)
julia> y = @. 1/(1+x)
julia> plot(x, y, label=L”\frac{1}{1+x}”)
julia> plot!(xscale=:log10, yscale=:log10, minorgrid=true)
julia> title!(L”Log-log plot of $\frac{1}{1+x}$”)
julia> xlims!(1e+0, 1e+4)
julia> ylims!(1e-5, 1e+0)
julia> xlabel!(L”x”)
julia> ylabel!(L”y”)
Figure 10.7:  A plot with logarithmic graph
Plots.jl allows for logarithmic scale plots to be created using the xscale and
yscale attributes. These attributes can be set to :log10 to create a log-log
plot, or :identity  to keep  the scale linear . Care should be taken to ensure
that the data and limits are positive.
Scatter plots in Plots.jl
In Plots. jl, there  are different options for plotting data, and line plots are the
traditional way. However , there are other options called “series types,” and
one common type is a scatter plot that displays individual data points.
For example, we can deﬁne a sine function and create a vector called
y_noisy  that adds some randomness. To change from a line plot to a scatter
plot, we can use the seriestype  attribute, or use the shorthand  function
scatter!.
The series types available depend on the backend used. Additionally , other
libraries can add new series types using recipes.
In the example code, the text provides common attributes used in scatter
plots, such as line color (lc), line width (lw), marker color (mc), marker size
(ms), and marker alpha (ma).
The ﬁnal example code provid ed puts everything together and creates a
scatter plot with a sine function and some noisy data points. The plot is
customized with different colors, marker sizes, and a legend. The plot is also
given a title, and the x and y axes are labeled  to make it easier to interpret the
data.
julia> using Plots
# Define a range of values for the x-axis
julia> x = range(0, 10, length=100)
# Define a sine function for the y-axis
julia> y = sin.(x)
# Add some noise to the sine function to create a “noisy”
dataset
julia> y_noisy = @. sin(x) + 0.1*randn()
# Create a basic line plot of the sine function
julia> plot(x, y, label=”sin(x)”)
# Change the plot type to a scatter plot and add the noisy
dataset
julia> scatter!(x, y_noisy, label=”data”)
# Customize the plot with different colors, marker sizes, and a
legend
julia> plot!(
legend=:bottomleft, # Position the legend in the bottom-left
corner
title=”Sine with noise”, # Add a title to the plot
xlabel=”x”, # Label the x-axis
ylabel=”y”, # Label the y-axis
lc=:black, lw=2, # Customize the line color and width of the
sine function
mc=:red, ms=2, ma=0.5, # Customize the marker color, size, and
transparency of the noisy data
)
Figure 10.8:  A plot with sine graph with random data
This code deﬁne s a range of x values and a corresponding sine function for
the y values. It then adds some random noise to the sine function to create a
new dataset, y_noisy .
The code ﬁrst creates a basic line plot of the sine function using the plot
function. It then changes the plot type to a scatter plot using the seriestype
attribute with the value :scatter . The scatter! function can also be used to
create the scatter plot directly .
Finally , the code customizes the plot using various attributes like legend,
title, xlabel, ylabel, lc, lw, mc, ms, and ma. These attributes control the
appearance of the plot, like the position and content of the legend, the color
and width of the sine function line, and the color , size, and transparency of
the noisy data points.
This code should produce a scatter plot with a sine function and some noisy
data points, like the example provided in the original text.
Plotting backends
Plots.jl is a package that acts as an interface over multiple plotting libraries,
which are called backends. The purpose of Plots.jl is to interpret your plot
commands and generate the plot using one of the supported backends. This
means you can use the same syntax across different plotting libraries, which
is very convenient.
The default backend is GR, but you can easily switch to a different backend
if you want. For example, if you want to use Plotly , you can install the
package by running Pkg.add(“PlotlyJS”)  in the REPL. Then, you can set
the back end to Plotly  by calling the plotlyjs()  functio n. Similarly , you
can switch back to GR by calling the gr() function.
Each backend has its features and limitations. For example, some backends
like Plotly  provide  interactive plots, while others like GR are better at
dealing with large datasets. Some backends can create 3D plots,  while others
are limited to 2D. Additionally , some backends like GR can save plots as
vector graphics, while others like Plotly  can only save plots as PNG
images.
Here’ s an example of how to switch between backends and create plots using
different backends:
julia> using Plots
# Set the backend to Plotly
julia> plotlyjs()
Plots.PlotlyJSBackend()
# Create a plot using Plotly
julia> x = range(0, 10, length=100)
julia> y = sin.(x)
julia> y_noisy = @. sin(x) + 0.1*randn()
julia> plot(x, y, label=”sin(x)”, lc=:black, lw=2)
[ Info: Listening on: 127.0.0.1:9171, thread id: 1
Figure 10.9:  A plot with sine graph
julia> scatter!(x, y_noisy, label=”data”, mc=:red, ms=2,
ma=0.5)
Figure 10.10:  A plot with sine graph with random data
julia> plot!(legend=:bottomleft)
julia> title!(“Sine with noise, plotted with Plotly”)
julia> xlabel!(“x”)
julia> ylabel!(“y”)
# Save the plot to a PNG file
julia> savefig(“plotlyjs_tutorial.png”)
“/home/dnabanita7/plotlyjs_tutorial.png”
Figure 10.1 1: A plot with sine graph with random data which means some noise is added
# Set the backend back to GR
julia> gr()
Plots.GRBackend()
# Create a plot using GR
julia> plot(x, y, label=”sin(x)”, lc=:black, lw=2)
julia> scatter!(x, y_noisy, label=”data”, mc=:red, ms=2,
ma=0.5)
julia> plot!(legend=:bottomleft)
julia> title!(“Sine with noise, plotted with GR”)
julia> xlabel!(“x”)
julia> ylabel!(“y”)
Figure 10.12:  A plot with sine graph with random data with GR backend
Subplots in Plots.jl
To combine multiple plots as subplots, we can use layouts.  The layout
command takes  in a 2-tuple layout=(N, M) which builds an NxM grid of
plots, and it will automatically split a series to be in each plot. For example,
if we type layou t=(3, 1) on a plot with three series, then we will get three
rows of plots, each with one series in it.
Here’ s an example of how to use the layout command to plot three separate
functions in three separate plots, each with only one series, and without a
legend:
julia> x = range(0, 10, length=100)
julia> y1 = @. exp(-0.1x) * cos(4x)
julia> y2 = @. exp(-0.3x) * cos(4x)
julia> y3 = @. exp(-0.5x) * cos(4x)
julia> plot(x, [y1 y2 y3], layout=(3, 1), legend=false)
Figure 10.13:  Three subplots with exponential and cosine function
We can also use layouts on plots of plot objects. For example, we can
generate four separate plots and make a single plot that combine s them into a
2×2 grid. Here’ s an example:
julia> x = range(0, 10, length=100)
julia> y1 = @. exp(-0.1x) * cos(4x)
julia> y2 = @. exp(-0.3x) * cos(4x)
julia> y3 = @. exp(-0.1x)
julia> y4 = @. exp(-0.3x)
julia> y = [y1 y2 y3 y4]
julia> p1 = plot(x, y)
julia> p2 = plot(x, y, title=”Title 2”, lw=3)
julia> p3 = scatter(x, y, ms=2, ma=0.5, xlabel=”xlabel 3”)
julia> p4 = scatter(x, y, title=”Title 4”, ms=2, ma=0.2)
julia> plot(p1, p2, p3, p4, layout=(2,2), legend=false)
Figure 10.14:  Multiple subplots of scatter ed data
Recipes in Plots.jl
In Plots.jl, a recipe is a method for creating custom plot types or modifying
existing plot types. Recipes allow users to extend the functional ity of Plots.jl
beyond the built-in series types, and they can be used to create custom plot
types, modify plot attributes, or even create entirely new backends.
A recipe  deﬁnes how to plot data with a speciﬁc type of plot and can be
thought of as a set of instructions for creating a plot. A recipe speciﬁes how
to transform data to create the plot, what type of plot to create, and how to
customize its appearance.
Recipes can be deﬁned by users, and there are many examples of recipes
available in the Plots.jl ecosystem. The Plots.jl documentation provides
extensive guidance on how to create and use recipes to extend the
capabilities of the package.
Using Statsplots.jl recipes
Plotting recipes in Julia’ s Plots. jl framework is an extension that adds new
plot commands,  default interpretations of Julia types as plotting data, new
functions for generating plots, and new series types. Recipe libraries, such as
PlotRecipes.jl and StatsPlots.jl, provide a collection of pre-made recipes that
can be used to make advanced plots without having to write your recipes.
StatsPlots.jl is a recipe library that adds a type recipe for distributions and a
plot recipe for marginal histograms, along with several new statistical plot
series. It also provides a specialized macro, @df, that enables plotting
directly from data tables.
Here are some examples of how to use StatsPlots.jl:
Using User  Recipes : The @df macr o allows you to plot a DataFrame
directly using column names. For instance, to plot a DataFrame  with
columns a, b, and c and use a as the x-axis and plot the series deﬁned
by columns b and c, you can use the following code:
(@v1.9) pkg> add StatsPlots
julia> using StatsPlots, DataFrames
julia> df = DataFrame(a=1:10, b=10*rand(10), c=10*rand(10))
10×3 DataFrame
Row │ a   b     c
│ Int64  Float64
─────┼──────────────────────────
  1 │  1  2.17912  9.95485
  2 │  2  5.54207  3.7425
  3 │  3  5.97152  1.39592
  4 │  4  1.92392  0.581316
  5 │  5  9.95078  2.85454
  6 │  6  1.19948  3.57068
  7 │  7  1.46569  0.900923
  8 │  8  8.10511  9.94493
  9 │  9  9.38279  5.05676
10 │ 10  9.7275   8.22645
julia> @df df plot(:a, [:b :c])
Figure 10.15:  Data plotted with Statsplots.jl
Using Type Recipes: StatsPlots.jl  extends  Distributions.jl  by adding
a type recipe for its distribu tion types, so they can be directly
interpreted as plotting data. For example, to plot a normal distribution
with a mean of 3 and a standard deviation of 5, you can use the
following code:
(@v1.9) pkg> add Distributions
julia> using Distributions, StatsPlots
julia> plot(Normal(3, 5), lw=3)
Figure 10.16:  Normal distribution plotted with StatsPlots.jl
Using Plot Recipes: StatsPlots.jl  adds the marginalhist  multiplot via a
plot recipe. For example, to plot a marginal histogram for the
PetalLength  and the PetalWidth  columns of the iris dataset, you can
use the following code:
julia> using RDatasets, StatsPlots
julia> iris = dataset(“datasets”, “iris”)
julia> @df iris marginalhist(:PetalLength, :PetalWidth)
Figure 10.17:  Iris dataset plotted with StatsPlots.jl
Using Series Recipes : StatsPlots.jl introduces new series recipes. For
instance, to use the Violin plot on some random data and add a boxplot
on top, you can use the following code:
julia> using StatsPlots
julia> y = rand(100, 4)
julia> violin([“Series 1” “Series 2” “Series 3” “Series 4”], y,
legend=false)
julia> boxplot!([“Series 1” “Series 2” “Series 3” “Series 4”],
y, legend=false)
Figure 10.18:  Violin and box plots plotted with StatsPlots.jl
Contour plots
Contour plots are a way of visualizing a 3D surface on a 2D plane by using
contour lines to represent the constant values of the surface. In Julia, we can
create contour plots using the contour() function from the Plots.jl package.
Here is an example code that creates a simple contour plot:
julia> using Plots
julia> # Define a function to plot
julia> f(x, y) = x^2 + y^2
julia> # Generate x and y coordinates for the plot
julia> x = -5:0.1:5
julia> y = -5:0.1:5
julia> # Evaluate the function at each (x, y) point
julia> z = [f(xi, yj) for xi in x, yj in y]
julia> # Create the contour plot
julia> contour(x, y, z, levels=10)
Figure 10.19:  3D contour plots using the Plots.jl package
In this code, we ﬁrst deﬁne a simple function f(x, y) that we want to plot.
We then generate x and y coordinates using the range()  functio n and
evaluate the function at each point to create a 2D array z of function values.
Finally , we use the contour()  function to create the plot by passing in the x,
y, and z arrays as arguments. We also specify the number of contour levels
we want to display using the levels ar gument.
We can also customize the appearance of the plot by passing in additional
arguments to the contour()  function. For example, we can add labels to the
contour lines and change the color scheme:
julia> # Create the contour plot with customizations
julia> contour(x, y, z, levels=10, labels=true, color=:viridis)
Figure 10.20:  3D contour plots using the Plots.jl package with various attributes
In this code, we add the labels=true argument to display labels for each
contour line and change the color scheme to color=:viridis  using the color
argument.
We can also create ﬁlled conto ur plots by using the contourf()  function
instead of contour() . Here is an example:
julia> # Create a filled contour plot
julia> contourf(x, y, z, levels=10, color=:plasma)
Figure 10.21:  3D ﬁlled Contour plots using the Plots.jl package with various attributes
In this code, we use the contourf()  function instead of contour()  to creat e
a ﬁlled contour plot. We also change the color scheme to color=:plasma
using the color ar gument.
We can also create a contour plot with labeled contour lines by using the
clabels  keyword argument. The clabels  argument takes a boolean value,
indicating whet her to add labels to the contour lines or not. Here’ s an
example:
using Plots
# Define a function to plot
julia> f(x, y) = sin(x) * cos(y)
# Generate x and y coordinates for the plot
julia> x = range(-5, stop=5, length=100)
julia> y = range(-5, stop=5, length=100)
# Evaluate the function at each (x, y) point
julia> z = [f(xi, yj) for xi in x, yj in y]
# Create the contour plot
julia> contour(x, y, z, levels=10, clabels=true)
Figure 10.22:  3D Contour sub-plots using the Plots.jl package with various attributes
In this example, we’ve deﬁned a new function to plot, f(x, y) = sin(x) *
cos(y) . We’ve also changed the x and y arrays to be generated using the
range()  function with a length ar gument instead of a step ar gument.
We’ve also added the clabels=true  argume nt to the contour()  function to
add labels to the contour lines.
Finally , we can also customize  the levels at which the contour lines are
displayed by passing in an array of levels using the levels argument. Here’ s
an example:
julia> using Plots
julia> # Define a function to plot
julia> f(x, y) = sin(x) * cos(y)
julia> # Generate x and y coordinates for the plot
julia> x = range(-5, stop=5, length=100)
julia> y = range(-5, stop=5, length=100)
julia> # Evaluate the function at each (x, y) point
julia> z = [f(xi, yj) for xi in x, yj in y]
julia> # Create the contour plot with custom levels
julia> contour(x, y, z, levels=[-0.8, -0.6, -0.4, -0.2, 0, 0.2,
0.4, 0.6, 0.8], clabels=true)
Figure 10.23:  3D Contour sub-plots using the Plots.jl package with various attributes
In this example, we’ve speciﬁed a custom set of contour levels using the
levels argument . The contour lines will be displayed only at these speciﬁed
levels.
These are some  examples of how to create and customize contour plots in
Julia using the Plots.jl package.
Histograms in Plots.jl
Histograms are a way of visualizing the distribution of a dataset by showing
the frequency or proportion of values that fall within each interv al, or bin, of
the dataset. In Julia, we can create histograms using the histogram()
function from the Plots.jl package.
Here is an example code that creates a simple histogram:
julia> using Plots
julia> # Generate some random data
julia> data = randn(1000)
julia> # Create the histogram
julia> histogram(data)
Figure 10.24:  Histograms using the Plots.jl package
In this code, we ﬁrst generate a random dataset using the randn()  function.
We then pass this dataset to the histogram()  function to create the plot.
By default, the histogram()  functio n will use 10 equally spaced bins to
display the distribution of the data. We can change the numbe r of bins by
passing in the nbins  argument:
julia> # Create the histogram with 10 bins
julia> histogram(data, nbins=10)
In this code, we’ve speciﬁed that we want to use 10 bins to display the
distribution of the data.
We can also customize the appearance of the histogram by passing in
additional arguments to the histogram()  function. For example, we can
change the color of the bars and add a title to the plot:
julia> # Create the histogram with customizations
julia> histogram(data, nbins=10, color=:yellow, title=”Random
Data Histogram”)
Figure 10.25:  Histograms using the Plots.jl package with various attributes
Finally , we can create a stacked histogram by passing in multiple datasets to
the histogram()  function:
julia> # Generate two random datasets
julia> data1 = randn(1000)
julia> data2 = randn(1000) .+ 2
julia> # Create a stacked histogram
julia> histogram(data1, data2, nbins=20, normalize=true,
color=”gray”, title=”Stacked Histogram”)
Figure 10.26:  Stacked histograms using the Plots.jl package with various attributes
In this code, we’ve added the normalize=true argument to create a
normalized histogram where the area of the bars sums to 1.
In this code, we’ve generated two random datasets, data1 and data2, and
passed them both to the histogram()  function. The resulting plot is a
stacked histogram where the bars for each dataset are stacked on top of each
other .
Weighted histograms
In a weighted histogram, each data point is associated with a weight, which
determines its contribution to the histogram. The histogram()  functio n in
Julia can also be used to create weighted histograms.
Here is an example code snippet to create a weighted histogram of some
random data:
julia> using Plots
# Generate some random data with weights
julia> data = [(randn(), rand()) for i=1:1000]
# Create a weighted histogram with 10 bins
julia> histogram(data, bins=10, weights=[d[2] for d in data])
Figure 10.27:  Weighted histograms using the Plots.jl package with various attributes
In the preceding code, we ﬁrst load the Plots package. Then, we generate
some random data with weights using a list comprehension. Finally , we
create a weighted histogram of the data using the histogram()  function and
specifying the weights ar gument.
Here is another example that demonstrates some additional options:
julia> using Plots
julia> # Generate some random data with weights
julia> data = [(randn(), rand()) for i=1:1000]
julia> # Create a weighted histogram with 20 bins and a red
color
julia> histogram(data, bins=20, weights=[d[2] for d in data],
color=:red)
julia> # Add a title and labels to the plot
julia> title!(“Weighted Histogram of Random Data”)
julia> xlabel!(“Values”)
julia> ylabel!(“Weighted Frequency”)
Figure 10.28:  Weighted histograms of random data against weight fr equency of data
In this code, we create a weighted histogram with 20 bins and set the color to
red. We also add a title and labels to the plot using the title!() , xlabel!() ,
and ylabel!()  functio ns. Note that we have changed the y-axis label to
reﬂect the fact that we are now plotting weighted frequencies.
In addition to standard and weighted histograms, the Plots package in Julia
offers several other variations of histograms. Here are some examples of
these variations, along with code samples:
Stacked histograms
A stacke d histog ram shows multiple histograms stacked on top of each other ,
with the heights of each bin representing the relative contributions of
different groups. Here is an example code snippet:
julia> using Plots
julia> # Generate some random data for two groups
julia> data1 = randn(1000)
julia> data2 = randn(1000) .+ 2
julia> # Create a stacked histogram with 10 bins for each group
julia> histogram([data1, data2], bins=10, label=[“Group 1”
“Group 2”], alpha=0.6)
Figure 10.29:  Stacked histograms with various color attributes
In this code, we generate some random data for two groups and then create a
stacked histogram of the data using the histogram()  functio n with two
arrays of data as input. We also specify the number of bins, add labels for
each group, and set the transparency level using the alpha ar gument.
Overlaid histograms
An overlaid histogram shows multiple histograms overlaid on top of each
other , with different colors repre senting different groups. Here is an example
code snippet:
julia> using Plots
julia> # Generate some random data for two groups
julia> data1 = randn(1000)
julia> data2 = randn(1000) .+ 2
julia> # Create an overlaid histogram with 10 bins for each
group
julia> histogram([data1, data2], bins=10, label=[“Group 1”
“Group 2”], alpha=0.6,
   normalize=true, xlim=(-4, 8), ylim=(0, 0.6), color=[:blue
:red])
Figure 10.30:  Stacked histograms overlaid on top of each other with various color attributes
In this code, we generate some random data for the two groups and then
create an overlaid histogram of the data using the histogram()  function with
two arrays of data as input. We also specify the number of bins, add labels
for each group, set the transparency level, normalize the histograms, set the
x- and y-axis limits, and set the colors using the color ar gument.
Cumulative histograms
A cumulative histogram shows the cumulative distribution function (CDF)
of a dataset, which represents the probability that a randomly selected value
from the dataset is less than or equal to a given value. Here is an example
code snippet:
julia> using Plots
julia> # Generate some random data
julia> data = randn(1000)
julia> # Create a cumulative histogram with 20 bins
julia> histogram(data, bins=20, norm=:cdf, fillalpha=0.3,
line=:step, label=”CDF”)
Figure 10.31:  Cumulative histograms displaying the cumulative distribution function of data
In this code, we generate some random data and then create a cumulative
histogram of the data using the histogram()  function with the norm
argument set to :cdf. We also specify the number of bins,  set the ﬁll
transparency level, use a step function to show the CDF and add a label.
2D histograms
2D histograms are a type of histogram that visualize the joint distribution of
two variables. In Julia, 2D histograms can be created using the
histogram2d()  functio n from the Plots package. Here’ s an example code
snippet to create a 2D histogram:
julia> using Plots
julia> # Generate some random 2D data
julia> data = randn(1000, 2)
julia> # Create a 2D histogram with 20 bins in each direction
julia> histogram2d(data[:,1], data[:,2], nbins=20, color=:heat)
Figure 10.32:  2D histograms displaying the joint distribution of data
In this code, we generate some random 2D data and then create a 2D
histogram of the data using the histogram2d()  function . We specify the x-
and y-coordinates of the data, the number of bins in each direction, and the
color scheme for the plot using the nbins  and color ar guments.
You can also create weighted 2D histograms by specifying the weights of
each data point using the weights argument. For example, to create a
weighted 2D histogram with the weight of each point proportio nal to its x-
coordinate, you can do:
julia> using Plots
julia> # Generate some random 2D data
julia> data = randn(1000, 2)
julia> # Create weights proportional to the x-coordinate
julia> weights = data[:,1]
julia> # Create a weighted 2D histogram with 20 bins in each
direction
julia> histogram2d(data[:,1], data[:,2], weights=weights,
nbins=20, color=:heat)
Figure 10.33:  Weighted 2D histograms displaying the joint distribution of data
In this code, we generate some random 2D data and then create weights
proportional to the x-coordina te of each data point. We then create a
weighted 2D histogram of the data using the histogram2d()  function with
the weights ar gument set to the weights we just created.
Conclusion
In this chapter , Plots.jl is a powerful and ﬂexible data visualiza tion package
in the Julia programming language, offering a wide range of plotting
functionalities to suit various needs. Installing and using Plots.jl is
straightforward, and users can choose from different backends and attributes
to custom ize their plots. Scatter plots and subplots are some commonly used
plot types in Plots.jl, while StatsPlots.jl provides a convenient interface for
creating statistic al plots with added functionalities. Users can also create
contour plots, histograms, and other types of plots using Plots.jl and
StatsPlots.jl. With its user-friendly interface and extensive functionalities,
Plots.jl is an essential tool for data visualization and analysis in Julia.
In the next chapter , we will learn about machine learning in Julia.

CHAPTER  11
Introducing Machine Learning in Julia
Introduction
Julia is a high-performance programming language that has been gaining
popularity in the ﬁeld of data science and machine learning due to its speed,
ease of use, and versatility . With its powerful mathematical and statistical
libraries and its ability to efﬁciently handle large datasets, Julia  provides an
excellent platform for performing machine learning tasks.
There are several libraries in Julia that support machine learning, each with
its own unique set of features and capabilities. One of the most popular
libraries is Flux.jl, which provides a high-level interface for building and
training deep neural networks. Flux.jl offers a wide range of layers, loss
functions, and optimization algorithms, making it a versatile and powerful
tool for building complex models.
Another popula r library for machine learning in Julia is MLJ.jl, which
provides a uniﬁed interface for working with various machine learning
algorithms, inclu ding classiﬁcation, regression, clustering, and more. MLJ.jl
also offers a range of tools for data preprocessing, feature selection, and
model tuning, making it a comprehensive solution for machine learning
tasks.
In addition to these libraries, several others provide specialized functionality
for mach ine learning tasks. For example, DecisionT ree.jl provide s a fast and
ﬂexible implementation of decision trees, while Clustering.jl  offers a range
of clustering algorithms for unsupervised learning.
Overall, Julia provides a powerful and ﬂexible platform for performing
machine learnin g tasks, with a range of libraries and tools available for
building, training, and deployin g models. Whether you are a data scientist,
machine learning researcher , or developer , Julia offers a rich set of tools and
capabilities for tackling complex machine learning problems.
Structure

The need for machine learning

The need for machine learning
Imagine you are a sales manager for a large retail chain. Your company has
stores across the country , and you are responsible for forecasting sales for
each store to ensure that enough inventory is available to meet customer
demand. However , forecasting sales accurately can be a challenging task,
especially with so many stores and products to consider .
To impro ve your sales forecasting, you decide to try using machine learning.
You gather historical sales data for each store, including information on
product sales, pricing, promotio ns, and more. You also collect external data,
such as weather and economic indicators, which may impact sales.
You begin by training a machine  learning model on this data to predict sales
for each store. You use a range of algorithms and technique s, including
regression analy sis, time series forecasting, and ensemble methods, to build
a robust and accurate model.
As you continue to reﬁne your model, you start to notice interes ting patterns
in the data. For example, you discover that sales for certain products are
highly correlate d with weather patterns, with sales increasing for certain
items during hot weather or rainy days. You also ﬁnd that promotions have a
signiﬁcant impact on sales, with certain types of promotions driving more
sales than others.
With this new insight, you begin to adjust your sales and marketing
strategies accordingly . You start to offer more targeted promotions based on
weather patterns and customer preferences, and you optimize pricing
strategies to maximize revenue. As a result, you are able to increase sales
and proﬁts for your company while improving customer satisfaction.
This story illustrates how machine learning can be used to improve sales
forecasting and drive business success. By analyzing large and complex
datasets, machine learning models can uncover valuable insights and
patterns that can inform decision-making and drive better outcomes.
Introducing machine learning
Machine learning is a branch of artiﬁcial intelligence that focuses on the
development of algorithms and statistical models that enable computers to
learn from and make predictions or decisions based on data. In other words,
machine learning is the process of teaching computers to learn from
experience and improve their performance on a speciﬁc task.
Machine learning algorithms can be broadly classiﬁed into three types:
supervised learning
unsupervised learning
reinforcement learning
Supervised learning involves training a model using labeled data, in which
the input and output pairs are provided to the algorithm. Unsupervised
learning, on the other hand, involves training a model on unlabeled data,
with the algorith m tasked with identifying patterns and relationships within
the data. Reinf orcement learn ing involves training a model to make
decisions based on feedback from its environment, with the goal of
maximizing a reward function.
Machine learning has numerous  applications across a wide range of ﬁelds,
from natural language processing and computer vision to ﬁnance and
healthcare. It is used to develop predictive models for tasks such as image
classiﬁcation, speech recognition, and fraud detection, as well as for
decision-making systems such as autonomous vehicles and recommendation
engines.
In recen t years, machine learning has seen signiﬁcant advancements, fueled
by the availabili ty of large amounts of data, powerful computing resources,
and breakthroughs in algorithm development. With these tools and
techniques, machine learning has the potential to revolutionize the way we
approach complex problems and make decisions in a wide range of domains.
MLJ.jl - the machine learning library of Julia
MLJ (Machine Learning in Julia) is a comprehensive machine learning
framework written in the Julia programming language. It offers a user-
friendly interface for building, training, and evaluating machine learning
models, as well as a wide range of algorithms and tools for data
preprocessing and feature selection.
One of the key features of MLJ is its ability to handle different types of data,
such as numerical, categorical, and text data, seamlessly and efﬁciently . This
allows users to work with a variety of datasets and tasks, from regression
and classiﬁcation to clustering and dimensionality reduction.
MLJ also supports various model composition and ensembling techniques,
allowing users to combine different models and algorithms  to improve
performance and generalize to new data. This includes techniques such as
stacking, bagging, and boosting, as well as meta-learning and
hyperparameter optimization.
Furthermore, MLJ provides a modular and extensible architecture, allowing
users to add new algorithms, models, and data types as needed. It also
integrates with other Julia packages, such as DataFrames.jl , Flux.jl , and
Knet.jl , enabling seamless integration with other data science and machine
learning tools.
Overall, MLJ is a powerful and ﬂexible machine learning framework that
offers a high-level and user-friendly interface, while also providing
advanced capabilities and extensibility for expert users.
Specialties of MLJ.jl
MLJ.jl is a machine learning library that is built in the Julia programming
language. It offers an extensive range of functionalities that make it suitable
for solving various machine learning problems. Here are some of the key
features of MLJ.jl:
Uniﬁed Interface: MLJ.jl provid es a uniﬁed interface for a wide range
of machine learning models, making it easy to compare and experiment
with multiple models.
Customizable and Extensible: MLJ.jl is highly customizable and
extensible, allowing users to create their own models, preprocessors,
and pipelines by implementing a few simple functions.
Data Preprocessing: MLJ.jl includes a comprehensive suite of data
preprocessing and feature engineering tools, enabling users to clean
and prepare their data for modeling.
Model Evaluation: MLJ.jl supports various model evaluation
techniques, such as cross-validation, bootstrapping, and holdout
validation, helping users to estimate the performance of their models.
Hyperparameter Tuning: MLJ.jl includes a wide range of tools for
hyperparameter tuning, such as grid search, random search, and
Bayesian optimization, helping users to ﬁnd the best hyperparameters
for their models.
The key goals of MLJ.jl are:
Ease of Use: MLJ.jl is designed to be easy to use and accessible to a
wide range of users, from beginners to experienced data scientists.
Flexibility: MLJ .jl is ﬂexible and can be used for a variety of machine
learning tasks, from classiﬁcation and regression to clustering and
anomaly detection.
Performance: MLJ.jl is designed for high-performance computing,
leveraging the power of Julia’ s just-in-time (JIT) compilation and
parallelization capabilities.
Interoperability: MLJ.jl can be easily integrated with other Julia
libraries, such as DataFrames.jl, Flux.jl, and DifferentialEqua tions.jl,
allowing users to leverage the power of multiple libraries and
frameworks.
Community: MLJ.jl has a large and active community of developers
and users, who contribute to the development and improvement of the
library .
Overall, MLJ.jl is a powerful and ﬂexible machine learning library that
offers a range of tools and functionalities for solving a variety of machine
learning problems. Whether you are a beginner or an experienced data
scientist, MLJ.j l can help you to build and evaluate effective machine
learning models.
Handling data in MLJ.jl
In machine learning, the quality  and quantity of data play a crucial role in
the success of a model. Before  building a model, you ﬁrst need to gather ,
clean, and prepare the data that you’ll use to train the model.
MLJ.jl provides a number of tools and methods to handle different types of
data. MLJ.jl provides a uniﬁed interface for handling tabular data, which is
data organized in a table forma t with rows and columns. This is the most
common type of data used in machine learning, and it’s usuall y stored in a
CSV  (Comma Separated Values) or TSV (Tab Separated Values) ﬁle format.
In addition to tabular data, MLJ.jl also supports handling other types of data,
such as text, images, and time series. For example, to work with text data,
MLJ.jl provides tools to preprocess and vectorize text data, which converts
text data into a numerical format that can be used as input for machine
learning models. Similarly , MLJ.jl provides methods for loading and
preprocessing image data, which is important for image classiﬁcation and
object detection tasks.
Data containers and Scitypes
In order to use the MLJ package for machine learning tasks, it is important
to understand the basic assump tions about the form of data expected by
MLJ. MLJ uses data containers and scientiﬁc types to represent data.
Data containers are objects that contain data, such as tuples, vectors,
matrices, and tables. Each data container has a scientiﬁc type parameterized
by scitype, which describes the scientiﬁc interpretation of the elements it
contains.
Scientiﬁc types are Julia types deﬁned in the ScientiﬁcT ypesBase.jl package,
and are used to declare the perm itted scientiﬁc type of the inputs and targets
that can be bound to a machine in MLJ. The package ScientiﬁcT ypes.jl
implements the convention used in the MLJ universe for assigni ng a speciﬁc
scientiﬁc type to each Julia object.
The basic “scalar ” scienti ﬁc types in MLJ are Continuous , Multiclass{N} ,
OrderedFactor{N} , Count , Textual , Missing , and Nothing . It is important
to ensure that scalar data is interpreted correctly by using tools to coerce the
data to have the appropriate scientiﬁc type. Here are some examples of using
scientiﬁc types in MLJ:
scitype(4.6)  returns Continuous , indicating that 4.6 has a continuous
scientiﬁc interpretation.
scitype(42)  returns Count , indicating that 42 has a count scientiﬁc
interpretation.
x1 = coerce([“yes”, “no”, “yes”, “maybe”], Multiclass)
coerces a vector of strings to have a Multiclass{3} scientiﬁc
interpretation.
X = (x1=x1, x2=rand(4), x3=rand(4))  creates a “column table”
containing three columns, each with a different scientiﬁc interpretation.
scitype(X)  returns Table{Union{AbstractVector{Continuous} ,
AbstractVector{Multiclass{3}}}} , indicating that the table contains
columns with either a continuous or Multiclass{3}  scienti ﬁc
interpretation.
By unde rstanding data contain ers and scientiﬁc types in MLJ, users can
ensure that their data is properly represented and interpreted for machine
learning tasks.
Handling 2D data in MLJ
When we talk about two-dimensional data in machine learning, we are
referring to data that is organized in rows and columns, like a spreadsheet. In
MLJ (a machine learning library in Julia), this type of data is typically
represented as a table. A table is a data container that can be accessed using
the Tables.jl  interfac e and is represented as a Table{K}, where K depends
on the scientiﬁc types of the columns.
We can create a table from this matrix by calling MLJ.table(M) , where M is
dimension. The resulting table  will have the same number of rows and
columns as the matrix, and the types of columns will be inferred from the
values in the matrix. If we have a matrix (that is, an array of numbers) that
we want to use as two-dimensional data in MLJ, we can wrap  it as a table
using the MLJ.table  function.
To inspect the types of data in a table, we can use the schema function. For
example, suppo se we have a table X with three columns, the ﬁrst column
containing categorical values, and the second and third columns containing
continuous values. We can use schema(X)  to get a summary of the types:
Figure 1 1.1: Two-dimensional tabular and non-tabular data
It is important to note that creating a table from a matri x using the
MLJ.table  function does not make a copy of the original matrix. Instead, the
matrix is simply wrapped as a table, so changes made to the original matrix
will also be reﬂected in the table. Conversely , if we want to convert a table
back to a matrix, we can use the MLJ.matrix  function.
To summarize, when using MLJ  models with tables or matrices  as input, it’s
important to make sure that the data is organized in the correct format. Each
row should represent a different observation, and each column should
represent a different feature of that observation. Additionally , the target data
should be an AbstractVector , and if it has multiple values per observation,
it can be represented as a table.
Machine Learning in Julia (MLJ) models typically assume tables as input,
following the Tables.jl  interfac e. However , it clariﬁes that any container
adhering to the Tables.jl  interface and containing compatible scientiﬁc
types can be used as input. This ﬂexibility means that users are not restricted
to using  only tables; they can employ various data structures or containers
that meet the speciﬁed interface requirements and hold the appropriate data
types. This design choice enhances the versatility of MLJ, allow ing users to
work with diverse data repre sentations while still beneﬁtin g from the
model’ s capabilities. Similarly , the target data should have a scientiﬁc type
that is compatible with the model’ s target_scitype .
By organizing the data correctly  and ensuring that it has the correct scientiﬁc
type, it becomes easier to work with MLJ models and get accurate
predictions from them.
When using MLJ models, it is important to know what types of data are
acceptable as inputs and targets. You can inspect the admissible scientiﬁc
types of a model’ s input and target without loading the code deﬁning the
model. This can be done using the info function in MLJ.
For example, if we want to inspect the admissible scientiﬁc types of the
DecisionTreeClassifier  model in the DecisionT ree package, we can do:
Figure 1 1.2: Basic information about the Decision T ree classiﬁer in the package
This returns an object that conta ins a lot of information about the model. We
can inspect the admissible scien tiﬁc types for the model’ s input and target by
accessing i.input_scitype  and i.target_scitype , respectively .
For the DecisionTreeClassifier , we see that the input is acceptable as a
Table with columns of type Continuous , Count , or OrderedFactor . The
target is acceptable as a vector with element scitype <: Finite .
Knowing the admissible scient iﬁc types of a model’ s input and target is
important because it ensures that we provide data that the model can process.
Scitypes of data
In machine learning, it is essential to understand the nature of the data and
its type before modeling it. MLJ follows the convention described in
ScientiﬁcT ypes.jl  to interpret the elements of container types. According to
this convention, here are the key features:
AbstractFloat  is interpreted as Continuous.
Integer is interpreted as Count.
CategoricalValue  is interpreted as Multiclass or OrderedFactor ,
depending on the value of isordered(x) .
Strings and Chars are not interp reted as Multiclass or OrderedFactor ;
they have scitypes  Textual and Unknown, respectively .
Integers (includi ng Bools) cannot represent categorical data, so use the
preceding coerce operations to coerce to a Finite scitype .
The scientiﬁc types of nothing and missing are Nothing and Missing,
native types that we also regard as scientiﬁc.
You can use the coerce function to change the scientiﬁc type of an array or a
table. Here’ s an example:
Figure 1 1.3: Varying scitypes of tabular data in MLJ
This code coerces a DataFrame  X so that the name column is of type
Multiclass, the height column is of type Continuous, and the rating column
is of type OrderedFactor . The schema function is then used to retrieve the
scitypes of the columns of the new DataFrame.
The function scitype(X)  in ScientiﬁcT ypes.jl  returns the scientiﬁc type
(interpretation) of X, as distinct from its machine type. For example,
scitype(3.14)  returns Continuous , scitype([1, 2, missing])  returns
AbstractVector{Union{Missing, Count}} , and scitype((5, “beige”))
returns Tuple{Count, Textual} .
To convert a table X to a copy of X with element scientiﬁc types matching a
new speciﬁcation specs, you can use the coerce function. The specs
argument can be either one or more column_name=>Scitype  pairs, one or
more OldScitype=>NewScitype  pairs, or a dictionary of scientiﬁc types
keyed on column names. For example, coerce(X, :name=>Multiclass,
:height=>Continuous, :rating=>OrderedFactor)  converts a DataFrame
X to a new DataFrame Xc with column scientiﬁc types Multiclass ,
Continuous , and OrderedFactor .
Generating data
Generating Synthetic Data mean s creating fake data that looks similar to real
data, but we know it’s not real. We can use this fake data to test and try out
our machine learning models, without worrying about hurting anyone’ s
privacy or violating any laws.
MLJ has some functions called make_blobs , make_circles , make_moons ,
and make_regression  that can help us create these mock data sets. For
example, we can use make_blobs  to create cluste rs of points (like bubbles)
that we can use to test clustering algorithms.
make_blobs  function can create a table of data with a certain number of rows
and columns (features) and a corresponding vector of “target” labels that tell
us which blob (cluster) each data point belongs to. We can adjust the
properties of these blobs, like the number of clusters, their size and shape,
and whether they are shufﬂed or not.
Here’ s an example code using  make_blobs  to create two clusters with
different sizes:
julia> using MLJ, DataFrames
julia> X, y = make_blobs(100, 3; centers=2, cluster_std=[1.0,
3.0])
(Tables.MatrixTable{Matrix{Float64}} with 100 rows, 3 columns,
and schema:
:x1  Float64
:x2  Float64
:x3  Float64, CategoricalValue{Int64, UInt32}[1, 1, 2, 1, 1,
2, 2, 2, 1, 1  …  2, 2, 1, 2, 2, 1, 1, 1, 2, 1])
julia> dfBlobs = DataFrame(X)
100×3 DataFrame

│ Float64 Float64 Float64
─────┼────────────────────────────────
1 │  -1.99141   8.24332   -4.65529
2 │  -4.04924   7.55333   -2.42757
︙
julia> dfBlobs.y = y

julia> first(dfBlobs, 3)

3 │ -9.00246  3.44369   6.91574  2
This code generates a table with 100 rows and 3 columns (features) and two
clusters of data points. The clusters have different sizes, with the second one
being three times larger than the ﬁrst one. We use the DataFrames  package to
create a DataFrame  from the table of data, and add the target labels to it.
Finally , we display the ﬁrst three rows of the DataFrame  to see what the data
looks like.
We can also choose how many blobs we want, how spread out they are, and
other things like that. After we make the pretend dataset, we can use it to test
different types of computer programs and see how well they can understand
the blobs and make predictions about them.
Regression data
Let’s say you have some data, and you want to ﬁnd a relations hip between
them. For example, you might have the height and weight of a bunch of
people, and you want to know how height is related to weight.
Now , imagine that there is a relationship between height and weight that can
be described by a straight line. The make_regression  function can help you
generate some data that follows  this relationship, but with some  added noise
to make it more realistic.
Here’ s an example code:
julia> using MLJ, DataFrames
julia> X, y = make_regression(100, 5; noise=0.5, sparse=0.2,
outliers=0.1)
(Tables.MatrixTable{Matrix{Float64}} with 100 rows, 5 columns,
and schema:
:x1  Float64
:x2  Float64
:x3  Float64
:x4  Float64
:x5  Float64, [-0.854598521465274,  … 2.287388237748642])
julia> dfRegression = DataFrame(X)
100×5 DataFrame

                                              54 rows

0.0327883  -7.82129
In this example, we’re gener ating 100 observations with ﬁve features
(columns) and one continuous target variable (y). The noise parameter
determines the amount of noise we add to the data, making it more difﬁcult
to see the true relationship between the features and the target. The sparse
parameter determ ines the sparsity of the generating weight vector (that is,
how many of the features are important). The outliers paramete r determines
the proportion of the response vector to make as outliers by adding a random
quantity with high variance.
The output of the function is a tuple (X, y) where X is a table (or matrix)
with the generated features, and y is a vector of the generated target values.
We then convert the output into a DataFrame  for easier manipulation and
visualization.
Categorical data
Categorical data is a type of data that represents the characteristics or
qualities of an object, person, or group. It consists of a limite d number of
values or categories that describe the attribute being measured. Examples of
categorical data include gender , race, color , occupation, and education level.
Working with categorical data involves several steps to analyze, process, and
model the data. Here are some key considerations when working with
categorical data:
Data exploration : Begin by examining the distribution of the
categorical variables in your dataset. This can be done by creating
frequency tables or visualizations, such as bar charts or pie charts.
Data cleaning : Categorical data may contain missing values,
inconsistent values, or typos. You may need to impute missing  values
or correct errors to ensure the data is accurate and complete.
Data encoding : Categorical data needs to be transformed into a
numerical form to be used in machine learning models.
Handling categorical data
We implement categorical variables in the MLJ machine learning library in
Julia by using the `CategoricalArrays.jl ` package to handle categorical
values.
To create a categorical vector from raw labels, one can use the categorical
function. The resulting v variable is of type CategoricalVector{Char,
UInt32, Char, CategoricalValue{Char, UInt32}, Union{}}  and
represents a Multiclass vector with three levels.
When indexing a CategoricalVector , one gets a CategoricalValue
instance instead  of a raw label. The CategoricalValue  knows the complete
pool of levels from the vector from which it came.
To construct a UnivariateFinite  distribution, which is the type of
distribution returned by most probabilistic classiﬁers, one can use the
UnivariateFinite  constru ctor. The resulting d variable represents a
distribution over the levels “no” and “yes” with probabili ties 0.9 and 0.1,
respectively . The pool argument is used to specify the set of levels that the
distribution can take. To create a vector of UnivariateFinite  distributions,
one can use the UnivariateFiniteVector  constructor .
Figure 1 1.4: Categorical values of varying mathematical and Boolean values
The resulting d_vec  variable is a vector of ﬁve UnivariateFinite
distributions, each with levels “no” and “yes” and probabilities speciﬁed by
the probs matrix.
Correct data scientiﬁc type for categorical data
In machine learning, it is impor tant to ensure that your data is in the correct
format for the algorithms you plan to use. MLJ uses scientiﬁc types to
articulate the data requirements  of models. There are three scientiﬁc types
for discrete data: Count , OrderedFactor , and Multiclass .
Count data is used for data that represents a count or frequency of
something. This type of data is generally unbounded, such as the number of
phone calls or the population of a city. In MLJ, Count is represented as an
AbstractVector{Count} .
OrderedFactor  is used for data that has a natural ordering, such as the
number of legs on an animal or the number of rooms in a house. This type of
data is ﬁnite and can be coerc ed to an ordered set of integers. In MLJ,
OrderedFactor  is represented as an AbstractVector{OrderedFactor} .
Multiclass is used for unordered categorical data, such as the color of a car
or the species of an animal. This type of data is also ﬁnite and can be
coerced to a set of integers. In MLJ, Multiclass is represented as an
`AbstractVector{Multiclass} `.
Binary data, which is data that can take only two values (for example,
true/false, yes/no), can be represented as either OrderedFactor{2}  if the
data has a natura l ordering or Multiclass{2} if the data is unordered. In MLJ,
Binary data is represented as an `AbstractVector{OrderedFactor} ` or
`AbstractVector{Multiclass} `, depending on whether the data is ordered
or unordered.
Changing the incorrect scitype of categorical data
First, we inspec t the scientiﬁc type of data using the scitype  function . To
inspect all column scientiﬁc types in a table simultaneously , the schema
function can be used.
Then, to coerce  a single colum n, the coerce function is used. The machine
type of the result is a `CategoricalArray `. To inspect the order of the
levels, the levels function can be used. If needed, the `levels!() ` functio n
can be used to change the order of the levels.
Lastly , to coerce all remaining types simultaneously , the coerc e function is
used with the desired types speciﬁed for each column. In this example, the
columns gender , likes_soup , height, and rating are coerced to
`Multiclass `, `OrderedFactor `, `Continuous `, and `OrderedFactor `,
respectively . In-place coercion can also be done using ` coerce!() `.
Suppose you have a dataset that contains information about the passengers
on the Titanic, and one of the columns is “Embarked ” which contains the port
of emba rkation for each passenger (either “S”, “C”, or “Q”). How ever, upon
inspecting the column with describe()  function, you notice that it’s being
treated as a string, rather than a categorical variable.
Figure 1 1.5: Varying ways to see a dataframe object
To properly represent this data as a categorical variable, you can use the
CategoricalArrays  package and the coerce()  function. Now , the
“Embarked ” column is properly represented as a categorical variable.
Tracking all various categories
Imagine you have a toy box with different types of toys, such as balls, cars,
and dolls. Let’s say you want to pick only the red toys from the box. You
don’t want to lose track of the other types of toys in the box, so you separate
the red toys while keeping the rest in the box.
In the same way, when we have data with different categories, such as
different colors or types of toys, we want to keep track of all the categories,
even if we only use some of them for a speciﬁc analysis. We do this by using
a special type of code called “OrderedFactor ” or “Multiclass ”, which
allows us to track all the categories even when we only use some of them.
Figure 1 1.6: Non-mathematical categorical values
Suppose you have a dataset containing information about cars, including the
make, model, and the number of cylinders in the engine. The number of
cylinders is a categorical variable since it can only take a small number of
discrete values (for example, 4, 6, or 8).
The cylinders column has been represented as integers, which is a common
mistake when working with categorical data. To properly represent this
variable, we can use the CategoricalArrays  package. Now , we can inspect
the levels of this variable. This returns an array of the possible values that
the cylinders variable can take.
We can also access individual rows or subsets of the data, while preserving
the information about the possible levels of the categorical variable. By
properly representing categorical data in this way, we can ensure that our
models are corre ctly handling the information and that we are not losing any
important information about the variable.
Preparing data
Preparing data is a crucial step in any data analysis or mach ine learning
project. It involves transforming raw data into a format that is suitable for
analysis, modeling, and decisio n-making. Proper data prepara tion ensures
that the data is accurate, consistent, complete, and in a structur e that can be
easily utilized by the algorithms or methods being applied.
Splitting data
There are two tools in MLJ for splitting data: partition and unpack.
partition is used for vertically splitting data (that is, splitting by
observations), while unpack is used for horizontally splitting data (that is,
breaking up a table based on feature names).
Using partition:
julia> using MLJBase
julia> # split multiple objects “synchronously”
(Xtrain, Xtest), (ytrain, ytest) = partition((X, y), 0.8,
rng=123, multi=true)
(((x1 = [6.546574333729479,  …, 6.402576222222763])),
(CategoricalArrays.CategoricalValue{Int64, UInt32}[3, 1,  …,
3], CategoricalArrays.CategoricalValue{Int64, UInt32}[1, …,
2]))
In the example, partition is used to split multiple objects “synchronously” ,
that is, X and y are split at the same indices, with 80% of the data in Xtrain
and ytrain , and 20% of the data in Xtest  and ytest .
Using unpack:
The unpack function is used to split the DataFrame into a dependent variable
y and a set of independent variables X. The ﬁrst argument to unpack is the
DataFrame to split, and the second argument is a predicate that selects the
dependent variable. In this case, ==(:Species)  selects the Species column,
which contains the names of the different types of Iris ﬂowers. The third
argument is a predicate that selects all other columns by returning true for
each column. In other words, colname -> true  selects all columns in the
DataFrame except for the Species column.
Figure 1 1.7: Using `unpack` with datasets
Finally , the pretty function is used to print the ﬁrst three rows of the X
DataFrame, which contains the measurements of the four attributes for each
ﬂower . The output shows the ﬁrst three rows of the X DataFrame, which has
four columns (SepalLength , SepalWidth , PetalLength , and PetalWidth )
and three rows. The Continuous annotation for each column indicates that
the data type is a continuous variable.
Note that partiti on has options like shufﬂe for shufﬂing the rows, rng for
specifying the random numbe r generator , and stratify for matching the
stratiﬁcation of a given vector. Similarly , unpack has options like
wrap_singles  for wrapping  a single-column table as a vector , and
coerce_options  for specifying options for data type conversion.
Addr essing the mismatched scitype of data-model
However , somet imes the function call fails because the input data has mixed
scientiﬁc types.  Speciﬁcally , height is a count variable, while time is
continuous, mark is textual, and admitted is multiclass. Most supervised
learning models  require input features to be of a single continuous type.
Therefore, we need to preprocess the data.
MLJ of fers two tools to address data-model type mismatches, including:
Scientiﬁc type coercion: This involves converting the data to the
intended scientiﬁc interpretation. For example, if height is supposed to
be conti nuous, mark is an ordered factor , and admitted is a binary
multiclass, we can use the coerce function to convert the data to the
intended scientiﬁc type.
Featur e engineering : This involves transforming the data by creating
new features, selecting relevant features, or reducin g the
dimensionality of the data.
We will learn about searching and loading models in MLJ. Then we will
revisit other transformation functions used for preparing data in MLJ.
Weights
In machine learning, weights can be used to assign more importance to
certain samples or classes during model training and evaluation. In the MLJ
(Machine Learning in Julia) framework, weights can be speciﬁed in both
training and performance evaluation. The two types of weights are:
per observation weights : weight vectors with the same length as the
number of observations
class weights : diction aries that are keyed on the target classes (levels)
used in classiﬁcation problems
To specify weights in training, we need to bind the weights to the model
along with the data when constructing a machine. To specify weights in
performance evaluation, we need to provide the weights as the last argument
when calling an MLJ measure (metric) that supports weights.
Models
In machine learning, a model is a mathematical representation of a real-
world system or process. The model takes inputs, processes them using
mathematical operations, and produces an output that can be used to make
predictions or decisions.
In super vised learning, a model is trained on a labeled dataset, where the
input data and the corresponding output or label are known . The model
learns to map inputs to outputs  by minimizing the difference between its
predictions and the true outpu ts in the training data. Once the model is
trained, it can be used to make predictions on new , unseen data.
In unsup ervised learning, the model is trained on an unlabeled dataset, where
only the input data is known. The goal is to ﬁnd patterns or structure in the
data without any prior knowledge of what the output should be.
Models in machine learning can take many forms, such as decision trees,
neural networks, linear regression, support vector machines, and clustering
algorithms, to name a few. The choice of model depends on the speciﬁc task
and the characteristics of the data being used.
Supervised Learning Models :
Linear  Regr ession : Linear regression is a type of regression model
used for predict ing a continuous output variable based on one or more
input features. It works by ﬁttin g a linear equation to the training data,
which can then be used to make predictions on new data.
Logistic Regr ession : Logistic regression is a type of classiﬁcation
model used for predicting a binary output variable (0 or 1) based on
one or more input features. It works by ﬁtting a logistic function to the
training data, which can then be used to predict the probability of a
given input belonging to the positive class.
Decision Trees: Decision trees are a type of model used for both
classiﬁcation and regression tasks. They work by recursively
partitioning the input space into smaller subsets based on the values of
the input features, and making decisions based on these partitions.
Random Forests: Rando m forests are an extension of decision trees
that use an ensemble of decision trees to make predictions. They work
by randomly sampling subsets  of the input data and features, and
building decisio n trees on each subset. The ﬁnal prediction is then
made by aggregating the predictions of all the individual decision trees.
Support Vector  Machines (SVMs): SVMs  are a type of model used
for classiﬁcatio n and regression tasks. They work by ﬁnding the
hyperplane that best separates the input data into different classes or
predicts the output variable in a regression problem.
Unsupervised Learning Models:
K-Means Clustering : K-means clustering is a type of clustering
algorithm used for partitioning a dataset into K distinct clusters based
on their similari ties. It works by iteratively updating the cluster centers
until the clusters are well separated.
Principal Component Analysis (PCA) : PCA  is a type of
dimensionality reduction technique used for ﬁnding the underlying
structure in high-dimensional data. It works by identifying the principal
components of the data that explain the maximum amount of variance.
Autoencoders : Autoen coders are a type of neural network used for
unsupervised learning. They work by learning to encode and decode
input data, whic h can be used for tasks such as anomaly detection and
image generation.
Gaussian Mixtur e Models (GMMs) : GMMs are a type of
probabilistic model used for clustering and density estimation tasks.
They work by modeling the input data as a mixture of Gaussian
distributions, and using the maximum likelihood method to estimate
the parameters of the model.
t-SNE : t-SNE is a type of dimensionality reduction technique used for
visualizing high-dimensional data in a low-dimensional space. It works
by modeling the similarity between data points in the high-dimensional
space and the low-dimensional space, and minimizing the difference
between the two.
Model search
In machine learning, a model is a mathematical representation of a system or
process that generates predictions or decisions based on input data.
In MLJ, a mode l registry is available to search models and their properties
efﬁciently without loading all the packages containing model codes. This
helps to ﬁnd all models that can solve a speciﬁc machine learning task. The
task is deﬁned using the matching method and the search is executed with
the models method.
To obtain information about a model, the info()  command can be used.
This returns a named tuple containing metadata, such as the name of the
model, the pack age it is from, whether it is supervised or unsupervised, the
input and output types it expects, the implemented methods, the
hyperparameters and their ranges, and other properties such as whether it
supports class weights, online learning, or training losses. You can ﬁnd all
the MLJ models at https://alan-turing-
institute.github.io/MLJ.jl/dev/list_of_supported_models/#model_list . For
example, the following code retrieves information about the DBSCAN
model from the Clustering pack age. You need to specify the package name
as the DBSCAN model is also in MLJScikitLearnInterface .
Figure 1 1.8: Retrieves information about the DBSCAN model fr om the Clustering package
The doc()  function can be used to retrieve the model’ s documentation string
without importing the code:
julia> doc(“RandomForestClassifier”, pkg=”DecisionTree”)
RandomForestClassifier
A model type for constructing a CART random forest
classifier, based on
DecisionTree.jl
(https://github.com/bensadeghi/DecisionTree.jl), and
implementing the MLJ model interface.
Model queries
This is used in the MLJ machine learning package to list and query available
models.
Here is a brief explanation of the code:
The models()  function lists all available mode ls in the MLJ package. It
returns a vector of named tuples, where each tuple represents a
machine learning model.
The localmodels()  function lists only the models for which the code
is already loaded. It returns the same output format as models() .
The named tuple returned by each function contains information about
the model, such as its name, package name, abstract type,
hyperparameters, and implemented methods.
The models()  function can be used to search for models containing
speciﬁed strings or regular expressions in their docstring attribute.
Here’ s a sample  code snippet showing how you can use these  functions to
query models:
julia> # List all available models
all_models = models()
208-element Vector{NamedTuple{(:name, …, :output_scitype)}}:
(name = ABODDetector, package_name = OutlierDetectionNeighbors,
… )
︙
(name = XGBoostRegressor, package_name = XGBoost, … )
julia> # List only the models for which code is already loaded
loaded_models = localmodels()
15-element Vector{NamedTuple{(:name, …, :output_scitype)}}:
(name = ConstantClassifier, package_name = MLJModels, … )
︙
(name = UnivariateTimeTypeToContinuous, package_name =
MLJModels, … )
This code ﬁrst imports the MLJ package and then uses the models()  and
localmodels()  functions to list all available models and the models for
which code is already loaded, respectively .
Models matching
In the context of the MLJ (Mach ine Learning in Julia) package, the matching
command is used to check whether a given model is compatible with input
data and target variables of certain scientiﬁc types. Speciﬁcally ,
matching(model, X, y) returns true if the model is supervised and can
accept inputs of type X and targets of type y, and matching(model, X)
returns true if the model is unsupervised and can accept inputs of type X.
To search for all supervised probabilistic models that can handle input X and
target y, one can deﬁne a testing function task that checks for model
compatibility and probabilistic prediction, as follows:
task(model) = matching(model, X, y) && model.prediction_type ==
:probabilistic
This function returns true if the model can handle inputs of type X and
targets of type y, and if it makes probabilistic predictions. Then, the search
for such models can be executed using the models function:
models(task)
The models function lists all models in the MLJ registry that satisfy a given
ﬁlter. By default, calling models() returns a list of all models in the registry ,
while calling models(filters..)  returns a list of models that satisfy all
given ﬁlters.
For example, to search for all models that are compatible with input X and
target y , one can execute:
models(matching(X, y))
This returns a list of all supervised models that can handle inputs of type X
and tar gets of type y .
Alternatively , one can use the models function with a do block and a
customized ﬁlter to achieve the same result:
models() do model
matching(model, X, y) &&
model.prediction_type == :probabilistic
end
The localmodels  function is similar to models, but it only lists models that
are currently available to the user from a given module (by default, the Main
module). The localmodels  functio n can also take one or more ﬁlters to
narrow down the list of models.
To load a speciﬁ c model from the list returned by localmodels , one can use
the load_path  function, as in the following example:
ms = localmodels()
model = ms[1]
load_path(model)
Here, ms is a list of all models available in the current module, model is the
ﬁrst model in the list, and load_path(model)  returns the path to the ﬁle
deﬁning the model.
Loading models
The process of loading a model in MLJ involves identifying the name of the
model and the package that provides the MLJ interface for that model. Once
these are identiﬁed, you can use the @load  macro to load the model type into
your current module, package, or function.
To use @load , you need to have the package providing the MLJ interface
installed in your environment. You can determine the package by calling
load_path(model_name, pkg=”algorithm-providing package name”) ,
where model_name  is the name of the model you want to use, and pkg is the
name of the package providing the algorithm for that model. This function
returns the load path for the model type. If the MLJ interface package is not
already installed in your environment, you can add it using
Pkg.add(“MLJAlgorithmInterface”) .
Once you have the MLJ interface package installed, you can use @load  to
load the model type. For example, if you want to use the
DecisionTreeClassifier  model from the DecisionT ree.jl package, you can
call @load  DecisionTreeClassifier  pkg=DecisionTree . This will load the
DecisionTreeClassifier  model type into your current module, package, or
function, and you can create an instance of the model using Tree() .
Figure 1 1.9: An example of loading the DecisionT reeClassiﬁer model using @load
Alternatively , you can use the @iload  macro for interactive loading.  This
provides the user with options to install the required interface package and
choose the relevant model-providing package in ambiguous cases.
Here’ s an example of loading the DecisionTreeClassifier  model using
@iload :
Figure 1 1.10:  Basic information about Decision T ree Classiﬁer
Note that for models that are provided by only a single pack age, the pkg
speciﬁcation can be dropped.
Let us revisit the topic on weights. The MLJ package provides a way to
specify weights during both model training and performance evaluation.
To speci fy weig hts during model training, you can bind the weights to the
model along with the data when constructing a machine. For example, to
specify weights for a KNNRegressor  model:
Figure 1 1.11: KNN r egressor model is used and ﬁt (trained on) random dat.
Note that the model needs to support per observation weight s for this to
work, which can be checked using supports_weights(model) .
Revisiting weights
To specify weights during performance evaluation, you can provide the
weights as the last argument when calling an MLJ measure (metric) that
supports weights. For example, to compute the macro F1 score with weights
for a classiﬁcation problem:
julia> _, y = @load_iris
((sepal_length = [5.1,  …, 5.9], sepal_width = [3.5,  …, 3.0],
petal_length = [1.4,  …, 5.1], petal_width = [0.2,  …  1.8]),
CategoricalArrays.CategoricalValue{String, UInt32}[“setosa”,  …
“virginica”])
julia> ŷ = shuffle(y)
150-element

“setosa”
︙

3)
Dict{String, Int64} with 3 entries:
“virginica”  => 3
“versicolor” => 1
“setosa”  => 2

You can use supports_weights  and supports_class_weights  on measures
to check  weight support. For example, to list all measures supporting per

julia> measures() do m

end
