Data preprocessing using transformers in MLJ
Let us revisit other data preprocessing techniques. We use Transformers in
MLJ for loading and using various data preprocessing techniques. In MLJ,
several unsupervised models are available out-of-the-box, including
transformers that perform common transformations such as one-hot
encoding. These models can be used to preprocess data before it is fed into a
supervised model.
A transformer is considered “static ” if it has no learned parameters. Such a
transformer is essentially an ordinary function but can be realize d as an MLJ
static transforme r by deﬁning it as a subtype of Static <: Unsupervised .
This can be useful, especially if the function depends on parameters that the
user would like to manipulate (which become hyperparam eters of the
model). Here is an example of deﬁning a static transformer:
using MLJ
struct MyTransformer <: Static{Tuple{}}
end
function MLJ.transform(::MyTransformer, X::DataFrame)
# do some transformation on X here
return transformed_X
end
You can learn more about built-i n and static transformers in MLJModels.jl at
https://alan-turing-institute.github.io/MLJ.jl/dev/transformers/#Built-in-
transformers  .
Conclusion
MLJ.jl is a powerful machine learning library in Julia that offers a variety of
functionalities for building, training, and evaluating machine learning
models. It offers a user-friendly  and efﬁcient API that enables users to work
with a diverse range of models and datasets. MLJ.jl’ s support for data
preprocessing through transfo rmers, generation of synthetic data, and
ﬂexible model handling makes it a go-to tool for machine learning tasks in
Julia. Additiona lly, its ability to handle supervised, unsuperv ised, and its
integration with other Julia packages make it a valuable asset  to the Julia
community . Overall, MLJ.jl provides an efﬁcient and ﬂexible platform for
machine learnin g, making it a valuable addition to the toolkit  of any data
scientist or machine learning practitioner .
In the next chap ter, we will learn about model tuning, model performance,
and modeling pipelines and various other transformers.

CHAPTER 12
Data and Models
Introduction
MLJ.jl is a machine learning framework for Julia that provide s a common
interface for working with data and models. It provides a wide range of
features for data preparation, model selection, tuning, evaluation, and
composition. MLJ.jl supports a variety of data formats, including CSV,
JSON, and HDF 5. It also provi des a number of tools for data preparation,
such as imputation, normalizati on, and feature selection. MLJ.jl supports a
wide range of machine learning models, including linear regres sion, logistic
regression, decision trees, and neural networks. It also provides several tools
for model selection, tuning, and evaluation.
MLJ.jl is an advanced framewo rk for machine learning that offers a diverse
set of capabilities to tackle a wide array of problems. Its user-friendly
interface and rich feature set make it an indispensable resource for machine
learning practitioners. Supporting various data formats like CSV, JSON, and
HDF5, MLJ.jl facilitates seamless data integration. Additionally , it equips
users with essential tools for data preprocessing, encompassing tasks such as
imputation, normalization, and feature selection.
When it comes to model selection, MLJ.jl boasts extensive support for an
extensive range of machine learning models, including linear regression,
logistic regressio n, decision trees, and neural networks. It further provides an
assortment of utilities to aid users in model evaluation, tuning, and selection.
Before initiatin g the training process, it is vital to prepare the data
meticulously . This typically involves data cleansing, outlier removal, and
data transformation into a format compatible with the chosen model. MLJ.jl
features a variety of tools that simplify the data preparation phase.
After preparing the data, the next step involves model selection. Given the
multitude of models available, the optimal choice depends on the unique
characteristics of the dataset. MLJ.jl furnishes several tools to facilitate the
selection of the most suitable model for the problem at hand. Once a model
is select ed, the subsequent task entails parameter tuning, aiming to identify
the parameter values that yield  the best performance on the given dataset.
MLJ.jl encompasses a range of tools designed to assist users in this crucial
tuning process.
Following parameter tuning, evaluating the model’ s performance on a
separate test set is crucial for assessing its generalization capabilities. This
evaluation aids in determining how effectively the model will handle new,
unseen data. MLJ.jl provides a comprehensive set of tools to facilitate
performance evaluation. Moreo ver, MLJ.jl supports model composition,
allowing users to combine multiple models into a composite model that
surpasses the capabilities of the individual models. MLJ.jl incorporates
numerous tools to simplify the process of model composition.

Model interface in MLJ.jl
A model is a container for the parameters of a machine learning algorithm.
The parameters are the values that the algorithm learns from  the data to
make predictions. The model  also stores the hyperparameters of the
algorithm, which are the settings that control how the algorithm learns.
The name of the Julia type associated with a model indicates the associated
algorithm. For example, the type DecisionTreeClassifier  indicates the
decision tree classiﬁer algorithm. The outcome of training a learning
algorithm is called a fitresult . A fitresult  contain s the information that
the algorithm learned during training. For example, the coefﬁcients and
intercept of a linear regression model are stored  in the ﬁtresult.  The ultimate
supertype of all models in MLJ.jl is MLJModelInterface.Model . This type
has two abstract subtypes: Supervised and Unsupervised.
Supervised models are used to predict a target variable from a set of features.
For example, a supervised model could be used to predict the price of a
house based on its features, such as its size, location, and number of
bedrooms.
Unsupervised models are used to ﬁnd patterns in data. For example, an
unsupervised model could be used to ﬁnd groups of customers who are
similar to each other .
Supervised models can be further divided into two types: probabilistic and
deterministic. Probabilistic models can predict the probability of a new
observation belonging to a particular class. Deterministic models can only
predict the class of a new observation. The division of model types is further
realized through Trait declarati ons. Traits are a way of grouping together
related types. For example, the Probabilistic trait is used to group together all
of the probabilistic models in MLJ.jl.
Every concrete subtype of Model must have a ﬁt method. The ﬁt method is
used to train the model on a set of data.
Every supervised model must have a predict method. The predict method is
used to predict the tar get variable for a new observation.
Every unsupervised model must have a transform method. The transform
method is used to transform the data into a new representation.
Methods such as ﬁt, predict, and transform are called operations. Operations
are dispatched on a model instance and a fitresult  (plus other data).
Probabilistic supervised mode ls optionally implement a predict_mode
operation (in the case of classiﬁers) or a predict_mean  and/or
predict_median  operations (in the case of regressors). These operations are
used to predict the mode (most likely class) or the mean (expected value) of
the tar get variable for a new observation.
Unsupervised models may implement an inverse_transform  operation.
This operation is used to transform the data back to its original
representation.
Model representation
In MLJ. jl, mode ls are represented by structs. To create a new model, you
must ﬁrst declar e a struct for the model. The struct declaration must include
the name  of the model, the type of the model, and the hyperpara meters of the
model.
The following is an example of a struct declaration for a simple model:
struct MyModel <: MLJ.Deterministic
lambda::Float64
end
The name of the model is MyModel . The type of the model is
MLJ.Deterministic . The hyperparameter of the model is lambda.
Once you have declared a struct  for the model, you can create a new instance
of the model using the keyword constructor . The keyword constructor takes
the hype rparameters of the model as arguments. The following is an example
of how to create a new instance of the MyModel  model:
my_model = MyModel(lambda=0.5)
The my_model  variable now contains an instance of the MyModel  model. The
lambda hyperparameter of the model is set to 0.5.
The clean!  method is a method that is used to clean the hyperparameters of
the model. The clean!  method can be used to check the hyperparameters for
errors and to correct any errors that are found. The following is an example
of the clean!  method for the MyModel  model:
function MyModel.clean!(model::MyModel)
if model.lambda < 0
warning = “Need lambda ≥ 0. Resetting lambda=0.”
model.lambda = 0
end
end
The clean!  method  checks the lambda hyperparameter to make sure that it
is greater than or equal to 0. If the lambda hyperparameter is less than 0,
then clean!()  method sets the lambda hyperparameter to 0.
The @mlj_model  macro is a macr o that can be used to simplify the process of
declaring a model struct. The @mlj_model  macro takes the name of the
model, the type of the model, and the hyperparameters of the model as
arguments. The following is an example of how to use the @mlj_model
macro:
@mlj_model mutable struct MyModel <: MLJ.Deterministic
lambda::Float64 = 0.5
end
The @mlj_model  macro is equivalent to the following struct declaration:
struct MyModel <: MLJ.Deterministic
lambda::Float64
end
function MyModel.clean!(model::MyModel)
if model.lambda < 0
warning = “Need lambda ≥ 0. Resetting lambda=0.”
model.lambda = 0
end
end
Supervised model
Supervised models utilize features in training data to predict a target
variable, which represents the value the model aims to predict.  The training
process involves learning the relationship between features and the target
variable. There are many different types of supervised models, each with its
own strengths and weaknesses. Some common types of supervised models
include:
Linear  regression models : Linear regression models are used to make
predictions for data that is linearly related. Linear regression models
are simp le to understand and implement. However , they are not always
accurate for data that is not linearly related.
Logistic regression models : Logistic regression models are used to
make predictions for data that is binary . Logistic regression models are
more complex than linear regression models, but they are more
accurate for data that is binary .
Decision tree models : Decision tree models are used to make
predictions for data that is non-linear . Decision tree models are more
complex than linear regression models or logistic regression models,
but they can be very accurate for data that is non-linear .
Supervised models can be used to solve a variety of problems, including:
Predicting the price of a stock
Predicting the likelihood of a customer clicking an ad
Predicting the likelihood of a patient developing a disease
Supervised models are a powerful tool that can be used to make predictions
for a variety of problems. If you are working on a problem that involves
making predictions, you should consider using a supervised model.
Here are some additional details about the supervised models in MLJ.jl:
The MLJ.jl library provides a number of supervised models, including
linear regression models, logistic regression models, and decision tree
models.
The MLJ.jl library also provides several tools for training and
evaluating supervised models.
The MLJ.jl library is open source and can be downloaded from the
MLJ.jl website.
User-deﬁned models in MLJ
To implement a new supervised model in MLJ, you need to follow three
steps:
1. Deﬁne a mutable struct to store hyperparameters. This struct is the
model, and it should be a subtype of either Probabili stic or
Deterministic, depending on whether probabilistic or ordinary point
predictions are intended.
2. Deﬁne a ﬁt method, dispatched on the model, which returns learned
parameters (fitresult ). The ﬁt metho d is used to learn the model’ s
parameters on the training data.
3. Deﬁne a predict method, dispat ched on the model and the fitresult ,
to return  predictions on new patterns. The predict method uses the
learned parameters to make predictions on new data.
julia> import MLJBase
julia> using LinearAlgebra
julia> mutable struct MyRegressor <: MLJBase.Deterministic
lambda::Float64
end
MyRegressor(; lambda=0.1) = MyRegressor(lambda)
# fit returns coefficients minimizing a penalized rms loss
function:
julia> function MLJBase.fit(model::MyRegressor, verbosity, X,
y)
x = MLJBase.matrix(X) # convert table to matrix
fitresult = (x’x + model.lambda*I)\(x’y) # the coefficients
cache=nothing
report=nothing
return fitresult, cache, report
end
# predict uses coefficients to make a new prediction:
julia> MLJBase.predict(::MyRegressor, fitresult, Xnew) =
MLJBase.matrix(Xnew) * fitresult
This code sample provides an implementation of a simple deterministic
regressor , speciﬁ cally a ridge regressor with no intercept, using the Julia
programming language and the MLJBase  package.
Deterministic r egressor
In MLJ.jl, a deterministic regres sor is a model that can be used to predict a
target variable from a set of features, and that always returns the same
predicted value for a given set of features. This means that a deterministic
regressor does not provide any information about the uncertainty in the
predictions.
Deterministic regressors are often used in machine learning applications
where it is important to be able to make accurate predictions. For example,
deterministic regressors can be used to predict the price of a stock, the
demand for a product, or the likelihood of a customer clicking an ad.
There are many different types of deterministic regressors, and the choice of
which regressor to use depends on the speciﬁc machine learning problem
that is being solved. Some common types of deterministic regressors
include:
Linear regression models
Ridge regression models
Lasso regression models
Support vector machines (SVMs)
Decision trees
Random forests
MLJ.jl provides  a number of deterministic regressors, and it also provides a
number of tools for working with deterministic regressors. For example,
MLJ.jl provides functions for calculating the mean squared error , the root
mean squared error , and the R-squared of deterministic regressors, and it
also provides functions for visualizing the predictions of deterministic
regressors.
First, a mutable struct called MyRegressor  is deﬁn ed, whic h implements the
MLJBase.Deterministic  abstract type. This struct takes one parameter ,
lambda, which is a scalar value representing the regularization strength. The
MyRegressor  struct is also initialized with a constructor that takes a named
parameter lambda with a default value of 0.1.
The ﬁt function is then deﬁne d, which takes as input the MyRegressor
model, a verbosity ﬂag, a matrix  of input features X, and a vector of output
values y. The function ﬁrst converts the input data from a table to a matrix
using the MLJBase.matrix  function. It then computes the coefﬁcients using
ridge regression, which minimizes a penalized root-mean-square (RMS) loss
function. The resulting coefﬁcie nts are returned along with cache and report
variables, which are set to nothing.
Finally , the predict function is deﬁned, which takes as input the MyRegressor
model, the coefﬁcients obtained  from the ﬁt function, and a new matrix of
input features, Xnew . The function multiplies the input matrix Xnew  by the
coefﬁcients to make a prediction.
julia> X, y = @load_boston;
julia> model = MyRegressor(lambda=1.0)
julia> regressor = machine(model, X, y)
julia> evaluate!(regressor, resampling=CV(), measure=rms,
verbosity=0)
Once this code is loaded, the MyRegressor  can be used to train and evaluate
a model using the Boston housing dataset. A new instance of the
MyRegressor  model is created with a regula rization strength of 1.0, and a
machine object is created using the model, input features X, and output
values y. The evaluate! function  is then used to train and evaluate the model
using cross-validation with root-mean-square (RMS) error as the
performance metric. The resulting performance evaluation object shows the
RMS error for each fold of the cross-validation.
Probabilistic r egressor
In MLJ. jl, a probabilistic classi ﬁer is a model that can be used to predict a
target variable from a set of features, and that also provides a probability
distribution for the predicted value. This means that a probabilistic classiﬁer
cannot only tell you what the most likely class label is for a new pattern, but
it can also tell you how likely it is that the new pattern belongs to each class.
Probabilistic classiﬁers are often used in machine learning applications
where it is important to be able to quantify the uncertainty in the predictions.
For example, probabilistic classiﬁers can be used to make decisions about
whether to approve a loan, whet her to admit a student to a schoo l, or whether
to diagnose a patient with a disease.
There are many different types of probabilistic classiﬁers, and the choice of
which classiﬁer to use depends on the speciﬁc machine learning problem
that is being solved. Some common types of probabilistic classiﬁers include:
Gaussian naive Bayes classiﬁers
Logistic regression classiﬁers
Support vector machines (SVMs)
Decision trees
Random forests
MLJ.jl provides a number of probabilistic classiﬁers as well as several tools
for working with probabilistic classiﬁers. For example, MLJ.jl provides
functions for calculating the accuracy , precision, and recall of probabilistic
classiﬁers, and it also provides functions for visualizing the probability
distributions of probabilistic classiﬁers.
The classiﬁer is called MyClassifier , and it is a subclass of
MLJBase.Probabilistic . This means that it is a probabilistic model that can
be used to predict a tar get variable from a set of features.
The ﬁt function for MyClassifier  ignores the inputs X and returns the
training target y probability distribution. This distribution is a
Distributions.UnivariateFinite  object, which is a probability
distribution for a single variable.
The predict function for MyClassifier  returns the passed fitresult  (pdf)
for all new patterns. This means that it returns the probability distribution for
each new pattern, based on the training tar get distribution.
Here is an example of how to use the MyClassifier  classiﬁer:
julia> using MLJBase
julia> using Distributions
julia> struct MyClassifier <: MLJBase.Probabilistic
end
julia> function MLJBase.fit(model::MyClassifier, verbosity, X,
y)
fitresult = Distributions.fit(MLJBase.UnivariateFinite,
y)
cache = nothing
report = nothing
return fitresult, cache, report
end
julia> MLJBase.predict(model::MyClassifier, fitresult, Xnew) =
[fitresult for i in 1:nrows(Xnew)]
julia> X, y = @load_iris
julia> mach = fit!(machine(MyClassifier(), X, y)
julia> predict(mach, selectrows(X, 1:2))
Figure 12.1:  Classiﬁcation example using MLJ functionalities
In this example,  we load the Iris dataset and ﬁt the MyClassifier  model to
the training target y. We then use the predict function to predict the
probability distribution for two new patterns. The output of the predict
function is a two-element array , where each elem ent is a
Distributions.UnivariateFinite  object. These objects represent the
probability distributions for the two new patterns.
The MyClassifier  classiﬁer is a simple probabilistic classiﬁer that can be
used to predict a target variable from a set of features. It is easy to use and it
can be used to solve a variety of machine learning problems.
Here is a more detailed explanation of the MyClassifier  classiﬁer:
The MyClassifier  classiﬁer is a probabilistic classiﬁer because it
returns a probability distribution for the predicted value. This means
that the classiﬁer can not only tell you what the most likely class label
is for a new pattern, but it can also tell you how likely it is that the new
pattern belongs to each class.
The MyClassifier  classiﬁer is simple because it only ﬁts a single
probability distribution to the training target. This makes the classiﬁer
easy to understand and implement.
The MyClassifier  classiﬁe r is versatile because it can be used to solve a
variety of machine learning problems. For example, the classiﬁer can be
used to classify images, to classify text, or to classify other types of data.
The MyClassifier  classiﬁer is a simple and versatile probabilistic classiﬁer
that can be used to solve a variety of machine learning problems.
There are a variety of other methods that are supported by MLJ. You can
refer to https://alan-turing-
institute.github.io/MLJ.jl/dev/list_of_supported_models/#model_list  for
using other models.
Fitting a model
When you are training or predicting using a model in MLJ.jl, you need to
provide the model with data. The data that you provide to the model needs to
be in a speciﬁc format. The format of the data that you provide to the model
is determined by the scientiﬁc type of the data.
`ﬁt` is a method that is used to train a machine learning model. It takes three
arguments: the model, the verbo sity level, and the training data. The model
is the object that will be trained, the verbosity level controls how much
information is printed to the console, and the training data is the data that
will be used to train the model.
The ﬁt method works by ﬁrst ﬁtting the model to the trainin g data. This
means that the model will learn the relationship between the features and the
target variable. Once the model has been ﬁtted, it can be used to make
predictions on new data.
The ﬁt method returns three objects, which are as follows:
The fitresult  object contains information about the training process,
such as the loss function, the number of iterations, and the time it took
to train the model.
The cache object contains any data that was used during the training
process, such as the model coefﬁcients and the feature importances.
The report object contains any information about the perform ance of
the model, such as the accuracy , the precision, and the recall.
The ﬁt method is an important  part of the machine learning process. It is
used to train the model so that it can be used to make predictions on new
data.
Predict method
The predict method is a method that is used to make predictions on new
data. It takes three arguments: the model, the fitresult , and the new data.
The model is the object that was trained, the fitresult  contains information
about the training process, and the new data is the data that will be used to
make predictions.
The predict method works by ﬁrst predicting the target varia ble for each
observation in the new data. It then returns a vector of predictions. The
predictions can be deterministic or probabilistic.
In the case of deterministic predictions, the predictions are simply the
predicted values for the target variable. In the case of probabilistic
predictions, the predictions are a distribution over the possible values of the
target variable.
The predict method is an important part of the machine learning process. It is
used to make predictions on new data, which can then be used to make
decisions.
Here is an example of how the predict method might be used to make
predictions on new data:
We have  a model that predicts the price of houses. The model was
trained on a dataset of houses that have already been sold.
We have a new house that we want to sell. We can use the predict
method to predict the price of the house.
The predict method will return a prediction for the price of the house.
We can then use this prediction  to decide how much to sell the house
for.
In this example, the predict method is used to decide how much to sell a
house for. Howe ver, the predict method can be used to make predictions on
any type of data. For example, we could use the predict method to predict
the likelihood of a customer clicking an ad, the probability of a patient
recovering from  a disease, or the number of sales that a company will make
in the next quarter .
The predict method is a powerf ul tool that can be used to make predictions
on new data. It is an important part of the machine learning process and can
be used to make decisions in a variety of dif ferent domains.
Here is a more detailed explanation of the predict method for probabilistic
models:
In the case of probabilistic models, the predict method returns a distribution
over the possibl e values of the target variable. This distribution can be used
to make predictions about the likelihood of different outcomes. For example,
if we have a model that predicts the probability of a patient recovering from
a diseas e, the predict method will return a distribution over the possible
values of the probability of recovery . We can then use this distribution to
make predictions about the likelihood of the patient recovering from the
disease.
The predict method for probabilistic models is a powerful tool that can be
used to make predictions about a variety of different outcomes. It is an
important part of the machine learning process and can be used to make
decisions in a variety of dif ferent domains.
Here are some additional details about the predict method for probabilistic
models:
The predict method for probabilistic models can return a variety of
different distributions. The most common distributions are the
Gaussian distribution and the binomial distribution.
The Gaussian distribution is a bell-shaped curve that is used to model
continuous data. The binomial distribution is a discrete distribution that
is used to model data that can take on only two values.
The predict method for probabilistic models can also return a
distribution over a set of possible values. For example, the predict
method for a model that predicts the probability of a patient recovering
from a disease could return a distribution over the possible values of
the probability of recovery .
The predict method for probabil istic models is a powerful tool that can
be used to make  predictions about a variety of different outcomes. It is
an impo rtant part of the machin e learning process and can be used to
make decisions in a variety of dif ferent domains.
Training losses
The MLJModelInterface.training_losses  function is used to get the
training losses of an iterative model. An iterative model is a model that is
trained using an iterative algorithm, such as gradient descent. The training
losses are the losses that the model experiences during training.
The MLJModelInterface.training_losses  function takes two arguments:
the model and the report. The model is the object that was trained, and the
report is an object that contains information about the training process.
The MLJModelInterface.training_losses  function returns an
AbstractVector  of the losses in historical order . The AbstractVector  is a
generic type that can be used to store any type of vector . The losses are
stored in historical order , which  means that the ﬁrst element of the vector is
the loss from the ﬁrst iteration of training, the second element of the vector
is the loss from the second iteration of training, and so on.
If the model calculates scores instead of losses, then the sign of the scores
should be reversed before returning them. This is because scores are
typically positive, while losses are typically negative.
The following trait overload is also required:
MLJModelInterface.supports_training_losses(::Type{<:M}) = true .
This trait overload tells MLJ that the model supports the training_losses
function.
Trait values can also be set using the metadata_model  method . The
metadata_model  method takes two arguments: the model and the trait name.
The model is the object that the trait values will be set for, and the trait name
is the name of the trait.
Feature importances
The MLJModelInterface.feature_importances  function is used to get the
feature importances  of a model. Feature importances are a measure of how
important each feature is to the model’ s predictions. The
MLJModelInterface.feature_importances  function takes three arguments:
the model, the fitresult , and the report.  The model is the model that you
want to get the feature importances  for, the fitresult  is the result of
training the model, and the report is a structure that contains information
about the model, such as the names of the features.
The MLJModelInterface.feature_importances  function returns an
AbstractVector  of feature importances. The AbstractVector  is a generic
type that can be used to represent any vector , and it is used to store the
feature importances in a way that they can be easily accessed.  The feature
importances are stored in the AbstractV ector in a dictionary , where the keys
are the names of the features and the values are the feature importances.
The following trait overload is also required:
MLJModelInterface.reports_feature_importances(::Type{<:M}) =
true . This trait overload tells MLJ that the model supports the
MLJModelInterface.feature_importances  function. If the model does not
support the MLJModelInterface.feature_importances  function, then an
error will be thrown when the function is called.
If for some reason a model is sometimes unable to report feature
importances then feature_importances  should return all importances  as
0.0, as in [:gender =>0.0, :height =>0.0, :weight => 0.0] . This is
because it is important to be able to get the feature importances of a model,
even if the model is not always able to calculate them.
Update! Method
A model can be updated multiple times to improve its accuracy . The
“Iterative models and the update! method” refers to a mechanism provided
by MLJ to efﬁciently update or retrain a model without repeating
unnecessary computations. It is an optional feature that can be implemented
by overloading the update method for a particular model.
The update method is called by MLJ to retrain a model on the same training
data when certain conditions are met, such as changes in hyperparameters or
the need to increase the number of iterations. The purpose  is to avoid
redundant computations and improve computational efﬁciency .
The update method has two variations:
MMI.update(model::SomeSupervisedModel, verbosity,
old_fitresult, old_cache, X, y) -> fit result, cache,
report
MMI.update(model::SomeSupervisedModel, verbosity,
old_fitresult, old_cache, X, y, w=nothing) -> fit result,
cache, report
The ﬁrst variation is used when the model does not support sample weights,
while the second variation is used when the model does support sample
weights.
If an MLJ Machine is being ﬁt and it is not the ﬁrst time, the update method
is called  instead of ﬁt, unless the ﬁt! method has been called with a new rows
keyword argume nt. However , if the update method is not implemented for a
speciﬁc model, MLJ provides a fallback that simply calls the ﬁt method
instead.
The update method is particula rly useful for iterative models or learning
networks wrapped as models.  In the case of iterative models, where
increasing the number of iterat ions only restarts the iterative procedure if
other hyperparameters have also changed, the update method ensures that
the model is retrained only when necessary . It helps to avoid unnecessary
retraining and saves computational resources.
Another use case for the update method is to avoid repeating time-
consuming preprocessing of the input data (X and y) required by certain
models. By storing the preprocessed versions of the data in the cache
returned by the ﬁt method, the update method can access and reuse them,
reducing redundant preprocessing computations.
If the fitresult  argument, returned by a preceding call to ﬁt, is not
sufﬁcient for performing an update, the author of the model can include any
additional information required in the cache return value of the ﬁt method.
This additional information can then be accessed and utilized by the update
method.
In summary , the update metho d in MLJ allows for efﬁcient retraining of
models by avoiding redundant computations. It is especially beneﬁcial for
iterative models, learning networks, and models with time-consuming
preprocessing steps. By implementing the update method, model authors can
optimize the training process and improve the overall performance of their
models within the MLJ framework.
Transform method
A supervised model in MLJ can optionally implement a transform method.
The signature of this method is the same as the predict method.  The purpose
of the transform method is to provide a mechanism for the model to perform
feature transform ations on new input data. This can be useful when the
model has learned feature embeddings or other transformations as part of its
training process.
If a supervised  model implements the transform method, it should also
deﬁne a value for the output_scitype  trait. This trait speciﬁes the scientiﬁc
type (scitype ) of the output of the transform  method. It assures that the
scientiﬁc type of the output of transform(model, fitresult, Xnew)  is
always a subtype of the speciﬁed scientiﬁc type.
One common use case for a transform method in a supervised model is when
the model learns feature embed dings for categorical input features during
training. The model can then act as a transformer , allowing other supervised
models to use it for transforming categorical features instead  of applying
traditional one-hot encoding representations. This can be particularly useful
in scenarios where high-dimen sional one-hot encoding is not desirable or
practical.
By implementing the transform method and deﬁning the output_scitype
trait, a supervised model can provide functionality for transforming input
data based on its learned representations. This enables the model to be used
as a transformer in conjunction with other supervised models within the MLJ
framework.
Models with a probability distribution
For models that ﬁt a probability distribution to data, they should be regarded
as Probabilistic models with the target variable y set to the data and the
feature variables X set to nothing. The predict method for such models
should return a single distribution.
Probability distribution models are considered to be a subtype of supervised
models in MLJ, where the target variable (y) is the data being modeled, and
there are no input variables (X = nothing). These models ﬁt a probability
distribution to the provided data. The predict method for such models should
return a single probability distribution as its output.
Serialization
Serialization refers to the process of saving a machine (model) object to a
persistent storag e format and restoring it. The MLJ user can serialize and
deserialize machines as they would with any other Julia object.
MLJ allows users to serialize and deserialize models using Julia’ s built-in
serialization mechanisms. However , some models may have ﬁt results that
are not serializa ble, such as C pointers. To address this issue,  models can
implement a save and restore method to switch between a ﬁt result and a
persistent, serializable representation of that result.
The save method takes a model object and a fitresult  object as inputs and
returns a persistent, serializable representation of the fitresult . The restore
method takes a model object and a persistent representation of the
fitresult  and returns a valid fitresult  object that can be used by the
model.
However , there can be a proble m when a model’ s fitresult  (returned by
the ﬁt method) is not a persistent object, such as a C pointer  that has no
meaning in a new Julia session. To handle this, model implemen tations need
to deﬁne a save and restore method for converting the fitresult  to a
persistent, serializable representation and reconstructing the fitresult ,
respectively . The save method takes the model and fitresult  as input and
returns a persistent serializable representation of the fitresult .
The restore method takes the model and the serialized fitresult  as input
and reconstructs a valid fitresult  that can be used as if it were returned by
the ﬁt method . If a model does not need any special handling for
serialization, the fallback implementation of save and restore performs no
action and returns the fitresult  or the serialized fitresult , respectively .
The need for a data frontend for MLJ
The data front-e nd in MLJ provides a mechanism for models to deﬁne how
user-supplied data is transformed and represented internally . It serves several
purposes and provides beneﬁts in the following ways:
Flexibility : MLJ supports a wide range of data types and formats, such
as table s, matrices, and more. By implementing a data front-end,
models can deﬁne how they expect the data to be formatted and
transformed to their speciﬁc internal representation. This allows
models to be more ﬂexible in accepting different types of data and
provides a consistent interface for users to work with.
Performance optimization:  The data front-end allows models to
perform preprocessing and data transformations once and reuse them
during subsequ ent operations. By deﬁning the reformatting  logic,
models can avoid redundant computations and improve perfor mance,
especially when dealing with large datasets or complex preprocessing
steps. This can be particularly  important in iterative procedures or
when repeated calls to fit! , predict , or transform  are made.
Memory efﬁciency : Some models might require memory-intensive
data representations, such as large matrices or specialized data
structures. The data front-end enables models to store and operate on
data in their preferred format, which can help optimize memory usage
and improve overall efﬁciency .
Resampling and evaluation : The data front-end also plays a role in
resampling and evaluation procedures. By implementing the
selectrows  method, models can deﬁne how the model-speciﬁc data
should be subsampled for speciﬁc observation indices. This enables
efﬁcient resampling of data during tasks like cross-validation or
bootstrapping. It also ensures that evaluation procedures operate on the
appropriate internal data representation.
Data frontend for MLJ
To implement a data front-end for a model in MLJ, you can follow the
guidelines provided. Here’ s an explanation of the steps involved:
Overload the reformat meth od: The reformat method is used to
transform user-supplied data into the model-speciﬁc representation.
Implement a reformat method for each form of args…  that appears in a
valid machine construction. This means there should be a reformat
method for each possible signature of fit(::SomeModelType, …). The
purpose of reformat is to avoid unnecessary computations when
memory resources allow . The fallback implementation returns the input
arguments as is.
Implement the selectrows  method : The selectrows  method is used
to speci fy how the model-speciﬁc data is subsampled for speciﬁc
observation indices. Implement the selectrows  method  for each
overloading of reformat(model::SomeModel, args…) -> data that
you deﬁned earlier . This allows  for more efﬁcient resampling of data
during operations like evaluate!
Implement other model operations : Implement operations like ﬁt,
predict, and transform, taking into account the reformatted versions of
user-supplied data. For example, in the case of supervised models, the
predict function  would take the reformatted version of the user-
supplied input data. Ensure that the reformatted data is used
consistently across the model operations.
By implementin g the data front-end, you can optimize the comp utations and
memory usage when working  with the model. It allows for efﬁcient
transformation, subsampling, and other operations speciﬁc to the model’ s
representation of data.
Machines in MLJ
In the context of MLJ.jl, “machines” refer to the primary building blocks
used to represent and perform machine learning tasks.
What ar e machines?
A machine is a type of model in MLJ that is composed of other models. This
allows you to combine the strengths of different models to create a more
powerful model.
For exam ple, you could create a machine that consists of a linear regression
model and a decision tree model. The linear regression model would be used
to predic t the main effects of the features, and the decision tree model would
be used to predict the interactions between the features. This would allow
you to create a model that is more accurate than either the linear regression
model or the decision tree model on its own.
How to cr eate machines?
To create a machine, you use the Machine constructor . The Machine
constructor takes a list of model s as input. The models in the list can be any
type of model that is supported by MLJ.
For example, the following code creates a machine that consists of a linear
regression model and a decision tree model:
model = Machine([LinearRegression(), DecisionTree()])
How to ﬁt machines?
To ﬁt a machine, you use the fit!  method . The fit!  method  takes the
machine and the training data as input. The ﬁt! method then trains the
machine on the training data.
The following code ﬁts the machine that was created in the previous
example:
model |> fit!(X, y)
How to pr edict with machines?
To predict with a machine, you use the predict method. The predict method
takes the machine and the test data as input. The predict method then
predicts the labels for the test data.
The following code predicts the labels for the test data using the machine
that was created and ﬁtted in the previous examples:
predictions = model |> predict(X_test)
How to evaluate machines?
To evaluate a machine, you use the evaluate method. The evaluate method
takes the machine, the training data, and the test data as input. The evaluate
method then calculates the accuracy of the machine on the training data and
the test data.
The following code evaluates the machine that was created, ﬁtted, and
predicted in the previous examples:
accuracy = model |> evaluate(X, y, X_test, y_test)
How to compose machines?
You can compose machines by using the compose function. The compose
function takes two machines as input and returns a new machine that is
composed of the two input machines.
The following code composes the machine that was created in the previous
example with a logistic regression model:
composed_model = compose(model, LogisticRegression())
composed_model  is a machine that consists of a linear regression model, a
decision tree model, and a logistic regression model. This allows you to
create a model that is more powerful than any of the individual models on its
own.
Model composition
Model composit ion is the proce ss of combining multiple models to create a
pipeline that can solve a speciﬁc machine learning task. The motivation
behind model composition is that it allows us to take adva ntage of the
strengths of dif ferent models to build a more powerful and accurate model.
The three common ways of combining models are:
Linear  Pipelines (Pipeline) : This method is used for unbranching
chains where the output of one model is used as the input for the next
model. An example of a linear  pipeline is dimension reduction (e.g.,
PCA) followed by a classiﬁcation model (e.g., EvoTreeClassifier ).
Linear pipelines  can also include transformations of the target variable
in supervised learning tasks.
Homogeneous Ensembles  (EnsembleModel ): This method is used to
blend the predictions of multip le supervised models, all of the same
type, but which receive differen t views of the training data to reduce
overall variance. This technique is known as observation bagging.
Model Stackin g (Stack) : This method is used to combine the
predictions of a smaller number of models of possibly different types
with the help of an adjudicating model.
Learning Netw orks : In addition to these three common methods, there
are Learning Networks, which are “blueprints” for combining models
in ﬂexible ways. Learning Networks are simple transformations of
existing workﬂo ws that can be “exported” to deﬁne new, stand-alone
model types. This allows for more complex model compositions
beyond the three common methods mentioned earlier .
Performance measures in MLJ.jl
A performance measure is a way of quantifying the accuracy of a machine
learning model. Performance measures are used to compare different models
and select the best model for a particular task.
Many different performance measures that can be used to evaluate machine
learning models.
How to choose performance measur es?
The choice of performance measure depends on the speciﬁc machine
learning task. For example, if you are building a model to predict whether a
customer will churn, you might  use the accuracy or the F1 score. If you are
building a model to predict the price of a house, you might use the mean
absolute error or the root mean squared error .
Built-in performance measures
MLJ provides a number of built-in performance measures. These measures
can be used to evaluate machine learning models without having to write any
custom code.
The built-in performance measures in MLJ include:
Accuracy
Precision
Recall
F1 score
Mean absolute error
Root mean squared error
Custom performance measures
MLJ also allows you to create custom performance measures. Custom
performance measures can be used to evaluate machine learning models for
tasks that are not supported by built-in performance measures.
To creat e a custom performance measure, you use the Measure constructor .
The Measure constructor takes  a function as input. The function that is
passed to the Measure constructor must take two arguments: the predicted
values and the actual values.
The following code creates a custom performance measure that calculates
the accuracy of a model:
function accuracy(predicted, actual)
sum(predicted .== actual) / length(predicted)
end
accuracy_measure = Measure(accuracy)
The accuracy_measure  variable can now be used to evaluate machine
learning models.
Model evaluation measures
Model evaluation is the process of assessing the accuracy of a machine
learning model. It is an impor tant part of the machine learning process
because it allows you to identify areas where the model can be improved.
There are many different ways to evaluate model performance.  Some of the
most common methods include:
Cross-validation:  Cross-validatio n is a method of evaluating model
performance by training the model on multiple subsets of the data and
then testing the model on the remaining subset of the data.
Holdout sets:  Holdout sets are a subset of the data that is not used to
train the model. The holdout set is used to test the model after it has
been trained.
Bootstrapping:  Bootstrapping is a method of evaluating model
performance by repeatedly sampling the data with replacement and
then training the model on each sample. The model is then tested on the
remaining data.
How to evaluate model performance?
To evalu ate model performance, you need to choose a performan ce metric. A
performance metric is a way of quantifying the accuracy of a model. Some
of the most common performance metrics include:
Accuracy:  Accuracy is the percentage of predictions that are correct.
Precision:  Precision is the percentage of positive predictions that are
actually positive.
Recall:  Recall is the percentage of positive instances that are correctly
identiﬁed as positive.
F1 scor e: The F1 score is a weighted average of precision and recall.
Mean absolute error: The mean absolute error is the average of the
absolute dif ference between the predicted values and the actual values.
Root mean squar ed error: The root mean squared error is the square
root of the average of the squared difference between the predicted
values and the actual values.
The choice of performance metric depends on the speciﬁc machine learning
task. For example, if you are building a model to predict whether a customer
will churn, you might use the accuracy or the F1 score. If you are building a
model to predict the price of a house, you might use the mean absolute error
or the root mean squared error .
Evaluating model performance on a hold-out set
The best way to evaluate model performance is to use a held-out set. A held-
out set is a subset of the data that is not used to train the model. The held-out
set is used to test the model after it has been trained.
The following steps are used to evaluate model performance on a held-out
set:
1. Split the data into a training set and a held-out set.
2. Train the model on the training set.
3. Test the model on the held-out set.
4. Calculate the performance metric for the model.
Evaluating model performance over time
It is important to evaluate model performance over time. This allows you to
identify any changes in the accuracy of the model.
The following steps are used to evaluate model performance over time:
1. Train the model on the data.
2. Test the model on the data.
3. Calculate the performance metric for the model.
4. Repeat the ﬁrst two steps for a while.
By evaluating model performanc e over time, you can identify any changes in
the accuracy of the model and take steps to improve the model if necessary .
Model tuning
Model tuning is the process of adjusting the hyperparameters of a model to
improve its performance. Hype rparameters are the parameters of a model
that are not learned from the data. Some examples of hyperparameters
include the number of trees in a decision tree model, the learning rate of a
neural network, and the regularization strength of a linear regression model.
How to tune models?
There are many  different ways to tune models. Some of the most common
methods include:
Grid search: Grid search is a brute-force method of tuning models.
Grid search involves creating a grid of hyperparameter values and then
training the model on each combination of hyperparameter values. The
model with the best performance is then selected.
Random search: Random  search is a more efﬁcient method of tuning
models than grid search. Random search involves randomly sampling
hyperparameter values from a distribution and then training the model
on each combination of hyperparameter values. The model with the
best performance is then selected.
Bayesian optimization:  Bayesian optimization is a more sophisticated
method of tuning models than grid search or random search. Bayesian
optimization involves using a Bayesian model to estimate the best
hyperparameter values for the model. The model with the best
performance is then selected.
Hyperparameters
Some of the most common hyperparameters for machine learning models
include:
Number  of tr ees: The number of trees in a decision tree model.
Learning rate:  The learning rate of a neural network.
Regularization strength:  The regulariz ation strength of a linear
regression model.
Evaluating tuned models
It is important to evaluate tuned models to ensure that they have improved
performance over the original model. To evaluate tuned models, follow these
steps:
Tune the model on the training set.
Evaluate the tuned model on the hold-out set.
Compare the performance of the tuned model to the performanc e of the
original model.
If the tuned model has improved performance over the original  model, then
you can use the tuned model for making predictions.
What ar e iterative models?
Iterative models  are models that can be updated multiple times to improve
their accuracy . They work by starting with a basic model and then making
small changes to the model based on the results of the previous update. This
process is repeated until the model reaches the desired level of accuracy .
Some examples of iterative models include:
Linear regression
Logistic regression
Decision trees
Neural networks
How to contr ol iterative models?
There are a number of ways to control iterative models. Some  of the most
common methods include:
Setting the number  of iteration s: You can set the number of iterations
that the model will be updated for. This is a simple way to control the
complexity of the model.
Setting the learning rate:  The learning rate controls how much the
model is updated  in each iteratio n. A higher learning rate will cause the
model to learn more quickly , but it may also cause the model to overﬁt
the data.
Using regularization:  Regular ization is a technique that can be used to
prevent overﬁtting. Regularization works by adding a penalty  to the
loss function that is proportional to the complexity of the model.
Common stopping criteria
There are various common stopping criteria that can be used to control
iterative models. Some of the most common stopping criteria include:
Maximum number  of iterations:  You can set the maximum number
of iterations that the model will be updated for. This is a simple way to
control the complexity of the model.
Minimum impr ovement:  You can set a minimum improvement that
the model must make in each iteration before it is updated again. This
can help to prevent the model from overﬁtting the data.
Early stopping:  Early stopping is a technique that can be used to stop
the model from updating before it has reached the maximum number of
iterations. Early  stopping is based on the idea that the model will not
improve signiﬁcantly after a certain point.
Regularization
Regularization is a technique that can be used to prevent overﬁtting.
Regularization works by adding a penalty to the loss function that is
proportional to the complexity of the model. This can help to prevent the
model from learning too much from the training data and to generalize better
to new data.
There are a number of different  regularization techniques that can be used.
Some of the most common regularization techniques include:
Lasso regularization:  Lasso regularization adds a penalty to the loss
function that is proportional to the sum of the absolute values of the
model weights. This can help to prevent the model from learn ing too
many features.
Ridge regulariz ation:  Ridge regularization adds a penalty to the loss
function that is proportional to the sum of the squares of the model
weights. This can help to prevent the model from learning too large of
weights.
What ar e learning curves?
Learning curves  are a way of visualizing the performance of a model as a
function of the amount of data that is used to train the model. Learning
curves typically plot the performance of the model on a held-out set of data
as a function of the number of training examples used to train the model.
How to cr eate learning curves?
To create learning curves, follow these steps:
1. Split the data into a training set and a held-out set.
2. Train the model on the training set.
3. Evaluate the model on the held-out set.
4. Repeat the ﬁrst two steps for a number of dif ferent training set sizes.
5. Plot the perform ance of the model on the held-out set as a function of
the number of training examples used to train the model.
Interpreting learning curves
Learning curves  can be used to interpret the performance of a model and
identify areas where the model  can be improved. Here are some things to
look for when interpreting learning curves:
The shape of the learning curve:  The shape of the learning curve can
tell you about the complexity of the model and the amount of data that
is needed to train the model. A model with a linear learning curve is a
simple model that can be trained  with a small amount of data. A model
with a quadratic learning curve is a more complex model that requires
more data to train.
The location of the peak of the learning curve:  The location of the
peak of the learning curve tells you about the amount of data that is
needed to train the model to its best possible performance. A model
with a peak at a small number of training examples is a mode l that is
overﬁtting the data. A model with a peak at a large number of training
examples is a model that is underﬁtting the data.
The spread of the points on the learning curve:  The spread of the
points on the learning curve tells you about the variance of the model.
A model with a small spread of points is a model with low variance. A
model with a lar ge spread of points is a model with high variance.
Using learning curves to improve models
Learning curves can be used to improve models by identifying  areas where
the model can be improved. Here are some things you can do to improve a
model based on its learning curves:
Reduce the complexity of the model:  If the learning  curve has a
quadratic shape, you can reduce the complexity of the model by using a
simpler model.
Increase the amount of data:  If the learning curve has not reached a
peak, you can increase the amount of data to train the model.
Use regularization:  Regularization can help to reduce the variance of
the model and improve its performance.
Conclusion
MLJ.jl recognizes the importance of data preprocessing and feature
engineering, offering a data frontend that efﬁciently handles diverse data
types, missing values, and transformations. It promotes ﬂexibility and
reproducibility by providing a wide range of machine learning algorithms for
model selection and evaluation. Model composition enables the construction
of sophisticated models by combining individual components. MLJ.jl’ s
comprehensive performance measures aid in selecting the best model for a
given problem. It supports iterative models, beneﬁcial for large datasets and
online learning.  Learning curves in MLJ.jl provide insights into model
performance as training data size increases, aiding in identifying underﬁtting
or overﬁtting and informing data collection and model development
decisions.
In the next chapter , we will learn about Bayesian statistics. Bayesian
statistics is a framework that combines prior knowledge and observed data to
make probabilistic inferences and update beliefs. Optimization is the process
of ﬁnding the best possible solution to a problem by systematically  searching
for optimal values of variables or parameters.
