CHAPTER 14
Parallel Computation in Julia
Introduction
Parallel computi ng is a computing paradigm that involves breaking down a
computational task into smaller subtasks that can be executed
simultaneously on multiple processing units. It aims to solve problems more
quickly by dividing the workload among multiple computational resources,
such as multiple processor cores, CPUs, GPUs, or even distributed
computing systems.
In a traditional serial computi ng model, a program or task is executed
sequentially , with each instruction or operation being processed one after
another . Parallel computing, on the other hand, allows multiple instructions
or operations to be executed simultaneously , potentially reducing the
overall execution time.
Parallel computing can of fer several advantages, including:
Speedup : By distributin g the workload across multiple processing
units, parallel computing can signiﬁcantly reduce the execution time
of computationally intensive tasks. This is particularly beneﬁcial for
tasks that can be divided into independent subtasks that can be
processed concurrently .
Scalability : Parallel computing provides a means to scale the
computational power of a system by adding more processing units. As
the size of the problem or the complexity of the comp utation
increases, parall elization allows  for efﬁcient utilization of available
resources.
Handling large datasets : Parallel computing can be used to process
and analyze large datasets efﬁciently . By splitting the data and
distributing it among multiple processors or nodes, parallel algorithms
can process the data in parallel, accelerating the analy sis or
computations.
Parallel computing can be achieved using dif ferent approaches, such as:
Shar ed-memory parallelism : In shared-mem ory parallelism,  multiple
processing units, such as CPU cores, share a common memory space.
Threads or processes can be used to divide the workload, and
synchronization mechanisms are used to coordinate their execution.
Distributed computing : In distributed computing, multiple
independent systems or nodes work together to solve a problem. Each
node typically has its own mem ory and processing capabilitie s, and
communication mechanisms are used to exchange data and coordinate
the computation.
GPU computin g: Graphics Processing Units (GPUs) are highly
parallel processors that excel at performing computations on large
datasets. GPU computing utilizes the parallel processing capabilities
of GPUs to accelerate computations for tasks that can be parallelized.
Parallel comput ing requires careful consideration of factors such as task
decomposition, load balancing, communication overh ead, and
synchronization to ensure efﬁcient utilization of resources and avoid
potential pitfalls such as race conditions and data dependencies. Modern
programming languages and frameworks, including Julia, provide tools and
libraries to facilitate parallel programming and make effective use of
parallel computing resources.
Structure
In this chapter , the following topics will be covered:
Parallel computation in Julia
Asynchronous programming
Asynchronous tasks in Julia
Task operations
Connecting via channels in Julia
Multi-threading
Speeding up data science workﬂows
Using the power of JuliaHub
Parallel computation in Julia
Julia provides robust support for parallel computation, allowing you to take
advantage of multiple cores or distributed computing resources to speed up
your code. There are several mechanisms and libraries available in Julia for
parallel programming:
Shar ed-memory parallelism : Julia has built-in support for shared-
memory parallelism using multi-threading. The Threads module in
Julia allows you to create and manage lightweight threads that can run
concurrently . You can use the @threads  macro to paralle lize loops and
execute iterations in parallel. Julia’ s SharedArrays  package provides a
way to create arrays that can be shared among threads.
Distributed computing : Julia has a built-in distribute d computing
framework that enables parallel  execution across multiple processes,
either on a single machine or across a cluster of machine s. The
Distributed module in Julia provides functions for launching and
managing worker processes. You can distribute tasks using constructs
like @distributed  and @spawn .
Parallel computing libraries : Julia ecosystem offers several libraries
for parallel computing. For example, the SharedArrays  package
mentioned earlier provides a way to work with large shared arrays
across multiple processes. The DistributedArrays  package allow s
you to work with distributed arrays that can be automatically
partitioned across multiple processes. The ParallelAccelerator
package provide s automatic parallelization of computations using just-
in-time (JIT) compilation.
GPU programming : Julia has excellent support for GPU
programming. The CUDA.jl  package allows you to write GPU-
accelerated code in Julia, utilizing the power of NVIDIA  GPUs. With
CUDA.jl , you can write parallel kernels and ofﬂoad computations to
the GPU, achieving signiﬁcant speedups for certain types of
calculations.
When working with parallel computation in Julia, it’s important to consider
factors like data dependencie s, load balancing, and communication
overhead. Julia’ s parallel programming constructs and libraries provide the
ﬂexibility and tools to handle these aspects efﬁciently and ef fectively .
Asynchronous programming
Asynchronous programming is a programming paradigm that allows for
concurrent execution of multip le tasks without blocking the execution of
the main program. It enables programs to initiate tasks, continue with other
operations, and handle the results of the tasks once they are completed,
without waiting for each task to ﬁnish before moving on.
In synchronous programming (also known as blocking programming), each
operation is executed sequentially , and the program waits for each operation
to complete before moving on to the next one. This can be inefﬁcient if
there are long-running operati ons, such as I/O operations or network
requests, that cause the program to wait for a response.
Asynchronous programming, on the other hand, enables non-blocking
execution. It allows the program  to start a task and then move  on to other
tasks or operatio ns while the ﬁrst task is being processed in the background.
The program can later handle  the results of the tasks once they are
completed.
Asynchronous programming is particularly useful in scenario s involving
I/O-bound opera tions, where the program spends a signiﬁcant amount of
time waiting for input/output operations to complete, such as reading from
or writing to a ﬁle, making network requests, or interacting with a database.
Key concepts and techniques used in asynchronous programming include:
Asynchr onous functions or methods : These are functions or methods
that are marked or designed to be executed asynchronously . They
typically return a “promise ” or a “future ” object that represents the
result of the computation that will be available in the future.
Callbacks : Callba cks are functions that are registered to be called
once a speciﬁc asynchronous operation completes. They allow the
program to specify the actions to be taken once the task is ﬁnished.
Promises and Futur es: These are objects that represent the results of
asynchronous computations. They allow the program to retrieve the
result of the computation once it is available.
Event loops : An event loop is a central component in asynchronous
programming that manages the execution of asynchronous tasks. It
continuously checks for completed tasks and triggers the associated
callbacks or notiﬁes the program when the results are available.
Asynchronous programming is widely used in various programming
languages and frameworks to build responsive and efﬁcient applications
that can handle concurrent operations effectively . It allows programs to
make optimal use of system resources, such as CPU and I/O, and can
greatly improve the performance and responsiveness of applications that
involve waiting for I/O or other external operations.
Asynchronous tasks in Julia
Asynchronous programming in Julia is facilitated through the use of Tasks.
A Task represents a computation  that can be suspended and resumed at any
point, allowing for non-blocking, concurrent execution of code.
When a piece of code is designated as a Task, it can be interrupted
(suspended) at certain points and switched to another Task, allowing other
computations to proceed concurrently . This is different from traditional
function calls, where the control must wait for a function to complete before
returning.
Tasks in Julia can be created using the `@task`  macro or by explicitly
calling the `Task() ` constructor . Once created, a Task can be started using
the `schedule` function or by invoking the `start ` function on the task
object. This initiates the execution of the Task in the background.
To suspend a Task and allow other computations to proceed, the `yield `
function is used. When `yield ` is called within a Task, it temporarily
pauses the execution of that Task, allowing other Tasks to be scheduled and
executed. The `yield ` function can be used to explicitly yield control, or it
may be implicitly called when certain operations (such as I/O) are
performed.
Tasks can also communicate and synchronize with each other using
channels. Chann els are communication constructs that allow Tasks to send
and receive values between each other . By sending and receiving values
through channels, Tasks can coordinate their execution and exchange
information.
Asynchronous programming with Tasks in Julia enables the execution of
multiple operations concurrently , making it useful for scenarios involving
I/O operations, network communication, and other situations where waiting
for external events is required. It allows programs to make efﬁcient use of
system resourc es and can improve performance by overlapping
computations that would otherwise block each other .
It’s worth noting that Julia also provides other constructs and libraries for
asynchronous programming, such as coroutines and libraries built on top of
Tasks (for example, `async ` and `await ` syntaxes). These constructs offer
more expressive and high-level ways of working with asynchronous code.
Task operations
In Julia,  Tasks represent units of computational work that can be created,
scheduled, and executed asynchronously . Here are the basic operations
involved in working with Tasks:
1. Task Creation : Tasks can be created using the `Task()`  constructor
or the `@task`  macro. The `Task()`  constructor takes a 0-argument
function as an argument, specifying the code that the Task will
execute. The `@task`  macro is a shorthand for creating a Task with an
anonymous function. For example:
julia> t = @task begin
sleep(5)
println(“done”)
end
In this example,  a Task `t` is created that sleeps for ﬁve seconds and
then prints “done”.
2. Task Schedulin g: After creating a Task, it needs  to be scheduled for
execution. Sched uling a Task adds it to an internal queue of tasks that
are ready to run. The `schedule()` function is used to schedule a Task
for execution. For example:
julia> schedule(t)
This schedules the Task `t` for execution.
3. Task Execution : Tasks are executed asynchronously , meaning they
run independently of the main program ﬂow. The actual execution of
Tasks occurs when the Julia runtime system has available resources,
such as when the program is idle or waiting for input. The execution
of a Task can be interrupted and resumed at any point.
4. Waiting for Task Completion : The `wait() ` function allows a Task
to be explicitly waited upon. When `wait() ` is called on a Task, the
calling Task blocks until the speciﬁed Task completes its execution.
For example:
julia> schedule(t)
Task (runnable) @0x000000010e3fb850
julia> wait(t)
done
In this case, the program will pause for ﬁve seconds before proceeding
to the next line after ` wait(t) `.
5. @async Macro : The `@async ` macro is provi ded as a conve nient way
to create and schedule a Task in a single step. It is equivalent to calling
`schedule(@task …) `. For example:
julia> @async begin
sleep(5)
println(“done”)
end
Task (runnable) @0x000000010e3a2ef0
julia> done
This creates and schedules a Task that sleeps for ﬁve seconds and prints
“done ”.
By using Tasks and asynchronous programming, Julia enables concurrent
execution of code, allowing for efﬁcient utilization of system resources and
handling of I/O operations without blocking the program’ s execution.
Yieldto function in tasks
In Julia, the low-level primitive  `yieldto ` is used to suspend the current
task, switch to another speciﬁe d task, and specify a value that the target
task’ s last `yieldto ` call will return . `yieldto(task, value) ` allows for
switching between tasks, enabling task-style control ﬂow. This mechanism
is why tasks are referred to as “symmetric coroutines ,” as each task can
be switched to and from using the same mechanism.
While `yieldto ` is a powerful tool, most uses of tasks in Julia do not
directly invoke it. This is becau se manually managing task switching and
determining when to switch back can be complex and require careful
coordination. Instead, higher -level constructs such as `put!` and `take! ` are
provided, which internally handle task switching and maintain state to
manage blocking operations like those used with channels.
In addition to `yieldto `, several other basic function s are available to
effectively use tasks:
1. `current_task `: This function returns a reference to the currently
running task. It allows you to access and manipulate properties  of the
current task, such as querying its state or associating data with it.
2. `istaskdone `: This function  is used to check whether a task has
exited, meaning  it has completed its execution. It returns `true` if the
task has ﬁnish ed, and `false` otherwise. It helps determine the
completion status of a task.
3. `istaskstarted `: This function checks whether  a task has run yet. It
returns `true` if the task has started executing, and `false` otherwise. It
helps determine if a task has been scheduled and is currently running.
4. `task_local_storage `: This set of functions allows manipulation of a
key-value store speciﬁc to the current task. It provides a way to store
and retrieve data associated with a task. Functions like
`get_task_local ` and `set_task_local ` are used to interact with the
task-local storage.
The rest of the functions complement `yieldto ` and provide additional
capabilities for managing and interacting with tasks. They help in tracking
task status, accessing task-speci ﬁc data, and determining when tasks have
completed their execution while `yieldto ` is a foundational primitive,
higher -level constructs and functions provided by Julia’ s task system
simplify the usage of tasks and manage task switching, making  it easier to
write code that leverages the beneﬁts of task-based concurrency .
Scheduler in Julia
In Julia, task scheduling and event handling are managed by the scheduler
in the Julia Base library . The scheduler keeps track of runnable tasks and
executes an event loop based  on external events like I/O requests or
message arrivals. Most task switches occur when tasks are waiting for
events, and the scheduler handles the resumption of tasks once the events
they are waiting for occur .
The primary function used for waiting for events in Julia is ‘wait’ . Many
objects implement the wait function, and when called, it causes  the task to
wait for a speciﬁc event to happen. For instance, wait can be used with a
Process object to wait for it to exit, or inside a call to read to wait for data to
become available.
Under the hood, wait operate s on a Condition object responsible for
queuing and restarting tasks. When a task call wait on a Condit ion, the task
becomes non-runnable, added to the condition’ s queue, and yields control to
the scheduler . The scheduler can then choose another runnable task to
execute or block and wait for external events. When the awaited event
occurs, an event handler typically calls notify on the Condition, which
makes all tasks waiting for that condition runnable again, and the scheduler
will eventually resume those tasks.
Tasks that are explicitly created using Task are initially unkn own to the
scheduler . This allows for manual task management using ‘yieldto’  if
desired. Howeve r, even if such a task waits for an event using wait, it will
still be automatically restarte d when the awaited event occurs. This
automatic resumption ensures that the tasks waiting for events can proceed
with their execution as expected.
Julia’ s task and event handling mechanisms, along with the scheduler , make
it easy to manage asynchronous operations and efﬁciently utilize
computational resources in applications that involve waiting for external
events or handling concurrent tasks.
Connecting via channels in Julia
In Julia, Channels provide a mechanism for communication and
synchronization between tasks. A Channel is a ﬁrst-in, ﬁrst-out (FIFO)
queue that allows multiple tasks to write to and read from it concurrently .
Creating a Channel is done using the `Channel() ` constru ctor, specifying
the type of objects that can be stored in the channel and an optional size
parameter to limit the number of elements it can hold. For example:
julia> chnl = Channel{Int}(32)
Channel{Int64}(32) (empty)
This creates a channel named `chnl ` that can hold up to 32 elements  of type
`Int`.
To write  data into a Channel, the `put! ` functio n is used. It allows multiple
tasks to write to the channel concurrently . For example:
julia> put!(chnl, 42)
42
This puts the value `42` into the channel ` chnl `.
To read data from a Channel, the `take! ` function is used. It retrieves the
next available value from the channel, blocking if the channel is empty . For
example:
julia> val = take!(chnl)
42
This retrieves the next value from the channel `chnl ` and assigns it to the
variable ` val`.
Channels also support other operations such as `fetch ` (retriev es but does
not remove the value), `isready ` (check s if the channel has a value
available), and ` wait ` (waits until a value is available).
Channels are useful for solvin g problems where multiple tasks need to
communicate and synchronize their execution. For example, in producer -
consumer scenarios, where one task produces values and another task
consumes them, Channels provide a way for the producer to write values
into the channel, and the consumer to read those values.
Channels can be used in conju nction with Tasks to orchestrate complex
work distributio n patterns. Multiple tasks can be bound to the same channel,
allowing them to read from and write to the channel concurrently .
Additionally , channels can be used as iterable objects in `for` loops. The
loop variable takes on each produced value until the channel is closed. For
example:
julia> function producer(c::Channel)
put!(c, “start”)
for n=1:4
put!(c, 2n)
end
put!(c, “stop”)
end;
julia> chnl = Channel(producer);
julia> take!(chnl)
“start”
julia> take!(chnl)
2
julia> take!(chnl)
4
julia> take!(chnl)
6
julia> take!(chnl)
8
julia> take!(chnl)
“stop”
julia> for x in Channel(producer)
println(x)
end
start
2
4
6
8
stop
In this example, the `producer ` function produces values into a channel,
and the `for` loop consumes those values until the channel is closed.
Closing a channel is done automatically when the associated task
terminates. However , a channe l can also be manually closed using the
`close ` function.
Channels provide a powerful tool for coordinating and communicating
between tasks, enabling concurrent execution and synchro nization in
complex scenarios.
Multi-threading
Multithreading is a programming technique that allows a single process to
perform multiple tasks concurrently . It enables a program to execute
multiple threads of execution within the same process, taking advantage of
modern multi-core processors to improve performance and responsiveness.
In traditional single-threaded programs, instructions are executed
sequentially , one after the other . This means that if a task takes a long time
to complete (for example, reading data from a slow network source), the
entire program will be blocked, waiting for that task to ﬁnish before moving
on to the next one.
Multithreading overcomes this limitation by creating multiple threads, each
of which  can execute a separate task concurrently . These threads can run
simultaneously on separate CPU cores or logical processors, allowing the
program to make better use of available resources.
Here are some key points about multithreading:
Threads : A threa d is the smallest unit of execution within a process. It
represents an independent sequence of instructions that can be
scheduled for execution by the operating system. Threads within the
same process share the same memory space, allowing them to
communicate and share data easily .
Concurr ent Execution : Multithreading allows tasks to be executed
concurrently , meaning that multiple threads can execute instru ctions
simultaneously . This can signiﬁcantly improve performance,
especially in applications that perform many independent tasks or
require responsiveness to user interactions.
Shar ed Memory : Thread s within the same process have access to
shared memory , which makes it easy to share data and communicate
between threads. However , this also introduces the challenge of
managing data synchronization and avoiding race conditions when
multiple threads try to access shared resources simultaneously .
Parallelism versus Concurr ency : Multithreadin g enables both
parallelism and concurrency . Parallelism refers to the simult aneous
execution of multiple tasks on multiple cores, resulting in increased
processing speed. Concurrency , on the other hand, allows tasks to
make progress independently , even if they are not executing
simultaneously .
Thread Safety : When  multiple threads access shared resources,
proper synchronization mechanisms (for example, locks and
semaphores) are required to ensure thread safety and prevent data
corruption or inconsistent results.
Context Switching : The operating  system manages the scheduling
and execution of threads. It performs context switching, which
involves saving the state of one thread, loading the state of another ,
and switching execution between threads. Context switching
introduces some overhead, so excessive use of threads may lead to
diminishing returns.
Multithreading is commonly used in various applications, such as web
servers, graphica l user interfaces (GUIs), video game engines, and scientiﬁc
simulations. It allows programs to efﬁciently utilize modern hardware,
which often includes multi-core  CPUs, and provides a smoothe r and more
responsive user experience. However , multithreading introduces
complexities related to thread safety and synchronization, making it
essential for developers to design their code carefully to avoid potential
issues.
Multiple threads in Julia
Julia is a high-le vel programming language known for its performance and
ease of use. It has built-in support for multi-threading, allowing users to
leverage multipl e threads for concurrent execution of tasks. The number of
threads can be speciﬁed using the --threads  command line argument or the
JULIA_NUM_THREADS  environment variable. Let’s explore how to start Julia
with multiple threads and how it affects the number of threads available for
computation.
1. Starting Julia with Multiple Threads using Command Line
Argument :
To start Julia with 4 threads, you can use the following command in your
terminal or command prompt:
julia --threads 4
After starting Julia with 4 threads, you can verify the numbe r of threads
using the Threads.nthreads()  function:
julia> Threads.nthreads()
4
You are currently on the master thread (the main thread) at this point. To
check the ID of the current thread, you can use the Threads.threadid()
function:
julia> Threads.threadid()
1
2. Starting Julia with Multiple Threads using Envir onment Variable :
Alternatively , you can set the JULIA_NUM_THREADS  environment variable to
specify the number of threads. For example, in Bash (Linux/macOS), you
can use:
export JULIA_NUM_THREADS=4
In C shell on Linux/macOS or CMD on Windows:
set JULIA_NUM_THREADS=4
In Powershell on Windows:
$env:JULIA_NUM_THREADS=4
Remember to set the environment variable before starting Julia.
3. Pr opagating Threads to Worker  Processes :
When you start Julia with multiple threads, the number of threads speciﬁed
will also be propagated to any worker processes you create using the -p or -
-procs  command line options. For example, if you start Julia with 2 worker
processes and 2 threads each, you can use the following command:
julia -p2 -t2
This will spawn 1 main process with 2 worker processes, and all three
processes will have 2 threads enabled. This can be useful for distributing
tasks across multiple processes and threads for parallel computing.
4. Specifying Auto Threads:
Instead of specifying a speciﬁc number of threads, you can use auto as the
value for JULIA_NUM_THREADS , starting  from Julia 1.7. This allows Julia to
infer a useful default number of threads to use based on your system’ s
capabilities. For example:
export JULIA_NUM_THREADS=auto
Please note that if you are using an older version of Julia, setting
JULIA_NUM_THREADS  to auto will be ignored.
Remember that not all operation s and packages in Julia are fully optimized
for multi-threading. Some tasks may not see signiﬁcant speed
improvements when using multiple threads, while others may beneﬁt
greatly from parallel execution.  Always benchmark and test your code to
ensure that multithreading improves performance for your speciﬁc use case.
ThreadPools
In Julia, threadpools  are used to manage the execution of tasks in different
contexts. By default, tasks are executed in the :default threadpool , but
you can specify that certain tasks are interactive using @spawn
:interactive  to help improv e the responsiveness and interactivity of the
program.
Here’ s an explanation of how threadpools  work and how you can start
Julia with multiple threadpools :
1. Default Threadpool : The :default threadpool is where most tasks run
if not speciﬁed otherwise. When you start Julia without explicitly
setting the threadpool , tasks will be executed in the :default
threadpool .
2. Interactive Threadpool : The :interactive threadpool  is
speciﬁcally meant for interactive tasks that require more
responsiveness and should avoid high-latency operations. Whe n you
use @spawn :interactive, the task is added to this threadpool , which
can help prevent delays and ensure better interactivity for the program.
3. Starting Julia with Multiple Threadpools : You can start Julia with
one or more threads reserve d for running interactive tasks by
specifying the number of threads for each threadpool . For example:
julia --threads 3,1
This starts Julia with 3 threads  in the :default threadpool  and 1
thread in the :interactive threadpool .
Alternatively , you can use the JULIA_NUM_THREADS  environment
variable to achieve the same ef fect:
export JULIA_NUM_THREADS=3,1
This command sets three threads for the :default threadpool  and 1
thread for the :interactive threadpool .
4. Using  ‘auto’  for Threadpool  Threads : Instead of specifying the
number of threads, you can use the word auto for either or both
threadpools . When auto is used, Julia will choose a reasonable
default number of threads for the respective threadpool .
For example:
julia --threads auto,1
This starts Julia with a reason able number of threads for the :default
threadpool  (decid ed by Julia) and 1 thread for the :interactive
threadpool .
To summarize, threadpools  in Julia allow you to manage different types of
tasks effectively . By designating tasks as interactive with @spawn
:interactive , you can ensure that critical interactive tasks are given
priority , leading to improved responsiveness and better user experience.
Starting Julia with multiple threadpools , with the ability  to use auto for the
number of threads, gives you ﬂexibility in optimizing performance based on
your speciﬁc use case.
Communication and synchronization in Julia
In multi-threaded programming, communication and synchronization
between threads  are crucial to ensure correctness and prevent data races.
Data races occur when multip le threads access and modify shared data
concurrently , leading to unpredictable and erroneous behavior . Julia
provides threa d-safe commu nication channels to facilitate safe
communication between threads. However , it is essential to be vigilant and
follow proper synchronization mechanisms, like using locks, to guarantee
data-race freedom.
The recommended way to ensure data-race freedom in Julia is to use locks
around any access to shared data that can be observed from multiple
threads. A common pattern to achieve this is to use the lock function to
acquire a lock before accessing  shared data and release it afterward. There
are two common ways to implement this pattern in Julia:
1. Using the  lock  function with  do block :
julia> lock(lk) do
use(a)
end
Using  try-finally  block with lock :
julia> begin
lock(lk)
try
use(a)
finally
unlock(lk)
end
end
Here, lk is a lock, like ReentrantLock() , and a is the shared data that needs
to be protected.
By using these patterns, you ensure that only one thread can access the
shared data at a time, avoiding data races and maintaining data consistency .
Furthermore, it is crucial to understand that Julia is not memory safe in the
presence of data races. If multiple threads access shared data without proper
synchronization, it can lead to undeﬁned behavior and unexpected results.
Therefore, always use the lock pattern when modifying data (for example,
assigning to a global or closure variable) accessed by other threads.
In the provided  example, accessing variable a in Thread 2 and Thread 3
without proper synchronization (for example, without using locks) can lead
to data races, making the code unsafe and potentially causing unpredictable
behavior .
In summary , when writing multi-threaded code in Julia, always use proper
synchronization mechanisms like locks to ensure data-race freedom and
maintain the integrity of shared data accessed by multiple threads.
Following these best practices  will help you write correct and reliable
multi-threaded programs in Julia.
@threads macro
The @threads  macro in Julia is a powerful tool to parallelize loops and
distribute work across multiple threads. However , as shown in the example
provided, using @threads  without  proper care can lead to data races and
incorrect results. To avoid data races, you should use appropriate
synchronization mechanisms, like using local buf fers or atomic operations.
The example demonstrates how to ﬁx the data race issue in the
sum_multi_bad  functio n by employing the sum_multi_good  functio n,
which correctly segments the sum into race-free chunks using local buffers
for each thread. Here’ s a breakdown of the solution:
1. sum_single function : This function calculates the sum of elements
in a given collection (a). It’s a basic implementation without
considering parallelization.
julia> function sum_single(a)
s = 0
for i in a
s += i
end
s
end
sum_single (generic function with 1 method)
2. sum_multi_bad  function : This function uses @threads  to parallelize
the loop, but it exposes a data race on the shared variable s, resulting
in incorrect results.
julia> function sum_multi_bad(a)
s = 0
Threads.@threads for i in a
s += i
end
s
end
sum_multi_bad (generic function with 1 method)
3. sum_multi_good  function : This function ﬁxes the data race issue by
dividing the array into chunks and calculating the sum of each chunk
independently . The chunks are then combined to get the ﬁnal sum.
Each thread works on its own local buf fer, avoiding data races.
julia> function sum_multi_good(a)
chunks = Iterators.partition(a, length(a) ÷
Threads.nthreads())
tasks = map(chunks) do chunk
Threads.@spawn sum_single(chunk)
end
chunk_sums = fetch.(tasks)
return sum_single(chunk_sums)
end
sum_multi_good (generic function with 1 method)
By using @spawn  inside the loop, each chunk of the array is processed
asynchronously by a different thread, ensuring that there are no data races.
The results from each chunk are combined using sum_single  to obtai n the
ﬁnal result.
Remember , as stated in the note, buffers should not be managed based on
threadid()  because concurrent tasks can yield, causing multiple tasks to
use the same buffer on a given  thread, introducing the risk of data races.
Instead, use local buf fers or atomic operations to ensure data-race freedom.
In summary , when using @threads  or any other multi-threading constructs
in Julia, make sure to handle shared data carefully to avoid data races.
Proper synchronization mechanisms and data segmentation can be used to
achieve correct and efﬁcient multi-threaded code.
Atomic Operations
Julia provides the Threads.Atomic  type to acces s and modify values
atomically , ensuring thread-safe  operations and avoiding race conditions.
This allows concurrent threads to modify shared data without causing data
races. Here’ s a summary of how it works:
1. Creating an Atomic Value: To create an atomic value, you use the
Threads.Atomic  constru ctor. The atomic value must be of a primitive
type, such as Int, Float64, and so on.
julia> i = Threads.Atomic{Int}(0)
Atomic{Int64}(0)
In this example, i is an atomic integer initialized to 0.
2. Using Atomic Operations : You can perfor m atomic operations on the
atomic value using speciﬁc functions like atomic_add! , atomic_sub! ,
atomic_min! , atomic_max! , and so on. These operations ensure that
the modiﬁcation is performed atomically , avoiding race conditions.
In the provided  example, the @threads macro is used to perform
atomic additions  to i. Each thread performs an atomic add operation
on i, and the resulting values are stored in the old_is  array .
julia> old_is = zeros(Int, 4)
4-element Vector{Int64}:
0
0
0
0
julia> Threads.@threads for id in 1:4
old_is[id] = Threads.atomic_add!(i, id)
end
3. Avoiding Race Conditions : Without atomic operations , race
conditions can occur , leading to incorrect results when multiple
threads modify shared data simultaneously . The second example in the
provided code illustrates this issue when using a non-atomic reference
(Ref) for accumulation.
acc = Ref(0)
@threads for i in 1:1000
acc[] += 1
end
In this case, the ﬁnal value of acc is incorrect due to race conditions,
and the result may vary on dif ferent runs.
4. Corr ect Usage with Atomic : To avoid race conditions and obtain the
correct result, you can use an atomic value:
julia> acc = Atomic{Int}(0)
Atomic{Int64}(0)
julia> @threads for i in 1:1000
atomic_add!(acc, 1)
end
julia> acc
Atomic{Int64}(1000)
Now , with the atomic value acc, the ﬁnal value is correct (1000).
Per-ﬁeld atomics
In Julia 1.7 and later versions, you can use per-ﬁeld atomics to work with
atomic operations at a more granular level within a struct. This allows you
to speci fy atom ic operations on speciﬁc ﬁelds of a struct and control the
ordering constraints for reads and writes to those ﬁelds. This feature enables
you to create more ﬁne-grained atomic operations in your code, providing
better control over concurrent access to individual ﬁelds.
Here are the key points related to per -ﬁeld atomics in Julia:
Decorate Fields  with  @atomic: You can annota te individual ﬁelds in
a struct declaration with the @atomic  macro to indicate that these ﬁelds
should be accessed atomically .
Annotate Writes with  @atomic  and Atomic Orderings : When
writing to an atomic ﬁeld, you must use the @atomic  macro before the
assignment operation. Additionally , you can specify the atomic
ordering constraint for the write operation. The available atomic
orderings are :monotonic , :acquire , :release , :acquire_release ,
or :sequentially_consistent .
Annotate Reads with Atomic Orderings (Optional) : You can also
specify the atomic ordering constraint when reading from an atomic
ﬁeld using the @atomic  macro. If no atomic ordering is speciﬁed for
the read operation, it will be done with the relaxed ordering, which is
:monotonic .
This feature allows you to have more control over the atomicity and
ordering of operations on speci ﬁc ﬁelds within a struct, ensuri ng safe and
consistent concurrent access.
Here’ s a conceptual example of using per -ﬁeld atomics in Julia:
struct AtomicData
@atomic field1::Int
@atomic field2::Float64
end
data = AtomicData(0, 0.0)
# Writing to atomic fields with specific atomic orderings
@atomic data.field1 = 42 :sequentially_consistent
@atomic data.field2 = 3.14 :release
# Reading from atomic fields with specific atomic orderings
(optional)
value1 = @atomic data.field1 :acquire
value2 = @atomic data.field2
Side Effects of using threads
When working with multi-threading and concurrent code in any
programming language, it’s crucial to be mindful of side effects and
mutable functio n arguments. As you mentioned, functions in Julia (and
some other languages) that have names ending with ! are conventionally
considered to be non-pure functions, meaning they modify their input
arguments (in-place) rather than returning new values.
In multi-threaded code, these non-pure functions can introduce unexpected
behavior and race conditions. If multiple threads concurrently  modify the
same mutable data (for example, arrays, dictionaries) without proper
synchronization, data races can occur , leading to incorrect results and
undeﬁned behavior .
To ensure correctness and data-race freedom, you should follow these best
practices:
Use Pure Functions : Prefer pure functions whenever possible. Pure
functions are functions that do not have any side effects and always
produce the same output for the same input. Using pure functions
ensures that you can safely call them from multiple threads without
worrying about data races.
Avoid In-Place Modiﬁcations : Avoid modifying shared data in-place
when working with multiple threads. Instead, prefer creating new data
structures or using atomic operations for safe updates.
Use Atomic Operations : When you need to perform atomic updates
on shared data, use atomic operations provided by Julia’ s atomic
types. This ensures that modiﬁcations to shared data are performed
atomically and without data races.
Use Locks or Other  Synchr onization Mechanisms : For situations
where in-place modiﬁcations are unavoidable or where more complex
synchronization is needed, use locks or other synchronization
mechanisms (for example, semaphores) to ensure that only one thread
can access the shared data at a time.
Be Mindful of Side Effects : Be aware of functions or libraries that
have side effects or modify the global state. When using such
functions in multi-threaded code , take extra precautions to ensure that
they are called in a thread-safe manner .
Test and Benchmark : Always thoroughly test your multi-threaded
code and perform benchmarks to verify that the parallelization
improves performance and produces correct results.
When using threads in Julia, there are some caveats and limit ations to be
aware of. While most operations in the Julia runtime and standard libraries
can be used in a thread-safe manner , it’s essential to pay attention to
potential issues to avoid undesirable behavior in multi-threaded programs.
Here are some speciﬁc limitations and warnings:
Manual Locking for Base Collection Types : If multiple threads
access Base collection types (for example, arrays and dictionaries)
simultaneously , and at least one thread modiﬁes the collection, manual
locking is required to avoid data races. For example, if multiple
threads are pushing elements into an array or inserting items into a
dictionary , proper synchronization mechanisms like locks should be
used.
Non-Deterministic Scheduling  for @spawn : The scheduling of tasks
created with @spawn  is nond eterministic. This means you should not
rely on the order of execution for tasks created using @spawn .
Memory Alloca tion and Garbage Collection (GC) : Compute-boun d
tasks that do not allocate memory can prevent garbage collection from
running in other threads that are allocating memory . This can lead to
memory-related issues and inefﬁciencies. To allow GC to run, you
may need to insert manual calls to GC.safepoint()  at appropriate
points in your code.
Avoid Running Top-Level Operations in Parallel : Running top-
level operations like include or evaluating type, method, and module
deﬁnitions in parallel can lead to unexpected behavior and conﬂicts.
It’s best to avoid parallel execution for such operations.
Finalizers and Threading : Be cautious when using libraries that
register ﬁnalizers (functions that are run when an object is garbage-
collected) becau se they may break when threads are enabled. This is
due to the inher ent complexitie s of managing ﬁnalization in a multi-
threaded enviro nment. As a result, some transitional work may be
required across the ecosystem before threading can be widely adopted
with conﬁdence.
@threadcall
The @threadcall  macro in Julia provides a mechanism to avoid stalling the
execution of tasks when calling external C libraries that perform blocking
operations. Whe n a C library performs a blocking operation, it can prevent
the Julia scheduler from executing other tasks until the call returns , which
can lead to poor performance and reduced responsiveness in multi-threaded
code.
To overcome this issue, the @threadcall  macro schedules a C function for
execution in a separate thread using a threadpool with a default size of 4
threads. The size of the threadpool  can be controlled via the environment
variable UV_THREADPOOL_SIZE . While waiting for a free thread, and during
function executi on once a thread is available, the requesting task (on the
main Julia even t loop) yields to other tasks, ensuring that other tasks can
continue execution even when a blocking C function is being called.
Important points to note about @threadcall :
1. Blocking Call: From a user’s point of view , @threadcall  is a
blocking call, similar to other Julia APIs. It does not return until the
execution of the C function is complete. However , the critical
difference is that during the execution of the C function, the Julia
scheduler can continue to execute other tasks due to the yielding
behavior .
2. Avoid Calling Back into Julia: The C function called using
@threadcall  must not call back into Julia, as doing so can lead to
segfaults. This is because the C function is executed in a separate
thread, which is not managed by Julia’ s scheduler , and calling back
into Julia can cause synchronization issues and undeﬁned behavior .
3. Futur e Changes : As stated in the documentation, @threadcall  may
be remo ved or changed in future versions of Julia. This suggests that
the implementat ion and behavior of @threadcall  might be subjec t to
modiﬁcations in subsequent Julia releases. Therefore, it’s important to
keep track of updates in Julia’ s documentation and release notes.
Task migration
In Julia 1.7 and later versions, task migration was introduced, allowing
tasks to move to different threads during their execution. Task migration
occurs when a task yields, and it may result in the task being resumed on a
different thread than the one it started on. This behavior applies to tasks that
have been started with @spawn  or @threads , except for tasks spawned using
the :static  schedule option for @threads , which freezes the thread ID.
Due to task migration, threadid()  should not be treated as a constant
within a task. If a task uses threadid()  to index into a vector of buffers or
stateful objects, there is a risk of data races and incorrect beha vior. This is
because, after task migration, the thread ID obtained using threadid()
might change, leading to unintended consequences when accessing shared
data.
To ensure data-race freedom and correct behavior when working with
multi-threaded tasks, it’s important to avoid relying on threadid()  as a
constant and to be mindful of potential task migration. Instea d, consider
using other synchronization mechanisms, like locks or atomic operations, to
manage shared data safely .
As a general guideline, when writing multi-threaded code in Julia, it’s
essential to understand task migration and to design your code in a way that
accounts for it. By using proper synchronization and avoiding direct
reliance on thread IDs, you can write thread-safe and robust multi-threaded
code in Julia.
Use of ﬁnalizers
When dealing with ﬁnalizers in Julia, it’s essential to be cautious due to
their potential to interrupt any code and interact with global state. Finalizers
are typically used to update global state, which can introduce complications
when working with multi-thread ed code. To ensure the safe use of ﬁnalizers
in multi-threaded contexts, you can employ dif ferent strategies:
Preventing Finalizers During Critical Regions : In single-threaded
code, you can call the internal C function jl_gc_enable_finalizers
to preve nt ﬁnalizers from being scheduled inside critical regions. This
can be combined with locks to ensure that ﬁnalizers do not interfere
with critical operations.
Delaying Finali zers Until Lock is Acquir ed: A strate gy used by Base
in some places is to explicitly delay a ﬁnalizer until it can acqu ire its
lock non-recursively . This ensures that the ﬁnalizer doesn’ t run
concurrently with other operations that require the same lock. An
example of this strategy is demonstrated in the code snippet for
finalize_ref .
Using Yield-Fr ee Queues : Another approach is to use yield-free
queues to handle ﬁnalizers. While Julia’ s Base library doesn’ t have a
lock-free queue implementation,
Base.IntrusiveLinkedListSynchronized{T}  can be used as an
alternative. This approach is suitable for code with event loops. The
idea is to avoid performing any explicit work inside the ﬁnalizer and
instead add the ﬁnalizer to a queue to be processed at a safer time. For
example, this strategy is emplo yed by Gtk.jl to manage lifetim e ref-
counting. In this approach, you can deﬁne the ﬁnalizer to use @spawn
and a separate thread to run the cleanup work.
It’s essential to carefully consider the impact of ﬁnalizers on multi-threaded
code, as they can introduce potential race conditions and other issues. By
employing one of the strategies mentioned above, you can make the use of
ﬁnalizers safer in multi-threaded contexts. Remember that ﬁnalizers should
be used with caution, and it’s essential to thoroughly test and verify the
behavior of your code when using them in multi-threaded environments.
Speeding up data science workﬂows
Speeding up data science workﬂows can be achieved through various
techniques, including the use of threads, asynchronous programming, and
parallel computation. Each approach has its strengths and is suitable for
different scenarios. Let’s explore how each technique can contribute to
faster data science workﬂows:
Threads : Julia’s multi-threading capabilities (via the Threads module)
can be used to execute multiple tasks concurrently within a single
Julia process. This can be beneﬁcial for certain data science tasks,
such as:
Data Loading : When working with large datasets, loading data
from ﬁles or databases can become a bottleneck. Using threads,
you can parallelize data loading and processing tasks, reducing
the overall loading time.
Preprocessing : Data preprocessing, includin g data cleaning,
feature engine ering, and transformations, can often be
parallelized. By distributing these tasks across threads, you can
speed up the data preparation phase.
Hyperparameter  Tuning : When  tuning hyperparamete rs for
machine learnin g models using techniques like cross-validatio n,
each evaluation can be independent. Threads can be used to
parallelize these  evaluations, signiﬁcantly reducing the tunin g
time.
Asynchr onous Programming : Asynchronous programming allows
you to handle multiple tasks concurrently without waiting for each to
be completed before moving on to the next. In data science, this
approach is useful for tasks, such as:
Web Scraping : When gatherin g data from web APIs or scraping
websites, reque sts to different endpoints can be performe d
asynchronously , improving the overall data acquisition speed.
Real-time Data  Streams : Analyz ing real-time data streams, such
as stock market data or sensor readings, can beneﬁt from
asynchronous processing to handle incoming data in parallel.
Parallel Computation : Parallel computation involves executing tasks
simultaneously on multiple cores or nodes of a computing cluste r. It is
particularly useful for tasks that can be divided into indep endent
subproblems, including:
Large-Scale Machine Learning : Training complex machine
learning model s on large datasets can be computationally
intensive. Parallelizing the training process across multiple cores
or nodes can signiﬁcantly reduce the training time.
Monte Carlo Simulations : In statistical simulations, or Monte
Carlo methods, individual simulations can be executed in
parallel, improving the efﬁciency of the overall analysis.
Bootstrapping and Cross-validation : Bootst rapping and cross-
validation involv e repeatedly resampling data and ﬁtting model s,
making them suitable candidates for parallel computation.
However , it’s important to note that not all data science workﬂows will
beneﬁt equally from each of these techniques. Some tasks may not be easily
parallelizable or asynchronous , and introducing concurrency can add
complexity , potentially leading to performance degradati on due to
overhead.
Careful consideration, proﬁling, and benchmarking are necessary to identify
the speciﬁc bottlenecks in your data science workﬂow and determine the
most appropriate techniques to speed up the process. Additionally , the
availability of hardware resources and the architecture of the data
processing pipeline also play crucial roles in optimizing the workﬂow’ s
performance.

programming language, including package hosting, collaboration tools, and
computational resources. While JuliaHub itself is not responsible for
enabling async, parallel, or multi-threaded computations directly , it can
certainly be utilized in conjun ction with the power of Julia ’s language
features and ecosystem to enhance data science workﬂows.
Using the power of JuliaHub
Here’ s how JuliaHub can be leveraged for async, parallel, and multi-
threaded computations:
Package Hosti ng and Management : JuliaHub provides package
hosting services, allowing developers to share and distribute their Julia
packages. Many packages in the Julia ecosystem are speciﬁcally
designed to support asynchronous, parallel, and multi-th readed
computations. By leveraging these packages, data scientists can easily
incorporate advanced concurrency features into their workﬂows.
High-Performance Computing  Resour ces: JuliaHub offers access to
high-performance computing resources, such as cloud-based clusters
and servers. These resources can be used to execute computationally
intensive tasks in parallel, signiﬁcantly speeding up data analysis and
model training processes.
Collaboration and Community : JuliaH ub fosters collaborat ion
within the Julia community . Data scientists can share knowledge, best
practices, and efﬁcient code snippets related to async and parallel
computing throu gh JuliaHub’ s collaboration tools. By learning  from
the community , developers can improve their concurrency skills and
optimize their data science workﬂows.
Jupyter  Notebo oks and Interactive Computing : JuliaH ub supp orts
Jupyter noteboo ks, providing an interactive environment for data
exploration and analysis. With Julia’ s built-in support for async and
parallel programming, data scientists can use Jupyter notebooks on
JuliaHub to experiment with concurrent solutions and optimize their
code iteratively .
Benchmarking and Proﬁling : JuliaHub’ s computational resources
can be used for benchmarking and proﬁling data science work ﬂows.
By running experiments on different conﬁgurations of async and
parallel code, developers can ﬁne-tune their implementations for better
performance.
Job Management and Sche duling : JuliaHub may provide job
management and scheduling capabilities, allowing users to submit and
manage concurr ent jobs. This can be useful for running a large number
of data science experiments in parallel, efﬁciently utilizing the
available computing resources.
It’s essential to keep in mind that Julia is a versatile language with powerful
concurrency features built into its core. While JuliaHub provides a platform
to suppo rt these features, the actual implementation of async, parallel, and
multi-threaded computations largely depends on the Julia packages and
code written by developers. By leveraging the language’ s capabilities and
the resources provided by JuliaHub, data scientists can efﬁciently harness
the power of async and parallel computing for their data science tasks.

CHAPTER 15
Distributed Computation in Julia
Introduction
Distributed computing refers to a ﬁeld of computer science and engineering
that deals with the design, implementation, and management of systems in
which multiple computers or devices work together to achieve a common
goal. The goal of distributed computing is to leverage the combined
processing power and resource s of multiple machines to solve complex
problems, process large amounts of data, or provide high-performance
services.
In a distributed computing system, the individual machines, often referred
to as nodes, are connected through a network. These nodes communicate
and coordinate their activities to accomplish tasks that would be difﬁcult or
impossible for a single machine to handle efﬁciently . This approach offers
several advanta ges, including improved performance, fault tolerance,
scalability , and resource utilization.
There are various models and architectures for distributed computing,
including:
Client-Server  Architectur e: In this model, there are central servers
that provide services or resources to client machines. Clients request
services from the servers, which then respond with the required data or
results. Web servers and databases often use this architecture.
Peer -to-Peer  (P2P) Architectur e: In a P2P model, all participating
nodes can act as both clients and servers. Nodes share resources
directly with each other , such as ﬁles or processing power . Examples
of P2P  systems include ﬁle-sharing networks like BitT orrent.
Cluster  Compu ting:  Cluster computing involves connecting multiple
machines to form a cluster . Thes e machines work together to complete
computational tasks, often for parallel processing or high-perfor mance
computing purposes.
Grid Computing:  Grid computing extends the concept of cluster
computing by connecting geogr aphically distributed clusters to create
a large, virtualized computing resource. This allows organizations to
share resources and collaborate on tasks that require signiﬁcant
computing power .
Cloud Computing:  Cloud computing provides resources and services
over the internet on demand. It abstracts the underlying infrastr ucture
and allows users to access computing resources, storage, and
applications as needed. Cloud platforms like Amazon Web Services
(AWS), Micros oft Azure, and Google Cloud are examples of this
model.
Distributed computing systems come with challenges related to
communication, synchronization, consistency , fault tolerance, and load
balancing. Designing and implementing effective distributed systems
require careful consideration of these challenges to ensure that the system
performs reliably and efﬁciently .
Structure
In this chapter , the following topics will be covered:
