Distributed computation in Julia
In the realm of high-performance computing, the demand for faster and
more efﬁcient computation has led to the evolution of parallel and
distributed computing. Julia, a modern programming language designed for
scientiﬁc and technical computing, embraces this demand by offering robust
support for both multi-processing and distributed computing. This chapter
delves into the fascinating world of parallelism, exploring how Julia’ s
Distributed module revolutionizes the way we leverage the computational
capabilities of modern hardware.
Harnessing the Strength of Many
In today’ s computing landscape, a single computer often hosts multiple
CPUs, while multiple compute rs can be combined into a cluster . This
abundance of processing power holds the potential to accelerate
computations signiﬁcantly . There are two critical factors that inﬂuence
performance: the speed of CPUs and their access to memory . Even in
multicore laptop s, variations in memory access speed and cache capabilities
impact performance. An efﬁci ent parallel computing environ ment must
enable precise control over mem ory allocation to each CPU. Julia answers
this need by providing a multiprocessing environment founded on message
passing, allowing programs to execute across multiple processes with
distinct memory domains simultaneously .
A Fresh Take on Message Passing
Unlike traditional message-passing libraries like MPI, Julia introduces a
novel approach to messag e-passing. It employs a “one-sided”
communication model, where the programmer primarily manages one
process in a two-process operation. This design simpliﬁes the interaction by
resembling high-level function calls rather than the classic “message send”
and “message receive” paradigm. Julia’ s distributed programming hinges on
two core primitives: remote references and remote calls.
Remote References: Bridging the Gap
Remote references are powerful constructs that enable communication
across processe s. They serve as handles to objects stored on speciﬁc
processes. Julia  offers two types of remote references: Future  and
RemoteChannel . A Future  represents the result of a remote call, providing a
way to access the outcome of an operation on a separate process . The result
is obtained using wait on the Future or by fetching it. RemoteChannels  are
dynamic communication channels that allow multiple processes to
coordinate their actions by accessing the same shared channel. This opens
avenues for synchronized processing among various processes.
Remote Calls: Executing from Afar
Remote calls are at the heart of Julia’ s distributed computing  paradigm.
They facilitate executing functi ons on remote processes. When a process
makes a remote call, it immed iately receives a Future  represe nting the
result of that call. This non-blocking approach lets the initiating process
continue its operations while the remote call progresses elsewhere. Waiting
for the result can be done using wait, and the outcome can be obtained
using fetch.
Process Identity and Worker Model
In Julia’ s distributed environment, each process is assigned a unique
identiﬁer . The process controlling the Julia prompt bears the ID 1.
Concurrently , processes employed for parallel tasks are known as
“workers ”. For single-pro cess scenarios, the solitary process is also treated
as a worker (Process 1). However , in true parallelism, all proce sses except
Process 1 are considered worke rs. This distinction is vital for methods like
pmap , which ef fectively parallelizes computations across multiple workers.
Embracing Parallelism: A Practical Guide
Putting theory into action, Julia  makes it seamless to harness parallel and
distributed computing. Launching multiple worker processes on a local
machine is as straightforward as invoking julia -p n, where n is the
desired number of workers. Typically , matching n with the number of CPU
threads (logical cores) optimizes  performance. It’s important to note that the
-p argument automatically incorporates the Distributed modu le, ensuring
access to its powerful capabilities.
$ julia -p 2
julia> r = remotecall(rand, 2, 2, 2)
Future(2, 1, 4, ReentrantLock(nothing, 0x00000000, 0x00,
Base.GenericCondition{Base.Threads.SpinLock}
(Base.IntrusiveLinkedList{Task}(nothing, nothing),
Base.Threads.SpinLock(0)), (8, 0, 141733920768)), nothing)
julia> s = @spawnat 2 1 .+ fetch(r)
Future(2, 1, 5, ReentrantLock(nothing, 0x00000000, 0x00,
Base.GenericCondition{Base.Threads.SpinLock}
(Base.IntrusiveLinkedList{Task}(nothing, nothing),
Base.Threads.SpinLock(0)), (8, 4572472576, 4883600368)),
nothing)
julia> fetch(s)
2×2 Array{Float64,2}:
1.18526 1.50912
1.16296 1.60607
Here, you are starting the Julia interpreter with 2 worker processes. The -p
ﬂag indicates the number of worker processes you want to launch. In this
case, you are requesting 2 worker processes.
1. remotecall(rand, 2, 2, 2):
The remotecall function initiates a remote call to a speciﬁ c
function on a particular worker process.
In this case, you are calling the rand function remotely .
The ﬁrst ar gument (rand) is the function you want to call.
The following arguments (2, 2, 2) are the dimensions for
generating random numbers.
The result of this remote call is a Future  that represents  the
outcome of the operation on the speciﬁed worker process.
The output Future(2, 1, 4, nothing)  indicate s that the result
will be available on worker 2, process 1, and the call has a uniqu e
ID of 4.
2. @spawnat 2 1 .+ fetch(r) :
The @spawnat  macro is used to initiate a computation on a
speciﬁc worker process, in this case, worker 2.
2 after @spawnat  indicates the tar get worker process.
1 .+ fetch(r)  is the computation to be performed on the
speciﬁed worker process.
fetch(r)  retrieves the value of the previous remote call’s result
(stored in the Future object r).
1 .+ fetch(r)  adds 1 element-wise to the fetched matrix.
The result of this spawned computation is a Future  that
represents the outcome of the computation on the speciﬁed
worker process.
The output Future(2, 1, 5, nothing)  indicate s that the result
will be available on worker 2, process 1, and the call has a uniqu e
ID of 5.
3. fetch(s) :
The fetch function retrieves the value of a Future .
In this case, you are fetching the result of the computatio n
initiated by the @spawnat macro.
The result is a 2× ٢ matrix of ﬂoating-point numbers.
In summary , the code demonstrates how to perform distributed
computations using Julia›s parallel and distributed computing features. It
generates random numbers remotely , adds 1 to the resulting matrix on a
speciﬁc worker process, and then fetches the ﬁnal result back to the main
process for display . This showcases the power of Julia’ s distributed
programming model and its ability to seamlessly perform computations
across multiple processes.
The landscape of computing is evolving rapidly , and the deman d for faster
and more efﬁcient computations continues to grow . Julia’ s innovative
approach to parallel and distributed computing empowers developers to tap
into the full potential of modern hardware. By simplifying message passing
and providing intuitive tools like remote references and calls, Julia paves
the way for seamless multi-processing and distributed computing. Whether
you›re working on a single laptop or orchestrating computations across a
cluster , Julia’s Distributed module equips you with the tools to conquer the
most demanding computational challenges.
Asynchronous Execution and Cooperative Task
Scheduling in Julia
In the realm of parallel and distributed computing, Julia stands out not only
for its powerful abstractions but also for its thoughtful optimizations. In this
chapter , we delve into two critical concepts: Future caching and cooperative
multitasking. These mechanisms are designed to enhance performance,
improve efﬁciency , and streamline the execution of complex parallel tasks.
The Magic of Future Caching
Julia’ s Future  abstraction is a gateway to a world of parallelism, allowing
tasks to execute remotely while main processes continue without waiting. A
notable feature of Futures is the ability to cache fetched values locally .
When a Future’ s value is fetche d, it is stored locally , minimizing network
hops and subsequent fetch calls for the same result. This optimization not
only reduces overhead but also promotes smoother execution by eliminating
redundant network communication.
Cooperative Multitasking: The @async and
@sync Approach
A signiﬁ cant stride in parallelism comes with Julia’ s @async  and @sync
mechanisms. These tools empower developers to manage tasks in a
cooperative multitasking environment, harnessing paralle lism while
synchronizing execution for seamless coordination.
Feeder  Tasks and @async
The @async  macro functions similarly to @spawnat , but it focuses on local
execution. It is an essential tool in creating “feeder” tasks, a concept pivotal
for distributing tasks across processes efﬁciently . Each feeder task takes
charge of computing a speciﬁc index, awaiting the completion of its
assigned process before procee ding to the next index. Importantly , feeder
tasks don’ t kick of f until the main task enters an @sync  block.
@sync: Coordinated Synchr onization
The @sync  macro serves as the conductor of Julia’ s cooperative
multitasking orchestra. It governs synchronization among tasks, ensuring
the main  task patiently waits until all local tasks, initiated by @async , are
concluded. This approach ensures an organized orchestration of parallel
tasks, preventing chaotic clashes and facilitating a well-structured execution
ﬂow.
Sharing State and Cooperative Multitasking
Julia’ s cooperati ve multitasking model enables tasks to share state, fostering
efﬁcient collabo ration among parallel processes. As of version 0.7 and
beyond, @async -spawned feede r tasks can seamlessly access shared
resources like the nextidx variable. This harmonious state-sharing among
tasks that run on the same process exempliﬁes Julia’ s careful design to
optimize cooperation in parallel execution.
Mindful Context Switching
Cooperative multitasking also comes with a deliberate approach to context
switching. Julia strategically schedules context switches at speciﬁc points,
such as when using remotecall_fetch . This calculated synchronization
helps to manage execution ﬂows efﬁciently , ensuring tasks are not
interrupted haphazardly .
Preparing for Future Innovations
It’s important to recognize that Julia’ s cooperative multitasking model and
resource sharing represent the current state of implementation. This model
is designed to facilitate running multiple tasks on a single process,
potentially leading to a fascinating M:N threading approach in future Julia
versions. However , this advancement might introdu ce the need for resource
locking, particularly when dealing with shared resources.
Julia’ s approach to Future caching and cooperative multitasking
demonstrates a deep commitment to optimizing parallel and distributed
computing. By locally caching Future values, Julia minimizes redundant
network hops and improves overall performance. The syner gy between
@async  and @sync  introduces a cooperative multitasking model that
elegantly orche strates parallel tasks, ensuring organized execution. The
capacity for tasks to share state and the thoughtful managemen t of context
switching showcase Julia’ s emphasis on balance and efﬁciency . As Julia
continues to evolve, these principles will likely play a crucial role in
shaping the future of parallel and distributed computing in the language.
Loading packages in Distributed Computing
We will understa nd the concept of code availability and loading packages in
the context of parallel and distributed computing using  the Julia
programming language. Let’ s break down the main points:
Code Availability:  When workin g with parallel and distributed
computing in Julia, the code you write must be available on all the
processes that will execute it. Each process runs independently , so if a
process doesn’ t have access to the required code, it won’ t be able to
execute functions or use data deﬁned in that code.
Example - Availability Issue:  In the given example, a function called
rand2  is deﬁned. When this function is called on process 1, it runs
successfully because the functio n is deﬁned there. However , when the
same function is called using the @spawnat  macro to run on another
process (process 2), an error occurs because process 2 doesn’ t have
access to the rand2  function.
Loading Packa ges and Files:  In Julia , you can load code from  ﬁles or
packages. The example introduces a ﬁle named DummyModule.jl ,
which deﬁnes a module named DummyModule . This module contains a
mutable struct MyType  and a function f. The @everywhere  macro is
used to ensure that the DummyModule.jl  ﬁle is loaded on every
process.
Scope and Accessibility:  Loading the code doesn’ t automatically
bring the module’ s deﬁnitions into the current scope of all processes.
You need to use using  or import  to make the module’ s contents
accessible. For example, after using .DummyModule , you can create
objects and use functions deﬁne d in DummyModule . If a process hasn’t
brought the module into scope, you can still send objects of types
deﬁned in the module to that process, even if the type itself  is not
directly accessible.
Preloading Files at Startup:  If you want to ensure that certain ﬁles
are preloaded on all processes when you start Julia, you can use the -L
ﬂag follo wed by the ﬁle names. This ensures that the speciﬁed ﬁles are
available on all processes from  the beginning. Additionally , you can
have a driver script that coordinates the computation across processes.
Packages and Scoping:  If your code is structured as a package, using
the pack age with using  will load the package’ s code on all processes,
but the package’ s contents will only be accessible on the process
where using  was called. This means that even though all processes
have access to the package’ s code, they don’t all have access to its
contents unless they explicitly bring it into scope.
The overall message is that in parallel and distributed comput ing in Julia,
you need  to ensure that your code is available on all processes that will run
it. You can achieve this by loading ﬁles using the @everywhere  macro, or by
using the -L ﬂag at startup. However , loading the code doesn’ t necessarily
bring the contents into scope, and you need to manage scoping and
accessibility explicitly across processes.
Managing processes
We will understa nd the concepts related to multi-processing and distributed
computing in Julia. It outlines the principles behind parallel processing,
message passing, and distributed programming in the languag e. Here’ s a
detailed breakdown:
Introduction to multipr ocessin g and Distributed Computing : Julia
provides support for distributed  memory parallel computing through
the Distributed module, which is part of the standard library . This is
particularly useful for utilizing the computational power of modern
computers with multiple CPUs or clusters of interconnected
computers.
Performance Factors:  Perform ance in distributed computing is
inﬂuenced by CPU speed and memory access speed. Local memory
access is faster than accessing memory on remote nodes. These
considerations hold for both multi-core computers and clusters.
Julia’ s Appr oach to Distributed Computing:  Julia’ s distributed
programming environment is based on message passing. Processes can
run on separate memory domains and communicate through message
passing, allowing for concurrent execution.
Message Passing in Julia:  Julia’s message  passing is typically “one-
sided,” meaning that one process expli citly manages communication
while the other proceeds with its operation. Communication doesn’ t
necessarily involve direct message send and receive operations but
often takes the form of higher -level function calls.
Distributed Programming Primitives:  Distributed programming in
Julia is built upon two core concepts: remote references and remote
calls. Remote references allow processes to refer to objects stored on
other processes, while remote calls involve requesting another process
to execute a certain function on speciﬁc ar guments.
Using Remote Refer ences:  Remote references come in two forms:
Future  and RemoteChannel . A Future  represen ts the result of a remote
call and can be fetched to obtain the result. A RemoteChannel  is a
communication channel between processes.
Creating and Fetching Remote Calls:  Remote calls return Future
objects representing the result of the computation. They return
immediately , and you can wait for them to ﬁnish using wait  and
obtain the result using fetch .
Using  @spawnat  Macr o: The @spawnat  macro evaluates an expression
on a speciﬁed process. It’s similar to a remote call but more
expressive. It’ s used to perform computations on remote processes.
Efﬁcient Fetching:  The remotecall_fetch  function combines
fetching and calling a remote function in one step, making it more
efﬁcient.
Parallel Processing and @async : @async  is a macro used to run tasks
concurrently on the local process. It’s useful for creating feeder tasks
for each process.
Code Availability and Loading Packages:  Code must be available
on all processes that use it. Load ing code from ﬁles or packages  using
@everywhere  or the -L ﬂag ensures code availability . Using packages
requires managing scope.
Starting and Managing Worker Processes:  The addprocs  functio n
adds worker processes, rmprocs  removes them, and workers  lists
active processes. It’s important to load the Distributed  module  on the
master process before adding workers.
Custom Cluste rManagers:  Julia supports various types of clusters,
including custom ones. A ClusterManager  can be implemented to
deﬁne how Julia interacts with dif ferent cluster setups.
Data transfer in distributed computing
We will unde rstand the concept of data movement in distributed
programming using Julia and the importance of minimizing data movement
for achieving performance and scalability , with examples illustrating
different ways data can be moved between processes and highlighting the
trade-of fs involved.
Data Movement Overhead:  Data movement , which involves sending
messages or transferring data between different processes,  often
constitutes a signiﬁcant portion of the overhead in distributed
programs. The goal is to reduce the number of messages and the
amount of data moved to improve program performance and
scalability .
Explicit and Implicit Data Movement:  In Julia’ s distributed
programming constructs, there are both explicit and implicit data
movement operations. The fetch  function is an explicit data
movement operation because it directly requests that an object be
moved from a remote process to the local machine. On the other hand,
constructs like @spawnat  have an implicit data moveme nt aspect that
might not be immediately obvious.
Example: Constructing and Squaring a Matrix:  The text provides an
example involvi ng two methods for constructing and squaring a random
matrix in a distributed context.
Method 1:
julia> A = rand(1000, 1000);
julia> Bref = @spawnat :any A^2;
julia> fetch(Bref);
1000×1000 Matrix{Float64}:
…
Method 2:
julia> Bref = @spawnat :any rand(1000, 1000)^2;
julia> fetch(Bref);
1000×1000 Matrix{Float64}:
…
Distinguishing the Methods:  While the difference between the two
methods may seem trivial, it has signiﬁcant implications due to the
behavior of @spawnat . In Method 1, a random matrix is constructed on
the local process, and then sent to another process where it is squared.
In Meth od 2, both matrix constr uction and squaring are performed on
the same remote process. As a result, the second method involves
sending much less data between processes compared to the ﬁrst
method.
Considerations for Data Movement:  Designing data movement
strategies in a distributed program requires careful consideration and
often measurement. Depending on various factors, such as the
availability of data on different processes and the computational cost
of operations, dif ferent approaches may be beneﬁcial.
If the current process needs the data, local construction might be
better .
If computing the data is expens ive and only the current proce ss
has it, moving it to another process might be necessary .
If the current process has minimal work between @spawnat  and
fetch , eliminating parallelism could be considered.
If the random matrix generation is replaced with a more
expensive operation, adding another @spawnat  might  be
advantageous.
Choosing the Right Appr oach:  The choice of data movement
strategy depends on the speciﬁc requirements and characteristics of
the problem. Optimizing data movement involves weighing trade-offs
between paralle lism, computatio n cost, and communication overhead
to achieve the best overall performance in a distributed program.
Variables in distributed computing
The provided text discusses the behavior of global variables in the context
of remote execution using Julia’ s distributed programming features, such as
@spawnat  and remotecall_fetch . It explains how global variables are
handled when used in remote calls and offers guidance on how to manage
them ef fectively . Let’ s break down the information:
Global Variables in Remote  Execution:  When executing code
remotely using constructs like @spawnat  and remotecall_fetch , you
might reference global variables . Global bindings in the Main  module
are treated dif ferently compared to global bindings in other modules.
Example:  Referencing Global Variables: Consider the code snippet:
julia> A = rand(10, 10)
10×10 Matrix{Float64}:
0.253969 0.00485857 0.871175 0.692261 0.375655 0.914166
0.488375 0.871096 0.406925 0.68845
…
0.704127 0.682123 0.185796 0.492256 0.629498 0.526364
0.447056 0.0307264 0.829556 0.42526
julia> remotecall_fetch(()->sum(A), 2)
52.61104291838339
Here, sum must be deﬁned  on the remote process (worker 2). While A
is a global variable in the local  workspace, worker 2 doesn’ t have a
variable named A in its Main  module. However , the act of shipping the
closure ()->sum(A)  to work er 2 results in Main.A  being deﬁned there.
Importantly , Main.A  on worker 2 continues to exist even after the
remotecall_fetch  returns.
Handling Global Refer ences in Remote Calls:  Remote calls with
embedded globa l references (limited to the Main  module ) behav e as
follows:
New global bindings are created on the destination workers if
they are referenced as part of a remote call.
Global constants are also declared as constants on remote nodes.
Globals are re-sent to a destination worker only within the
context of a remote call if their value has changed.
The cluster does not synchronize global bindings across nodes.
Example Illustration:  The text provides an illustrative example to
demonstrate these points. After calling remotecall_fetch(()-
>sum(A), 2) and then modifying A, calling  remotecall_fetch(()-
>sum(A), 3) results in different values of Main.A  on work ers 2 and 3,
while the value of Main.A  on the master node (node 1) is set to
nothing .
Managing Glob al Memory on Workers:  While memory  associated
with globals may be collected when they are reassigned to the master
node, no such action is taken on the worker nodes. The bindings for
globals continue to be valid. To release memory associated with them
on remote nodes, you can use the clear!  function to manually
reassign speciﬁc globals to nothing . This action will release the
memory as part of the regular garbage collection cycle.
Best Practices:  The text empha sizes the importance of being cautious
when referencing globals in remote calls. It’s preferable to avoid using
globals in such cases if possib le. If you need to reference globals,
consider using let blocks to localize global variables. This helps
avoid unexpected behavior due to global variable bindings on remote
workers.
Example of Using let Blocks:  The text provides an example of using
a let block to ensure a global variable remains local and doesn’ t
interfere with remote execution.
julia> A = rand(10, 10)
10×10 Matrix{Float64}:
0.414612 0.708716 0.451456 0.0138283 0.714173 0.825522
0.647969 0.615009 0.0255661 0.857037
…
julia> remotecall_fetch(()->A, 2)
10×10 Matrix{Float64}:
0.414612 0.708716 0.451456 0.0138283 0.714173 0.825522
0.647969 0.615009 0.0255661 0.857037
…
julia> B = rand(10, 10)
10×10 Matrix{Float64}:
0.181936 0.866609 0.352039 0.975791 0.847819 0.640851
0.51947 0.206783 0.366583 0.819961
…
julia> let B = B
remotecall_fetch(()->B, 2)
End

Maps and Loops
We will understand the concept of parallel map and loops in Julia’ s
distributed programming context, showcasing examples and best practices
for performing parallel computations efﬁciently . Let’s break down the
information:
Parallel Comp utation withou t Data Movement : Many  paralle l
computations don’t require data movement and can be executed
independently on multiple processes. A common example is a Monte
Carlo simulation  where multiple processes perform independen t trials
simultaneously .
Deﬁning a Parallelizable Function : The text introduces a function
called count_heads  that counts the number of “heads” in a sequence
of random coin ﬂips.
Using  @spawnat  for Parallel Execution : The @spawnat macro is
used to execute the count_heads  function on different processes
simultaneously . The results are then fetched and combined using the
fetch function.
Parallel For Loop with  @distributed : A more generalized approach
to achieve parallelism is demonstrated using a parallel for loop
implemented with the @distributed  macro. In this example, multiple
iterations of the loop are assig ned to different processes, and their
results are combined using a speciﬁed reduction operation (in this
case, +).
Parallel Loop Behavior : It’s highlighted that parallel for loops differ
from serial for loops. The iterations don’t necessarily occur in a
speciﬁc order , and variables or array writes aren’ t globally visible
since iterations run on dif ferent processes.
Avoiding Shar ed Variables in Parallel Loops : It’s cautioned that
using shared variables directly  within parallel loops can lead to
unexpected behavior due to the separate copies of variables on
different processes. Shared Arrays are introduced as a workaround for
this limitation.
Using Read-On ly “Outside ” Variables : While modifying shared
variables in parallel loops can lead to issues, using read-only variables
from outside the loop is reasona ble. The example demonstrates how a
read-only vector can be used within a parallel loop.
Asynchr onous Parallel Loops : It’s mentioned that the reduction
operator can be omitted if not needed. In such cases, the loop runs
asynchronously , spawning independent tasks on available workers and
returning an array of Future objects. The caller can wait for these
futures to complete later .
Parallel Map with pmap : The concept of parallel map is introduced
using the pmap function in Julia. This function applies a given
function to elements of a collection in parallel. An example is
provided where singular values of multiple large random matric es are
computed in parallel using pmap.
Comparison between pmap and @distributed for: The differences
between pmap and @distributed for are explained. pmap is designed
for cases where each function call involves a large amount of work,
while @distributed  for can handle smaller tasks. Both use worker
processes for the parallel computation, and the ﬁnal reduction is done
on the calling process for @distributed  for.
References and Channels
We will understand the concepts  of remote references and abstra ct channels
in Julia’ s distributed programming framework. Let’s break down the
information:
Remote Refer ences and AbstractChannels : Remote references in
Julia are used to refer to objects that are stored on remote processes.
These remote references are always associated with an implementation
of an AbstractChannel .
AbstractChannel  and Concrete Implementations: An
AbstractChannel  is an abstract type that deﬁn es the behavior of
communication channels between processes. Concrete
implementations of AbstractChannel , such as Channel, are required
to implement speciﬁc methods like put! , take! , fetch , isready , and
wait .
Remote Object Storage in Channels : A remote objec t referred to by
a Futur e (a type representing a reference to the result of an
asynchronous computation) is stored in a Channel{Any}(1) . This
means that the remote process  holds a channel with a size of 1,
capable of storing objects of any type.
RemoteChannel  and Rew ritable Refer ences : RemoteChannel  is a type
that allows for rewritable references to different types and sizes of
channels, as well as other implementations of AbstractChannel . This
ﬂexibility enables you to refer to various types of remote
communication mechanisms.
Constructing Remote Channels : The constructor
RemoteChannel(f::Function, pid)()  is used to create references to
channels that can hold more than one value of a speciﬁc type. The
function f is executed on the process with the given pid, and it must
return an AbstractChannel . This allows you to construct references to
channels with dif ferent properties on remote processes.
Example of RemoteChannel : An example is provided to illustrate the
use of RemoteChannel . For instance, RemoteChannel(()-
>Channel{Int}(10), pid)  constru cts a reference to a channel of type
Int and size 10. This channel exists on the worker process identi ﬁed by
pid.
Methods on RemoteChannel : Methods such as put! , take! , fetch ,
isready , and wait  can be used on a RemoteChannel . These methods
are proxied onto the actual channel implementation on the remote
process, allowin g you to interac t with the remote channel as if it were
local.
Custom Implementations with  RemoteChannel : Remot eChannel can
be used to refer to user-implemented AbstractChannel objects. For
example, you can create your custom channel types and use
RemoteChannel to interact with them on remote processes.
Example in dictchannel.jl : The text menti ons that there’ s a simple
example provided in the dictchannel.jl  ﬁle in the Examples
repository . This example uses a dictionary as the remote stora ge for
the channel, showcasing the ﬂexibility of remote channels for various
use cases.
We will understa nd the concepts of channels and remote channe ls in Julia’ s
distributed programming framework. It introduces their usage through an
example of parallel computation involving interprocess  communication .
Let’s break down the information:
Channels and  RemoteChannels :
A Chann el is a communication mechanism that is local to a single
process. It cannot be directly accessed by other processes.
In contra st, a RemoteChannel  serves as a hand le to a Channel  and
allows values to be exchanged across dif ferent worker processes.
The pid (process id) associated with a RemoteChannel  identiﬁes
the process where the backing store (the actual Channel) exists.
Any process with a reference to a RemoteChannel  can perform
operations such as putting and taking items from the channel.
Data is automatically sent to or retrieved from the process
associated with the RemoteChannel .
Serialization of Channels and  RemoteChannels :
When a Channe l is serialized, the data present in the channel is
also serialized. Deserializing it creates a copy of the original
object.
Serializing a RemoteChannel  involves serializing an identiﬁer  that
points to the location and instance of the underlying Channe l.
Deserializing a RemoteChannel  results in a reference to the same
backing store as the original.
Parallel Computation Example : For multiple worker processe s to
perform parallel computation using channels for interprocess
communication, we need to follow the steps:
Workers are added using addprocs(4)  to create four worker
processes.
Two RemoteChannel  instances, jobs and results, are created to
manage commu nication betwe en the master process and the
workers.
A function do_work  is deﬁned using @everywhere , making it
available on all worker processes. This function simulates the
execution of tasks.
The make_jobs  function populates the jobs channel with job IDs.
n jobs are created using make_jobs(n)  and fed into the jobs
channel asynchronously .
Parallel tasks are started on worker processes using remote_do  to
process jobs from the jobs chann el and write results to the results
channel.
Results are fetched from the results channel using a loop that
prints job IDs, execution times, and worker IDs.
Distributed Garbage Collection
When you have objects referen ced by remote references in a distributed
computing scena rio, these objects can only be freed when all the references
to them across the cluster have been deleted.
Here’ s how the process works:
Refer ence Tracking : The node that stores the actual value of the
object keeps track of which workers in the cluster have references to
it. Whe never a remote reference, like a RemoteChannel  or an
unfetched  Future, is serialized and sent to a worker , the node where
the value is stored is notiﬁed about this addition of reference.
Garbage Collection Notiﬁcation : Similarly , when a remote refer ence
or an unfetched  Future is garba ge collected locally (on the worker
where it was created), the owning node is notiﬁed again. This
notiﬁcation mechanism is handled by an internal serializer that is
aware of the cluster ’s structure.
Cluster  Context : It’s essential to understand that remote references
are only valid within the context of a running cluster . You can’t
serialize and deserialize referen ces to and from regular I/O objects;
this is not supported.
Tracking Messa ges: The notiﬁcations are managed using “tracking ”
messages. When a reference is serialized to another process, an “add
reference ” message is sent to the owning node. Conversely , when a
reference is locally garbage collected, a “delete reference ” message
is sent to the owning node.
Futur es Behav ior: Futures are designed to be written once and
cached locally . Fetching a Future also triggers an update to the
reference tracking information on the node that holds the value.
Value Freedom : The node that owns the value of an object will free it
from memory only when all references to it have been cleared.
Serializing Fetched Futur es: If you serializ e a fetched Future to a
different node, the value is also sent along with it. This is because, by
the time you serialize it, the original node might have already
collected the value.
Local Garbage Collection : The local garbage collection of objects
depends on their size and the current memory usage in the system.
Smaller local reference objects may not be immediately collected.
Explicitly Calling Finalize : It’s a recommended practice to explicitly
call the ﬁnalize function on local instances of RemoteChannels  or on
unfetched  Futures that you no longer need. This sends an immediate
message to the remote node, instructing it to remove its reference to
the value. This is particularly important because the local reference
may not be collected immediately due to memory management
considerations.
Refer ence Inva lidation : Once you call ﬁnalize on a reference, it
becomes invalid. You can’ t use it in any further operations or calls.
Local Invocations in Distributed Computing
When working with distributed computing in Julia, there’ s an interesting
behavior to consider regarding local invocations, which involv es how data
is copied and manipulated on remote nodes. Let’ s break down this behavior:
Data Copy for Remote Execution : In distributed computing, when
you use remotecalls  or store  data in a RemoteChannel  or Future on a
different node, the data is copied over to the remote node for
execution. This naturally results  in serialized copies of the objects on
the remote node.
Local Execution on Same Node : However , when the destination
node for a remotecall  or data storage is the same as the local node
(the node where  the call originates), the call is executed as a local call.
While it might run in a separate task, there’ s no serialization or
deserialization of data. Consequently , the call operates on the same
object instances that were passed, and no copies are created.
Demonstrating Local Invocation :
When you create a RemoteChannel  on the local node and perform
put! operations on it while modifying the same object instance
between calls, only a single object instance is stored in the
RemoteChannel . The modiﬁcat ions made to the object instance
are reﬂected across all operations.
On the other hand, if the RemoteChannel  is owned by a different
node, modiﬁcations between calls result in separate object
instances being stored.
Local Invocations and Data Copy :
This behavior is generally not problematic. It’s a consideration
mainly if the object is both stored locally and modiﬁed after the
call.
In such cases, it might be appro priate to store a deep copy of the
object to ensure that modiﬁcations don’ t affect the stored version.
Applying to  remotecall :
The same principle applies to remotecalls  execute d on the local
node.
When a remotecall  is made to the local node, it behaves just like
a direct invocation. The call modiﬁes local objects passed as
arguments.
However , when the remotecall  is execu ted on a remote node, it
operates on copies of the ar guments passed.
Demonstrations :
You can observe this behavior:
When invoking a remotecall_fetch  on the local node,
modiﬁcations to the input argument affect the fetched result
because it’ s executed locally .
When the remotecall_fetch  is executed on a remote node,
modiﬁcations to the input argument don’t affect the fetched
result since it operates on a copy .
Considerations and Use Cases :
This behavior is generally mana geable and might not be an issue
for most scenarios.
It becom es relev ant if the local  node is also used as a compu te
node and ar guments are modiﬁed after the call.
In such cases, you might need to consider using deep copies of
arguments for the call invoked on the local node.
Calls on remote nodes always operate on copies of ar guments.
Shared Arrays
Shared Arrays in Julia are a powerful tool for distributed computing,
allowing multipl e processes to jointly access and manipulate the same array
data using system-shared memory . Here’ s a comprehensive explanation of
how Shared Arrays work and how to use them ef fectively:
Basic Concept :
Shared Arrays map the same array across multiple processes
using system-shared memory .
Unlike Distributed Arrays (DArrays), where each process
accesses a chunk of data, Share d Arrays allow each participating
process to access the entire array .
Shared Arrays are particularly useful when large data needs to be
shared among multiple processes on the same machine.
Module and Loading :
The functionality for Shared Arrays is provided by the
SharedArrays  module.
This module must be explicitly loaded on all participating
workers using the @everywhere  using SharedArrays  directive.
Array Indexing and Behavior :
Shared Array indexing (assignment and access) follows the same
rules as regular arrays.
Operations are efﬁcient due to direct access to local process
memory .
Algorithms designed for regular arrays can work naturally with
Shared Arrays, though they will operate in single-process mode.
To work with algorithms expecting regular arrays, you can
extract the underlying array using the sdata function.
Creating Shar ed Arrays :
The constructor for a Shared Array follows the pattern:
SharedArray{T,N}(dims::NTuple; init=false,
pids=Int[])
This creates an N-dimensional shared array of type T with
dimensions dims, across the processes speciﬁed by pids.
Only processes speciﬁed by pids (and the creating process) can
access the Shared Array .
Note that only elements of bits type (isbits ) are supported in a
SharedArray .
Initialization and Parallel Initialization :
You can specify an init functio n when creating a Shared Array,
with the signature initfn(S::SharedArray) .
The init  function is called on all participating workers and can
be used to initialize the array .
Parallel initializ ation can be achieved by having each worker run
the init  function on a distinct portion of the array .
julia> using Distributed
julia> addprocs(3)
julia> @everywhere using SharedArrays
julia> S = SharedArray{Int,2}((3,4), init = S ->
S[localindices(S)] = repeat([myid()],
length(localindices(S))))
3×4 SharedMatrix{Int64}:
2 3 5 6
2 4 5 6
3 4 6 6
julia> S[3,2] = 7
julia> S
3×4 SharedMatrix{Int64}:
2 3 5 6
2 4 5 6
3 7 6 6
In this example, S is a 3×4 Shared Array of Integers, where each element is
initialized to the process ID.
Customizing Work Distribution :
The localindices  function provides disjoint one-dimensional
index ranges, useful for dividing work among processes.
You can deﬁne your own partitioning strategy using functions
like indexpids  and length.
Avoiding Conﬂicts :
Since all processes have access to the same underlying data,
conﬂicts can arise when multiple processes modify the same
element.
Care must be taken to avoid such conﬂicts, ensuring that
processes don’ t interfere with each other .
Parallel algorith ms should be designed to minimize overlappin g
work.
Parallel Kernel Example :
An example of parallelizing a kernel is provided:
function advection_shared!(q, u)
@sync begin
for p in procs(q)
@async remotecall_wait(advection_shared_chunk!, p,
q, u)
end
end
q
end
This function distributes chunks  of the array to different workers
for parallel processing.
Performance Comparison :
Shared Arrays offer signiﬁcant performance advantages in terms
of reduced communication overhead between workers.
Compared to parallel implementations using @distributed,
Shared Arrays can lead to faster execution times and better
resource utilization.
Cluster Managers
In Julia’s distributed computing framework, the concept of
ClusterManagers  plays a crucia l role in managing and organizing the
launching, networking, and event handling of worker processes within a
logical cluster . Here’ s an in-depth explanation of how ClusterManagers
function and their key characteristics:
Responsibilities of a ClusterManager:
A ClusterManager  oversees several critical tasks within a Julia
cluster , including:
Launching worker processes within the cluster environment.
Managing various events during the lifespan of each worker
process.
Optionally , providing data transport between workers.
Characteristics of a Julia Cluster:
A Julia cluster consists of several interconnected processes,
where each process serves a speciﬁc purpose.
The initial process, often referred to as the master process, is
assigned an ID of 1.
Only the master process has the authority to add or remov e
worker processes from the cluster .
Communication between processes is direct and seamless,
enabling any process to communicate with any other process.
Establishing Connections between Workers:
Connections between workers are established through a speciﬁc
process:
The addprocs  function is invoked on the master process,
along with a ClusterManager  object.
The appropriate launch method is called, spawning the
required numbe r of worker processes on the designated
machines.
Each worker process starts listening on a free port and
outputs its host and port information to the standard output.
The cluster manager captures the standard output of each
worker and makes this information available to the master
process.
The master process parses the output, establishes TCP/IP .
connections to each worker , and notiﬁes workers about
others in the cluster .
Each worker connects to every worker with an ID lower than
its own, forming a mesh netw ork where each worker is
directly connected to all others.
Transport Layer  and Customization:
The default transport layer for communication between worke rs
uses plain TCPSockets .
However , it’s possible to replace the default transport with a
custom transport to suit speciﬁc needs.
Custom transpor t implementatio ns require setting up connections
to remote workers and providing appropriate IO objects for
message-processing loops.
In-Built Cluster  Managers:
Julia provides two built-in cluster managers:
LocalManager : Used when addprocs()  or
addprocs(np::Integer)  is called. It launches additional
workers on the same host, utilizing multi-core and multi-
processor hardware.
SSHManager : Used when addprocs(hostnames::Array)  is
called with a list of hostnam es. It launches workers on
remote hosts via SSH and can also handle master -worker and
worker -worker connections via SSH.
Custom Cluster  Manager  Implementation:
A custo m cluster manager must be a subtype of the abstra ct
ClusterManager .
It should implement the launch method responsible for launching
new workers and the manage method, which handles various
events during a worker ’s lifetime (such as registration,
interruption, and ﬁnalization).
Worker  Conﬁguration with WorkerConﬁg:
A WorkerConfig  object stores information related to workers and
their behavior .
Fields include io, host , port , count , exename , exeflags ,
userdata , tunnel , bind_addr , sshflags , max_parallel , and
connect_at .
Custom cluster managers can utilize speciﬁc ﬁelds to tailor
worker behavior and connectivity .
Cluster  Cookie for  Authentication:
All processes within a cluster share the same cookie, serving as
an authentication mechanism.
Workers must be started with the same cookie as the mast er
process to ensure secure connections.
The cookie can be passed to workers through command-line
arguments or stdin.
Cluster managers can retrieve the cookie using the
cluster_cookie()  function.
Security Considerations for  LocalManager  and SSHManager:
LocalManager  and SSHManager  are designed for secure
environments.
LocalManager  binds only to the loopback interface by default,
ensuring local connections.
SSHManager  uses SSH to launch workers and can be conﬁgured
for passwordless login.
Security measur es, such as secure networking and encryption,
can be customized using custom cluster managers.
Conclusion
In this chapter , we delved into the realm of distributed computation in Julia,
exploring how to harness the power of multiple processes for parallel and
cooperative task execution. Through various concepts and techniques, we
learned how to leverage Julia’ s capabilities to distribute workloads across
multiple machines or cores, leading to enhanced performance and
efﬁciency .

scheduling in a distributed computing environment, and how do they
contribute to overall performance?

loading them in a single process, and why is it important to ensure all
processes have access to the required packages?

ﬂexibility valuable?

proper scoping and synchronization mitigate these challenges?

optimize data sharing among processes?

responsibilities do they have in terms of process launching,
management, and networking within a logical cluster?
